Sawm — Fasting
advanced
10 min
Intra-Hanafi Disputes on Water in the Ear and Wet/Dry Substances
Expansion on intra-Hanafi dispute regarding water in ear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the different Hanafi positions on whether water entering the ear during fasting invalidates the fast, and apply the mufta bihi ruling that deliberate pouring breaks the fast while incidental entry does not.

Core Idea
Water in the ear: deliberate pouring breaks the fast; incidental immersion does not.

This passage delves into a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute regarding water entering the ear during fasting. While oil is undisputed, water causes disagreement: al-Hidayah and al-Walwaliji hold it does not invalidate, but Qadikhan and Fath al-Qadir distinguish between deliberate pouring (invalidates) and incidental immersion (does not). The commentator also discusses the distinction between dry and wet substances, the role of certainty, and the positions of Abu Yusuf on expiation for snuff and oral medicine. This lesson equips you to navigate conflicting opinions within the school and apply the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the initial disagreement
The passage starts with water in the ear. The commentator notes that oil is undisputed (invalidates if reaches brain), but water is debated. Al-Hidayah chose that it does not break the fast whether it enters by itself or by action. Al-Walwaliji agreed absolutely.
2
Analyze Qadikhan's distinction
Qadikhan differentiates: if one wades in water and it enters without action, no invalidation; if one pours water into the ear deliberately, it invalidates because the water reaches the interior by one's own action. This distinction focuses on the element of causality (fi'l).
3
Identify the tarjih (preponderance)
Fath al-Qadir gives preponderance to invalidation when water is poured deliberately. This becomes the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position. The commentator also mentions that the same logic applies to bathing (ghusl) while fasting: if water enters the ear without intention, it does not break the fast.
The tarjih is based on the principle that deliberate action causing entry invalidates.
4
Examine the wet/dry substance distinction and the role of certainty
The passage then discusses that the criterion for invalidation is reaching the interior, not wetness. Al-Quduri conditioned wetness because wet usually reaches, but the commentator clarifies that if dry is known to reach, it also invalidates. When there is uncertainty about a wet substance, Abu Hanifa rules invalidation due to customary reach (ghalabat al-ada), while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not invalidate.
This highlights the difference between certainty (yaqin) and customary presumption (ghalabat al-zann).
5
Apply the principles to other scenarios
Apply the same logic to other cases: inserting a dry object (stick, finger) into the anus or vagina does not invalidate unless the object is wet. If an arrowhead remains in the body, it breaks the fast because it settles; if it exits, it does not. Oral medicine (wajur) invalidates but without expiation, except according to Abu Yusuf who required expiation for snuff and oral medicine.
The common thread: action + beneficial substance reaching interior = invalidation; no action or no settlement = no invalidation.

أقطر في أذنه أو داوى جائفة أو آمة بدواء ووصل الدواء إلى جوفه أو دماغه أفطر وإن من الشافعية في شرح المنهج بارتفاعه بدون تكفير فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى. وعبر بـ«من» المفيدة للعموم في قوله «من جامع أو جومع» ليفيد أنه لا فرق في الحكم وهو وجوب الكفارة بين الذكر والأنثى والحر والعبد، ولهذا صرح في البزازية بالوجوب على الجارية فيما لو أخبرات سيدها بعد طلوع الفجر عالمة بطلوعه فجامعها مع عدم الوجوب عليه، وكذا لا السلطان بين وغيره ولهذا قال في البزازية : إذا لزم الكفارة على السلطان وهو موسر بما له الحلال وليس عليه تبعة لأحد يفتى بإعتاق الرقبة. وقال أبو نصر محمد بن سلام: يفتي بصيام شهرين لأن المقصود من الكفارة الإنزجار ويسهل عليه إفطار شهر وإعتاق رقبة فلا فرق يحصل الزجر . قوله (ولا كفارة بالإنزال فيما دون الفرج أي في غير القبل والدبر كالفخذ والإبط والبطن لانعدام الجماع صورة وفسد صومه لوجوده معنى كما قدمناه في المباشره والتة .. وعمل المرأتين كذلك كما قدمناه. وفي المغرب : الفرج قبل الرجل والمرأة باتفاق أهل اللغة، وقوله القبل والدبر كلاهما فرج يعني في الحكم اهـ بلفظه يعني لا في اللغة قوله (وبإفساد صوم غير رمضان أي لا كفارة في إفساد صوم غير أداء رمضان لأن الإفطار في رمضان أبلغ في الجناية لهتك حرمة الشهر فلا يلحق به غيره لا قياساً إذا هو ممتنع لكونه على خلاف القياس، ولا دلالة لأن إفساد غيره ليس في معناه ولزوم إفساد الحج النفل والقضاء بالجماع ليس إلحاقاً بإفساد الحج الفرض بل هو ثابت ابتداء لعموم نص القضاء والإجماع. قوله: (وإذا احتقن أو استعط أو أقطر في أذنه أو داوى جائفة أو آمة بدواء ووصل إلى جوفة أو دماغه أفطر) لقوله عليه السلام الفطر مما دخل وليس مما خرج رواه أبو يعلى الموصلي في مسنده وهو مخصوص بحديث الاستقاء، أو الفطر فيه باعتبار أنه يعود شيء وإن قل حتى لا يحس كذا في فتح القدير. فإن قلت ظاهره أن الخارج لا يبطل الصوم أصلاً إلا في الاستقاء، والحصر ممنوع لأن الحيض والنفاس كل منهما يفسد الصوم كما صرح به في البدائع . . قلت : لا يرد لأن إفسادهما الصوم باعتبار منافاتهما الأهلية له شرعاً على خلاف القياس يإجماع الصحابة بخلاف الجنون والإغماء بعد النية لا يفسدان الصوم لأنهما لا ينافيان أهلية الأداء وإنما ينافيان النية. كذا في البدائع والرواية بالفتح في احتقن واستعط أي وضع الحقنة في الدبر وصب السعوط وهو الدواء في الأنف، وبالضم في أقطر. والجائفة اسم الجراحة وصلت إلى الجوف والآمة الجراحة وصلت إلى أم الدماغ. وأطلق في الإفطار في الأذن فشمل الماء والدهن وهو في الدهن بلا خلاف أما الماء فاختار في الهداية عدم الإفطار به، سواء دخل بنفسه أو أدخله. وصرح الولوالجي بأنه لا يفسد صومه مطلقاً بأنه لم يوجد الفطر صورة ولا معنى لأنه مما لا يتعلق به صلاح البدن بوصوله إلى الدماغ. وجعل السعوط كالإقطار في الأذن وصححه في المحيط . وفي فتاوى قاضيخان أنه إن خاض الماء فدخل أذنه لا يفسد، وإن صب الماء في أذنه فالصحيح أنه يفسد لأنه وصل إلى الجوف بفعله، ورجحه المحقق في فتح القدير. وبهذا يعلم حكم الغسل وهو صائم إذا دخل الماء في أذنه. وفي عمدة الفتاوى للصدر الشهيد : فلو دخل الماء في أنفه أو أذنه ووصل إلى الدماغ لا شيء عليه اهـ. ولو شد الطعام بخيط وأرسله في حلقه وطرف الخيط في يده لا يفسد الصوم إلا إذا انفصل . وذكر الولوالجي أن الصائم إذا استقصى في الاستنجاء حتى بلغ مبلغ المحقنة فهذا أقل ما يكون ولو كان يفسد صومه والاستقصاء لا يفعل لأنه يورث داء عظيماً. وفي الظهيرية : ولو أدخل خشبة أو نحوها وطرفاً منها بيده لم يفسد صومه . قال في البدائع : وهذ يدل على أن استقرار الداخل في الجوف شرط لفساد الصوم ، وكذا لو أدخل أصبعه في أسته أو أدخلت المرأة في فرجها هو المختار إلا إذا كانت الأصبع مبتلة بالماء أو الدهن فحينئذ يفسد لوصول الماء أو الدهن. وقيل: إن المرأة إذا حشت الفرج الداخل فسد صومها، والصائم إذ أصابه سهم وخرج من الجانب الآخر لم يفسد صومه ولو بقي النصل في جوفه يفسد صومه اهـ . وفي شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان : وإن بقي الرمح في جوفه اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنه لا يفسد لأنه لم يوجد منه الفعل ولم يصل إليه ما فيه صلاحه. وذكر الولوالجي: وأما الوجور في الفم فإنه يفسد صومه لأنه وصل إلى جوف البدن ما هو مصلح للبدن فكان أكلاً معنى لكن لا تلزمه الكفارة لانعدام الأكل صورة. وعن أبي يوسف في السعوط والوجور الكفارة، ولو استعط ليلاً فخرج نهاراً لا يفطر. وأطلق الدواء فشمل الرطب واليابس لأن العبرة للوصول لا لكونه رطباً أو يابساً، وإنما شرطه القدوري لأن الرطب هو الذي يصل إلى الجوف عادة حتى لو علم أن الرطب لم يصل لم يفسد ولو علم أن اليابس وصل فسد صومه . كذا في العناية لكن بقي ما إذا لم يعلم يقيناً أحدهما وكان رطباً فعند أبي حنيفة يفطر للوصول عادة. وقالا : لا
If he puts drops in his ear or treats a deep wound or a skull wound with medicine and the medicine reaches his interior or brain, he breaks his fast. Even from the Shafi'is in Sharh al-Minhaj, that it is lifted without expiation between him and Allah. And he expressed with 'min' indicating generality in his saying 'whoever has intercourse or is had intercourse with' to indicate that there is no difference in the ruling, which is the obligation of expiation, between male and female, free and slave. Thus it is stated in al-Bazzaziyyah that it is obligatory on the slave girl if she informs her master after dawn, knowing it is dawn, and he has intercourse with her despite no obligation on him. Likewise, it does not matter whether he is a sultan or other. Thus he said in al-Bazzaziyyah: If expiation becomes obligatory on the sultan and he is wealthy from lawful means and there is no liability on him towards anyone, he is given the fatwa to free a slave. Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Salam said: He is given the fatwa to fast two months because the purpose of expiation is deterrence, and it is easy for him to fast a month and free a slave, so there is no difference. He said: (And no expiation for emission below the private part) i.e., other than the front and back, like the thigh, armpit, and belly, because intercourse is absent in form and his fast is invalidated due to its presence in meaning, as we mentioned in direct contact... And the action of two women is likewise as we mentioned. In al-Mughrib: 'Farj' refers to the male and female private parts by agreement of the philologists, and his saying 'front and back' both are 'farj' in legal sense, not linguistically. He said: (And by spoiling a fast other than Ramadan) i.e., no expiation for spoiling a fast other than performing Ramadan, because breaking the fast in Ramadan is more severe in offense due to violating the sanctity of the month, so nothing else is attached to it, neither by analogy—since it is contrary to analogy—nor by indication, because spoiling other fasts is not in its meaning. And the obligation of spoiling supererogatory pilgrimage and make-up by intercourse is not by analogy to spoiling obligatory pilgrimage, but is established initially due to the generality of the text on make-up and consensus. He said: (And if he gives an enema, or takes snuff, or puts drops in his ear, or treats a deep wound or skull wound with medicine and it reaches his interior or brain, he breaks his fast) due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Breaking the fast is from what enters, not from what exits'—narrated by Abu Ya'la al-Mawsili in his Musnad. And it is restricted by the hadith on vomiting, or breaking the fast in it because it returns something even if little, so that he does not perceive it, as in Fath al-Qadir. If you say: Its apparent meaning is that what exits does not invalidate the fast at all except for vomiting, but the restriction is denied because menstruation and postpartum bleeding each invalidate the fast, as stated in al-Bada'i'... I say: That is not rebutted because they invalidate the fast due to their conflicting with legal capacity by consensus of the Companions, contrary to analogy. Unlike insanity and fainting after the intention, they do not invalidate the fast because they do not conflict with capacity for performance but rather conflict with the intention. Thus in al-Bada'i'. The pronunciation is with fatha (open vowel) in 'ihtaqana' and 'ista'ata' i.e., placing the enema in the anus and pouring snuff, which is medicine into the nose, and with dammah (u vowel) in 'aqtara'. 'Ja'ifah' is the name for a wound reaching the interior, and 'ammah' is a wound reaching the brain membrane. He generalized about breaking the fast with the ear, so it includes water and oil—regarding oil there is no disagreement. As for water, al-Hidayah chose that it does not break the fast, whether it enters by itself or he inserts it. And al-Walwaliji stated that it does not break his fast absolutely, because breaking the fast is not found in form nor meaning, since it does not involve benefit to the body by reaching the brain. And he made snuff like drops in the ear, and it was corrected in al-Muhit. In the Fatawa of Qadikhan, if he wades in water and water enters his ear, it does not break; but if he pours water into his ear, the correct view is that it breaks because it reaches the interior by his action, and the scholar in Fath al-Qadir gave preponderance to this. From this, the ruling is known for bathing while fasting if water enters his ear. In 'Umdat al-Fatawa of al-Sadr al-Shahid: 'If water enters his nose or ear and reaches the brain, there is nothing upon him.' And if he ties food with a string and sends it down his throat while holding the string, his fast is not broken unless it detaches. And al-Walwaliji mentioned that if the fasting person is thorough in istinja' until it reaches the level of an enema, then it is the least that it would break his fast, but thoroughness is not done because it causes great disease. In al-Zahiriyyah: If he inserts a stick or similar with one end in his hand, his fast is not broken. Al-Bada'i' said: This indicates that settling of what enters in the interior is a condition for breaking the fast. Likewise, if he inserts his finger into his anus or a woman inserts into her vagina, the chosen view is it does not break unless the finger is wet with water or oil, then it breaks due to water or oil reaching. It is said: If a woman stuffs her vagina with dry matter, her fast is broken. If the fasting person is struck by an arrow and it exits from the other side, his fast is not broken; if the arrowhead remains in his interior, his fast is broken. In Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir of Qadikhan: If the spear remains in his interior, they differed, and the correct view is that it does not break because no action came from him and nothing beneficial reached him. Al-Walwaliji mentioned: As for oral medicine, it breaks his fast because something beneficial to the body reaches the interior, so it is like eating in meaning, but expiation is not obligatory because eating is absent in form. From Abu Yusuf, concerning snuff and oral medicine, there is expiation. If he takes snuff at night and it comes out during the day, it does not break his fast. The word 'medicine' was general, so it includes wet and dry because the criterion is reaching, not whether it is wet or dry. Al-Quduri conditioned wetness because wet usually reaches the interior, such that if one knows that wet did not reach, it does not break, and if one knows that dry reached, his fast is broken. Thus in al-'Inayah, but there remains the case where one does not know with certainty which it was—if it was wet, then according to Abu Hanifa, he breaks due to usual reaching; they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: No.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding what invalidates fasting and the rules of expiation. The author first discusses the generality of the ruling on expiation for intercourse during Ramadan: it applies to both genders, free and slave, and even if the sultan is the one fasting. He cites al-Bazzaziyyah and a fatwa by Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Salam suggesting that for the wealthy, freeing a slave is recommended over fasting two months, though the purpose of deterrence remains. Then, he addresses the matn statement that no expiation is due for emission (inzal) that occurs without actual intercourse, i.e., through touching or contact short of the front or back passages. The fast is still invalidated because of the seminal emission, but since there was no intercourse in form, no expiation is required. This ruling is limited to interactions between men and women; the case of two women is mentioned as analogous. The author clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'farj' as the private parts in general, but legally both the front and back are considered private parts. Next, he comments on the matn that spoiling a fast other than Ramadan (like make-up or supererogatory fasts) does not require expiation. The reason is that the sanctity of Ramadan is unique; breaking it is a more severe offense. Therefore, other fasts cannot be analogized to it. The case of pilgrimage (hajj) is mentioned as an exception where spoiling a non-obligatory pilgrimage still requires a sacrifice (dam), but that is based on explicit text, not analogy. The main section deals with the entry of substances into the body through various means: enema, snuff, ear drops, and treatments for deep wounds or skull fractures. The hadith 'Breaking the fast is from what enters, not from what exits' is the primary proof, but it is restricted by the hadith on vomiting (which involves exit). The author explains that the fast is broken because something returns to the interior, even if minute. He also addresses an objection about menstruation and postpartum bleeding invalidating the fast despite being exits. The answer is that these invalidate due to legal incapacity, not by the general rule. He then delves into the pronunciation and definition of terms: 'ihtaqana' (enema), 'ista'ata' (snuff), 'aqtara' (drops), 'ja'ifah' (deep wound reaching the interior), and 'ammah' (wound reaching the brain). For the ear, he notes that oil is undisputed, but water is debated. Al-Hidayah holds it does not break the fast, while others say it does if poured intentionally. The relied-upon position from Fath al-Qadir is that if one pours water into the ear deliberately, it invalidates the fast because the water reaches the interior by one's own action. However, if it enters while bathing without intent, it does not break. The passage continues with various scenarios: inserting a food-filled string, thorough cleaning (istinja') that reaches the depth of an enema, inserting a dry stick or finger into the anus or vagina, being struck by an arrow or spear, and oral medicine (wajur). In all cases, the key principle is whether a substance that is beneficial to the body (like food or medicine) reaches the interior by one's own action. If it does, the fast is broken but without expiation, unless it was intercourse during Ramadan. Abu Yusuf held that snuff and oral medicine do require expiation, but the majority position is otherwise. The author concludes with a discussion of dry vs. wet substances: if the substance is wet, it is presumed to reach the interior, thus breaking the fast; if dry, it does not unless it is known to reach. At the end, he mentions a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) when there is uncertainty about whether a wet substance reached. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from textual proofs (the hadith) and applying principles of causation and customary practice.

Key Terms
الجائفة (al-ja'ifah): A wound that reaches the interior of the body (jowf), such as a deep stab in the abdomen or chest.
الآمة (al-ammah): A wound that reaches the membrane surrounding the brain (umm al-dimagh), i.e., a deep skull fracture.
السعوط (al-sa'ut): Medicine that is snuffed or inhaled through the nose.
المحقنة (al-mihqanah): An enema, i.e., injecting liquid into the rectum.
الوجور (al-wajur): Medicine poured into the mouth and swallowed.
الاستقرار (al-istiqrar): Settling of the entered substance in the interior.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi ruling in this passage, what happens if a fasting person deliberately pours water into his ear?
A) The fast remains valid because water is not food.
B) The fast is broken and requires qada, but no expiation.
C) The fast is broken and requires both qada and expiation.
D) The fast remains valid because the ear is not a passage.

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, deliberate pouring of water into the ear invalidates the fast according to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), while incidental entry during bathing does not; wet/dry substances are judged by actual reach, with customary presumption for wet.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Water Entering the Ear During Fasting: The Hanafi Ruling and Its Proofs
Ruling on water/oil in ear and differing opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water entering the ear during fasting, explain the reasoning based on the concepts of jawf and salah al-badan, and distinguish between the different opinions within the school.

Core Idea
Water in the ear does not break the fast unless it reaches the internal cavity (jawf) by a direct action—nutritional benefit is key.

The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between substances that break the fast and those that do not. One subtle case is water entering the ear. This lesson examines the relied-upon view from Al-Hidayah that water in the ear does not break the fast, alongside the dissenting opinion of Qadi Khan. You will learn the underlying principle: the fast is only broken if the substance reaches the body’s internal cavity (jawf) with nutritional benefit, or if deliberately introduced via a direct route.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its scope
Determine the main position in Al-Hidayah regarding water in the ear. Does it break the fast? For what cases?
The passage says Al-Hidayah chose that it does not break, whether by itself or inserted.
2
Understand the rationale: jawf and salah al-badan
Al-Walwalji explains that there is no form (surah) or meaning (ma'na) of breaking because water does not provide nutritional benefit (salah al-badan) and does not reach the stomach cavity (jawf).
This is the core proof: absence of nutrition and non-reach to jawf.
3
Examine the dissenting opinion (Qadi Khan)
Qadi Khan distinguishes between accidental entry (wading) and deliberate pouring. If deliberate, water reaches the jawf by one's action and breaks the fast.
This opinion is preferred by the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir.
4
Apply the principle to related cases
Consider oil, snuff, and bathing. Oil has no disagreement; snuff is like drops in the ear. For bathing, the ruling depends on whether water enters deliberately.
The passage mentions 'Umdat al-Fatawa: if water reaches the brain via nose or ear, nothing is incurred.
5
Resolve the ikhtilaf: which view is mufta bihi?
The majority (Al-Hidayah, Al-Walwalji, Al-Muhit) hold that it does not break. But Fath al-Qadir preferred Qadi Khan's distinction for deliberate pouring. The mufta bihi is that deliberate pouring causing entry to jawf breaks the fast.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأذن فشمل الماء والدهن وهو في الدهن بلا خلاف أما الماء فاختار في الهداية عدم الإفطار به، سواء دخل بنفسه أو أدخله. وصرح الولوالجي بأنه لا يفسد صومه مطلقاً بأنه لم يوجد الفطر صورة ولا معنى لأنه مما لا يتعلق به صلاح البدن بوصوله إلى الدماغ. وجعل السعوط كالإقطار في الأذن وصححه في المحيط . وفي فتاوى قاضيخان أنه إن خاض الماء فدخل أذنه لا يفسد، وإن صب الماء في أذنه فالصحيح أنه يفسد لأنه وصل إلى الجوف بفعله، ورجحه المحقق في فتح القدير. وبهذا يعلم حكم الغسل وهو صائم إذا دخل الماء في أذنه. وفي عمدة الفتاوى للصدر الشهيد : فلو دخل الماء في أنفه أو أذنه ووصل إلى الدماغ لا شيء عليه اهـ.
The ear includes water and oil. For oil, there is no disagreement. As for water, Al-Hidayah chose that it does not break the fast, whether it enters by itself or he inserts it. Al-Walwalji stated that it does not break his fast absolutely, because breaking the fast is found neither in form nor in meaning, since it does not involve nutrition of the body by reaching the brain. He made snuff like drops in the ear and corrected it in Al-Muhit. In Fatawa Qadi Khan, if he wades in water and water enters his ear, it does not break the fast; but if he pours water into his ear, the correct view is that it breaks the fast because it reaches the cavity by his action, and the investigator in Fath al-Qadir preferred this. By this, the ruling of bathing while fasting when water enters his ear is known. In 'Umdat al-Fatawa by al-Sadr al-Shahid: If water enters his nose or ear and reaches the brain, nothing is upon him.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on water entering the ear during fasting. The author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is explaining the differing opinions. The relied-upon view (al-mukhtar) from Al-Hidayah is that water in the ear does not break the fast, whether it enters by itself or the person puts it in. The reasoning, as given by Al-Walwalji, is that there is no literal swallowing (surah) nor any nutrition benefit (ma'na) for the body by reaching the brain. Snuff is treated like drops in the ear. However, Qadi Khan distinguishes: if water enters while wading, it does not break the fast; but if one deliberately pours water, it breaks because it reaches the internal cavity (jawf) by one's own action. The muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir preferred this distinction. The passage also cites 'Umdat al-Fatawa stating that if water reaches the brain via nose or ear, nothing is incurred. The overall lesson is that mere entry into an ear does not automatically break the fast; it depends on whether the water reaches the jawf (inner body cavity) and whether it is done deliberately.

Key Terms
الجوف (al-jawf): The inner cavity of the body, specifically the stomach or brain cavity, that if a substance reaches it, the fast may break.
صلاح البدن (salah al-badan): Nutritional benefit that the body derives from the substance, which is the basis for breaking the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in Fath al-Qadir, when does water in the ear break the fast?
A) Always
B) Never
C) Only if deliberately poured
D) Only if it reaches the brain

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that water in the ear does not break the fast unless it reaches the internal cavity (jawf) through a deliberate act; nutritional benefit is the key criterion.

Does Inserting Objects Break the Fast?
Ruling on insertion of objects during fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inserting dry and wet objects into body cavities while fasting and explain the condition of istiqrar (stability) as the determinant.

Core Idea
A dry insertion does not break the fast unless it settles in the cavity; moisture is decisive.

Does a fasting person break their fast if they insert a finger, stick, or arrow into a body orifice? The answer depends on whether the object is dry or wet and whether it settles and remains inside the cavity. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rulings on these cases, drawing on key texts like Al-Bada'i' and Qadi Khan's Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (e.g., inserting a dry stick into the anus or vagina). The fast remains valid (sahih) unless specific conditions are met.
The focus is on the effect on fasting, not the permissibility of the act itself.
2
Locate the proof
Find the reasoning in the text: the food-on-string case shows that stability is required for breaking; this is the dalil from Al-Bada'i'.
The principle is derived from analogy (qiyas) from that case.
3
Apply the condition of istiqrar
Stability (istiqrar) of the object in the cavity is a necessary condition for breaking the fast. If the object does not settle, the fast is not broken.
This excludes cases where the object passes through without lodging.
4
Distinguish dry vs. wet
If the object is wet (with water or oil), the moisture reaching the cavity breaks the fast. Dry insertion does not, unless it settles.
The moisture acts as a medium for dissolution.
5
Note the intra-madhhab difference on spear
Qadi Khan reports an ikhtilaf on a spear remaining in the body: the correct view is that the fast is not broken because no action came from the fasting person and no nutrition reached.
This shows that accidental penetration without sustenance does not invalidate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شد الطعام بخيط وأرسله في حلقه وطرف الخيط في يده لا يفسد الصوم إلا إذا انفصل . وذكر الولوالجي أن الصائم إذا استقصى في الاستنجاء حتى بلغ مبلغ المحقنة فهذا أقل ما يكون ولو كان يفسد صومه والاستقصاء لا يفعل لأنه يورث داء عظيماً. وفي الظهيرية : ولو أدخل خشبة أو نحوها وطرفاً منها بيده لم يفسد صومه . قال في البدائع : وهذ يدل على أن استقرار الداخل في الجوف شرط لفساد الصوم ، وكذا لو أدخل أصبعه في أسته أو أدخلت المرأة في فرجها هو المختار إلا إذا كانت الأصبع مبتلة بالماء أو الدهن فحينئذ يفسد لوصول الماء أو الدهن. وقيل: إن المرأة إذا حشت الفرج الداخل فسد صومها، والصائم إذ أصابه سهم وخرج من الجانب الآخر لم يفسد صومه ولو بقي النصل في جوفه يفسد صومه اهـ . وفي شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان : وإن بقي الرمح في جوفه اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنه لا يفسد لأنه لم يوجد منه الفعل ولم يصل إليه ما فيه صلاحه.
If he tied food with a string and sent it into his throat while the end of the string is in his hand, the fast is not broken unless it detaches. Al-Walwalji mentioned that if the fasting person goes to extremes in istinja' until it reaches the level of an enema, this is the least of it, and if he were to break his fast, the extreme would not be done because it causes a serious disease. In Al-Dhahiriyyah: If he inserted a stick or similar object with its end in his hand, his fast is not broken. He said in Al-Bada'i': This indicates that stability of what enters the cavity is a condition for breaking the fast. Similarly, if he inserted his finger into his anus or a woman into her vagina, that is the chosen view unless the finger is wet with water or oil; then it breaks because of the water or oil reaching. It was said: If a woman stuffs the inner vagina, her fast breaks. If an arrow hits a fasting person and comes out the other side, his fast is not broken; but if the arrowhead remains in his cavity, it breaks his fast. In Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadi Khan: If a spear remains in his cavity, they differed; the correct view is that it does not break because no action came from him and nothing with nutrition reached him.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on inserting objects into the body while fasting. The author lists several cases: (1) Food tied to a string: as long as the string is held, no food is swallowed, so fast remains valid. (2) Going to extremes in istinja' (cleansing after using toilet) that mimics an enema: such action is discouraged, but the fast is not broken because it is not a usual method. (3) Inserting a dry stick or finger into anus or vagina: the fast is not broken unless the finger is wet, then it breaks due to moisture reaching the cavity. The principle from Al-Bada'i' is that stability (istiqrar) of the inserted object in the cavity is necessary to break the fast. (4) Arrow or spear: if it passes through without lodging, fast intact; if the head stays, it breaks. However, Qadi Khan's commentary on Al-Jami' al-Saghir notes that if a spear remains, the correct view is it does not break because the fasting person did not perform an action (no intention to break) and no nutritional substance reached the body.

Key Terms
استقرار الداخل في الجوف (istiqrar al-dakhil fi al-jawf): The stability or settling of an inserted object in the internal cavity, considered a condition for breaking the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a fasting person inserting a dry finger into the anus?
A) The fast is broken immediately.
B) The fast is not broken unless the finger is wet.
C) The fast is broken only if the finger reaches the cavity.
D) The fast becomes makruh but not invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sets stability (istiqrar) as the condition for breaking the fast through insertion, distinguishing dry from wet and accidental from intentional.

Al-Wujur and Al-Sa'ut: Medicine and Snuff During Fasting
Ruling on oral medicine and nasal snuff, including expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wujur and sa'ut during fasting and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether doubt breaks the fast.

Core Idea
Wujur and sa'ut break the fast like eating, but expiation is not required—unless you follow Abu Yusuf.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on taking medicine orally (wujur) or nasally (sa'ut) while fasting. The Hanafi school generally holds that these break the fast because a beneficial substance reaches the internal cavity, but expiation is not obligatory because the act does not resemble eating in form. However, there is an important intra-madhhab difference: Abu Yusuf requires expiation, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not. Additionally, the passage delves into the question of doubt: if one is uncertain whether the substance reached the cavity, does it break the fast? This leads to a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on wujur/sa'ut
First, note that the text states wujur and sa'ut break the fast. Understand the reason: beneficial substance reaches the internal cavity (jauf).
The text uses the phrase 'ka-aklin ma'nan' (like eating in meaning).
2
Determine whether expiation (kaffarah) is required
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold no kaffarah; Abu Yusuf disagrees. Note that the mufta bihi is no kaffarah.
The text cites Al-Walwalji for the majority view and Abu Yusuf's opposing view next.
3
Distinguish between wet and dry substances
The criterion for breaking the fast is reaching the cavity, not the state of the substance. Al-Quduri conditioned that wet normally reaches, so if it is known that wet did not reach, the fast is not broken; if dry reached, it breaks.
The text generalizes medicine to include wet and dry, then clarifies the condition.
4
Analyze the doubt scenario
When there is doubt about reaching, and the substance is wet, Abu Hanifa breaks the fast due to normal reaching; the Sahibayn say no because certainty is required. The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view.
The text explicitly mentions the dispute and the tarjih in Fath al-Qadir.
5
Understand the anatomical connection
The head cavity and belly cavity have an original passage (manfadh asli), so what reaches the head reaches the belly. This explains why snuff used at night that exits during the day does not break the fast—its exit shows it did not settle in the cavity.
The text quotes Al-Nihayah and Al-Bada'i'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الولوالجي: وأما الوجور في الفم فإنه يفسد صومه لأنه وصل إلى جوف البدن ما هو مصلح للبدن فكان أكلاً معنى لكن لا تلزمه الكفارة لانعدام الأكل صورة. وعن أبي يوسف في السعوط والوجور الكفارة، ولو استعط ليلاً فخرج نهاراً لا يفطر. وأطلق الدواء فشمل الرطب واليابس لأن العبرة للوصول لا لكونه رطباً أو يابساً، وإنما شرطه القدوري لأن الرطب هو الذي يصل إلى الجوف عادة حتى لو علم أن الرطب لم يصل لم يفسد ولو علم أن اليابس وصل فسد صومه . كذا في العناية لكن بقي ما إذا لم يعلم يقيناً أحدهما وكان رطباً فعند أبي حنيفة يفطر للوصول عادة. وقالا : لا لعدم العلم به فلا يفطر بالشك بخلاف ما إذا كان يابساً ولم يعلم فلا فطر اتفاقاً. كذا في فتح القدير : وقوله إلى جوفه عائد إلى الجائفة، وقوله إلى دماغه عائد إلى الآمة. وفي التحقيق بين الجوفين منفذاً أصلياً، فما وصل إلى جوف الرأس يصل إلى جوف البطن. كذا في النهاية والبدائع. ولهذا لو استعط ليلاً ووصل إلى الرأس ثم خرج نهاراً لا يفسد كما قدمناه ، وعلله في البدائع بأنه لما خرج علم أنه لم يصل إلى الجوف أو لم يستقر فيه .
Al-Walwalji mentioned: As for wujur (pouring medicine into the mouth), it breaks his fast because something that benefits the body reaches the body cavity, so it is like eating in meaning, but expiation is not obligatory because the form of eating is absent. From Abu Yusuf, expiation is due for sa'ut (snuff) and wujur. If he used snuff at night and it came out during the day, it does not break the fast. The medicine is generalized to include wet and dry because the consideration is reaching, not being wet or dry. However, al-Quduri conditioned that wet is what normally reaches the cavity, so if he knows the wet did not reach, it does not break; and if he knows the dry reached, it breaks. This is in Al-`Inayah. But what remains is if he does not know for certain and it was wet: according to Abu Hanifa, he breaks because of normal reaching. The two companions say: no, due to lack of knowledge, so he does not break due to doubt. Contrast with dry when he does not know: there is no breaking unanimously. This is in Fath al-Qadir: 'His statement: to his cavity refers to the deep wound (ja'ifah), and to his brain refers to the skull wound (ammah). Upon investigation, between the two cavities there is an original passage, so what reaches the head cavity reaches the belly cavity. This is in Al-Nihayah and Al-Bada'i'. Hence, if he used snuff at night and it reached the head then came out during the day, it does not break as we preceded, and Al-Bada'i' reasoned that by its exiting, it is known that it did not reach the cavity or did not settle in it.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage deals with wujur (oral medicine) and sa'ut (nasal snuff). Al-Walwalji states that wujur breaks the fast because the substance is beneficial for the body, so it is like eating in meaning (ma'nan), even though the form (surah) of swallowing is absent. However, expiation (kaffarah) is not required because the form of eating is missing. Abu Yusuf holds that expiation is required for both sa'ut and wujur. The author then discusses the difference between wet and dry medicine: the criterion is reaching the cavity (wusul), not the state of the substance. Al-Quduri conditioned that wet normally reaches, so if it is known that wet did not reach, the fast is not broken; if dry reached, it breaks. A dispute arises when there is doubt: Abu Hanifa says if the substance is wet and we do not know for sure whether it reached, he breaks the fast because wet normally reaches. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say no, because fasting is based on certainty and doubt does not break it. The passage also explains the passage between the head cavity and belly cavity — they are connected by an original passage (manfadh asli), so what reaches the head reaches the belly. This explains why snuff at night that later exits during the day does not break the fast: because its exit proves it did not settle in the cavity.

Key Terms
الوجور (al-wujur): Pouring medicine into the mouth, usually without voluntary swallowing.
السعوط (al-sa'ut): Medicine or snuff taken through the nose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on expiation (kaffarah) for al-wujur?
A) Kaffarah is obligatory according to all.
B) Kaffarah is not obligatory because the form of eating is absent.
C) Kaffarah is obligatory only if the medicine is dry.
D) Kaffarah is obligatory according to Abu Yusuf only.

Key Takeaway
Wujur and sa'ut break the fast because beneficial substance reaches the cavity, but expiation is not required; in case of doubt, the fast is not broken (relying on Sahibayn).

Drops in the Urethra: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Breaking the Fast
Ruling on drops into urethra and disagreement with Abu Yusuf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on drops in the male urethra during fasting, explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa's two companions and Abu Yusuf, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Drops in the male urethra do not break the fast according to the preferred Hanafi view, because no passage exists for them to reach the stomach.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a specific scenario: a fasting person puts drops (iqtar) into the urethra. The ruling hinges on a medical question: is there a passage between the bladder and the stomach cavity? The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say no, so the drops cannot reach the stomach and the fast remains valid. However, Abu Yusuf himself disagreed, and the text explores the intra-Hanafi dispute, the reasoning behind each view, and the eventually preferred position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (Drops in the male urethra). What is the initial ruling stated in the text? (It does not break the fast).
The text says 'لا يفطر' (does not break).
2
Locate the Intra-Madhhab Disagreement
Who disagrees with this ruling? (Abu Yusuf). What is the basis of the disagreement? (Whether there is a passage between bladder and stomach).
Abu Yusuf analogizes to an enema, which breaks the fast.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response and Proof
How do the two companions refute Abu Yusuf? They argue the urethra is an exit only, and urine reaches the bladder by transudation (رشح) which does not reverse. The medical fact is that no passage exists for drops to enter the stomach.
Al-Hidayah comments this is medical, not purely fiqh.
4
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
What is the practical difference? If drops break the fast, qada' is required. If not, the fast remains valid.
This determines whether the fasting person must make up the day.
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi Position
Which view is relied upon for fatwa? The text says Shaykh Qasim in his Tashih preferred the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) that it does not break the fast.
This is the standard Hanafi fatwa position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'And if he drops into his urethra' — meaning no, he does not break his fast. It is general, covering water and oil. This is according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf, and it is based on whether there is a passage between the bladder and the cavity or not, and it is not a real difference; they said no. The reaching of urine from the stomach to the bladder is by transudation, and what comes out by transudation does not return by transudation, like a jar whose top is sealed and thrown into a basin: water comes out but does not enter. Al-Walwalji mentioned this and said yes. In Al-Hidayah: This is not from the chapter of fiqh because it relates to medicine. The disagreement is when it reaches the bladder; as long as it is in the shaft of the penis, it does not break the fast unanimously, as in Al-Khulasah. Fath al-Qadir objected to what is in Khizanat al-Akmal: if he stuffed his penis with cotton and concealed it, it breaks like stuffing it, and he elaborated. This is in Ghayat al-Bayan. In Al-Walwaljiyyah, it breaks by consensus. Fath al-Qadir explained it as similar to an enema. In Sharh al-Majma' by Ibn Farishtah: The urethra is the exit of urine and the exit of milk from the breast. His statement: 'And it is disliked to taste something'
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on putting drops (iqtar) into the urethra (ihlil) while fasting. The text states that according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it does not break the fast, contrary to Abu Yusuf's own view? Actually the text says 'عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف' meaning that the ruling of not breaking is from the two companions, and Abu Yusuf disagrees (he says it breaks). The basis is whether there is a passage between the bladder and the stomach cavity. The two companions say no, so drops in the urethra do not reach the stomach. Al-Walwalji confirms. Al-Hidayah comments that this is medical, not fiqh. The disagreement is only when it reaches the bladder; while in the urethral shaft, it is unanimous that it does not break. The author then notes an objection from Fath al-Qadir regarding stuffing cotton, and the preference of the two companions' view. However, Shaykh Qasim preferred the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) that it does not break. The passage also distinguishes the male urethra from the female vagina, where drops break the fast by consensus. It ends with a note about the dislike (karaha) of tasting something, likely continuing to the next ruling.

Key Terms
الإحليل (al-ihlil): The urethra, the passage for urine from the bladder to the outside of the body.
المثانة (al-mathana): The bladder, where urine collects.
الترشح (al-tarashshuh): Transudation – the process by which fluid passes through a membrane, like urine from the stomach to the bladder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The fast is broken.
B) The fast is not broken.
C) The fast is broken only if the drops reach the bladder.
D) The fast becomes invalid only if oil is used.

Key Takeaway
Drops in the male urethra do not break the fast in the preferred Hanafi view because the urethra is an exit-only passage with no connection to the digestive system.

Inserting into the Urethra During Fasting – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute with anatomical reasoning and tarjih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inserting liquid into the urethra during fasting and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf, including the underlying anatomical reasoning and the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Inserting into the urethra does not break the fast because there is no passage to the stomach.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a specific case: does putting liquid or oil into the urethra (ihlil) break the fast? The matn states it does not, but there is a known intra-Hanafi disagreement. The commentary unpacks the anatomical basis, the positions of the three imams, and the later tarjih. Understanding this ruling helps sharpen the distinction between what reaches the stomach (jawf) and what does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the fast remains valid. Abu Yusuf holds it breaks the fast. There is also a narration from Abu Hanifa agreeing with Abu Yusuf.
2
Analyze the anatomical basis
The disagreement hinges on whether there is a natural passage between the bladder (mathanah) and the stomach (jawf). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say no, so the liquid does not reach the stomach. Abu Yusuf apparently holds that there is a passage.
The text notes this is a medical issue, not purely fiqh.
3
Understand the evidence
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad analogize it to a sealed jar in water: water seeps out but does not enter. So urine seeps from stomach to bladder but not back. Thus the liquid cannot travel from bladder to stomach.
4
Note the tarjih and exceptions
Sheikh Qasim preferred the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) that it does not break the fast. This is the mufta bihi. However, once liquid reaches the bladder, some disagreement exists; as long as it stays in the urethra, all agree it does not break.
The text also mentions that inserting into the vagina breaks the fast by consensus.
5
Apply to related cases
The commentary contrasts with enema (huqnah) which does break the fast because it reaches the cavity. Also discusses inserting a stick or cotton and ear drops.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وإن أقطر في إحليله (لا أي لا يفطر . أطلقه فشمل الماء والدهن، وهذا عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف وهو مبني على أنه هل بين المثانة والجوف منفذ أم لا وهو ليس باختلاف فيه على التحقيق فقالا : لا. ووصول البول من المعدة إلى المثانة بالترشح وما يخرج رشحاً لا يعود رشحاً كالجرة إذا سد رأسها وألقي في الحوض يخرج منها الماء ولا يدخل فيها ذكره الولوالجي وقال : نعم قال في الهداية : وهذا ليس من باب الفقه لأنه متعلق بالطب. والخلاف فيما إذا وصل إلى المثانة أما ما دام في قصبة الذكر فلا يفسد صومه اتفاقاً كذا في الخلاصة. وعارض به في فتح القدير ما في خزانة الأكمل لو حشا ذكره بقطنة فغيبها أنه يفسد كاحتشائها وأطال فيه وصحح في التحفة قول أبي يوسف ومحمد وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة لكن رجح الشيخ قاسم في تصحيحه ظاهر الرواية وقيد بالإحليل الذي هو مخرج البول من الذكر لأن الإقطار في قبل المرأة يفسد الصوم بلا خلاف على الصحيح. كذا في غاية البيان. وفي الولوالجية أنه يفسد بالإجماع. وعلله في فتح القدير بأنه شبيه بالحقنة. وفي شرح المجمع لابن فرشته : الإحليل مخرج البول ومخرج اللبن من الثدي

قوله (وكره ذوق شيء) المحقنة يوجب أن ما هنا تحريف من النساخ قوله : (لأنه لم يوجد منه (الفعل) ذكر في النهر أنه يشكل عليه مسألة الاستنجاء السابقة ومسألة ما إذا أدخل خشبة وغيبها حيث يفطر في الصورتين مع أنه لم يوجد منه الفعل أعني صورة الفطر وهو الابتلاع ولا معناه وهو ما فيه صلاحه لما ذكروه من أن إيصال الماء إلى داء عظيماً قال : وجوابه أن هذا مبني على تفسير الصورة بالابتلاع كما . في الهداية، والأولى تفسيرها بالإدخال بصنعه كما علل به الإمام قاضيخان الفساد بإدخال الماء أذنه بأنه موصل إليه بفعله فلا يعتبر فيه صلاح البدن كما لو أدخل خشبة وغيبها إلى آخر كلامه ا هـ . نعم يرد ذلك على تعليل الولوالجي لعدم الفساد بإدخال الماء أذنه، ويرد عليه أيضاً كما قاله الرملي: الإفطار بوصول الماء إلى الدماغ في الإستنشاق فإنه إذا فسد مع عدم القصد فكيف لا يفسد في الإقطار والسعوط مع ثم : قال : لكن مع ذلك هو معارض بما في الشروح، وإذا عارض ما في الفتاوى ما في الشروح يعمل بما في الشروح ا هـ وفيه أن ما في الولوالجية اختاره في الهداية كما . والهداية مر معدودة من المتون وهي مقدمة على الشروح فأين المعارضة؟ .

قوله : (وصحح في التحفة قول أبي يوسف ومحمد قال الرملي : تقدم أن محمداً مع أبي يوسف لكن قال : ومحمد توقف فيه، وقيل هو مع أبي يوسف والأظهر أنه مع أبي حنيفة فما تقدم نقله هو
His saying: 'And if he puts drops into his urethra' (no, meaning it does not break the fast). He made it absolute, so it includes water and oil. This is according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf. It is based on whether there is a passage between the bladder and the stomach. In reality, there is no difference of opinion: the two said no. And the arrival of urine from the stomach to the bladder is by seepage, and what comes out by seepage does not return by seepage, like a jar whose mouth is sealed and placed in a basin: water comes out of it but does not enter it. Al-Walwalji mentioned this and said: Yes. He said in al-Hidayah: This is not from the chapter of fiqh because it relates to medicine. The disagreement is when it reaches the bladder. As long as it is in the urethra, it does not break his fast by consensus, as in al-Khulasa. And it was opposed in Fath al-Qadir by what is in Khizanat al-Akmal: if he stuffed his penis with a cotton piece and made it disappear, it breaks the fast like its stuffing. He elaborated on it. And he corrected in al-Tuhfa the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and it is a narration from Abu Hanifa, but Sheikh Qasim in his Tashih preferred the apparent narration. He restricted it to the urethra, which is the exit of urine from the penis, because putting drops into the woman's vagina breaks the fast without disagreement according to the correct view, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And in al-Walwaljiyya, it breaks the fast by consensus. And he gave the reason in Fath al-Qadir that it resembles an enema. And in Sharh al-Mujma' by Ibn Farishta: The urethra is the exit of urine and the exit of milk from the breast.

His saying: '(And disliked is tasting something)' - the enema requires that what is here is a scribal alteration. His saying: '(Because there is no action from him) mentioned in al-Nahr that it is problematic for the previous issue of istinja' and the issue of if he inserted a stick and made it disappear, where it breaks the fast in both situations even though there is no action from him, I mean the form of breaking the fast which is swallowing, nor its meaning which is what benefits him, because of what they mentioned that conveying water for a grave ailment. He said: Its answer is that this is based on interpreting the form as swallowing, as in al-Hidayah. The preferred interpretation is as entering by his action, as Imam Qadikhan gave the reason for breaking the fast by entering water into his ear: because he is a means to it by his action, so the benefit to the body is not considered, just as if he inserted a stick and made it disappear, to the end of his words. Yes, that is a criticism of al-Walwalji's reasoning for not breaking the fast by entering water into his ear. And also it is criticized, as al-Ramli said: Breaking the fast by water reaching the brain in istinshaq (sniffing), for if it breaks the fast without intention, how does it not break the fast in iqtar and su'ut (snuff) with intention? Then he said: But despite that, it is opposed by what is in the commentaries. And if what is in the fatwa collections opposes what is in the commentaries, we act on what is in the commentaries. And in it, what is in al-Walwaljiyya is chosen in al-Hidayah, and al-Hidayah is counted among the matn texts and is given precedence over commentaries, so where is the opposition?

His saying: 'And he corrected in al-Tuhfa the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.' Al-Ramli said: It was stated earlier that Muhammad is with Abu Yusuf, but he said: Muhammad hesitated about it, and it is said he is with Abu Yusuf, but the most apparent is that he is with Abu Hanifa. So what was transmitted earlier is that he is with the most apparent.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the ruling of putting liquid or oil into the urethra during fasting. The matn states that it does not break the fast. The commentator explains that this ruling is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, contrary to Abu Yusuf who holds that it breaks the fast. The disagreement is based on whether there is a natural passage between the bladder and the stomach. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold there is no passage, so the liquid does not reach the stomach. Abu Yusuf disagrees. The commentator notes that this is a medical issue, not purely fiqh. By consensus, as long as the substance remains in the urethra (qasabah), it does not break the fast. The passage then discusses objections and tarjih: some sources prefer the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, while others uphold the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) that it does not break the fast. Sheikh Qasim preferred the apparent narration. The commentator also clarifies that inserting into the woman's vagina breaks the fast by consensus. The discussion extends to related issues like inserting a stick or cotton and comparisons with enema and ear drops.

Key Terms
الإحليل (al-ihlil): The passage for urine from the male organ (urethra).
المثانة (al-mathanah): The bladder, the organ that stores urine.
الجوف (al-jawf): The body cavity, specifically the stomach or internal space. Reaching the jawf breaks the fast.
الحقنة (al-huqnah): Enema; insertion of liquid into the rectum.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the source, what is the primary basis for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's ruling that inserting liquid into the urethra does not break the fast?
A) The liquid is not food or drink.
B) There is no passage between the bladder and the stomach.
C) The act is not intentional.
D) The amount is negligible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that inserting liquid into the urethra does not break the fast, based on the absence of a passage to the stomach, as preferred by Sheikh Qasim from the zahir al-riwayah.

Ruling on Tasting and Chewing Without Excuse During Fasting: Makruh but Not Invalidating
Commentary on the ruling of tasting and chewing without excuse: it is makruh but does not break the fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for tasting and chewing food without excuse during a fast, distinguish between obligatory and voluntary fasts, and identify the conditions that make the act permissible.

Core Idea
Tasting or chewing without excuse is disliked but does not break the fast.

You are fasting and need to check the salt in the soup. Is it allowed? This lesson from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that while tasting food is not haram, it is makruh tanzihan (disliked) because it exposes the fast to potential invalidation. However, if there is a legitimate need—like a woman dealing with a bad-tempered husband or a mother chewing food for her toddler—the dislike disappears. We will also explore an intra-Hanafi difference on voluntary fasts.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
The matn says tasting and chewing without excuse is makruh. Identify that it is disliked but does not break the fast.
2
Identify the condition that changes the ruling
The presence of an 'udhr (excuse) makes the act permissible (not makruh). Examples from the text include a woman with a bad-tempered husband or a mother chewing for her child.
3
Note the scope: obligatory vs. voluntary fasts
The text first gives an absolute statement, then clarifies that some scholars specify the dislike for obligatory fasts only, based on a weak narration.
The weak narration is from Hasan ibn Ziyad.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute and the relied-upon position
The narration of Hasan ibn Ziyad allows breaking a voluntary fast without excuse, so tasting would not be makruh. But the mufta bihi is that it is makruh in both, because breaking a voluntary fast without excuse is not permitted.
This illustrates the principle that weak narrations are not relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقطر في إحليله لا وكره ذوق شيء ومضغه بلا عذر ومضغ العلك لا كحل ودهن
ومضغه بلا عذر لما فيه من تعريض الصوم للفساد ولا يفسد صومه لعدم الفطر صورة ومعنى. قيد بقوله بلا عذر لأن الذوق بعذر لا يكره كما قال في الخانية فيمن كان زوجها سيء الخلق أو سيدها لا بأس بأن تذوق بلسانها وليس من الأعذار الذوق عند الشراء ليعرف الجيد من الرديء بل يكره كما ذكره في الولوالجي وتبعه في فتح القدير. وفي المحيط: يجوز أن يقال لا بأس به كي لا يغبن والمضغ بعذر بأن لم تجد المرأة من يمضغ لصبيها الطعام من حائض أو نفساء أو غيرهما ممن لا يصوم ولم تجد طبيخاً و لبناً حليباً لا بأس به للضرورة، ألا ترى أنه يجوز لها الإفطار إذا خافت على الولد فالمضغ أولى. وأطلق في النسوم فشمل الفرض والنفل وقد قالوا إن الكراهة في الفرض، أما في الصوم التطوع فلا يكره الذوق والمضغ فيه لأن الإفطار فيه مباح للعذر وغيره على رواية الحسن . كذا في التجنيس. وتبعه في النهاية وفتح القدير وغيرهما وفيه بحث لأن المذهب أن الإفطار في التطوع لا يحل من غير عذر فما كان تعريضاً له عليه يكره لأن كلامنا عند عدم العذر، وأما على رواية الحسن فمسلم وسيأتي أنها شاذة .
He put drops into his urethra: no, and disliked is tasting something and chewing it without excuse, and chewing mastic, not kohl and oil. And chewing it without excuse because it exposes the fast to corruption, but it does not break his fast because there is no breaking of the fast in form and meaning. He restricted it by saying 'without excuse' because tasting with an excuse is not disliked, as he said in al-Khaniyya: For one whose husband is bad-tempered or her master, there is no harm in her tasting with her tongue. And among the excuses is tasting at the time of purchase to know the good from the bad; rather it is disliked, as mentioned in al-Walwalji and followed in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Muhit: It can be said there is no harm in it so that he is not cheated. And chewing with an excuse: if a woman cannot find someone to chew food for her child among a menstruating woman, a postpartum woman, or others who are not fasting, and she cannot find cooked food or fresh milk, there is no harm due to necessity. Do you not see that it is permissible for her to break the fast if she fears for the child? So chewing is more appropriate. And he made it absolute in 'al-nasum' (likely 'al-sawm') so it includes obligatory and voluntary. And they said: the dislike is in obligatory, as for voluntary fast, tasting and chewing are not disliked because breaking the fast is permissible with excuse and without excuse according to the narration of Hasan. This is in al-Tajnisi. And it was followed in al-Nihaya, Fath al-Qadir, and others. And there is discussion because the madhhab is that breaking a voluntary fast is not permissible without excuse, so what exposes it to that is disliked because our discussion is when there is no excuse. As for the narration of Hasan, it is accepted but it will come that it is weak.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on tasting (dhawq) and chewing (madhgh) food without an excuse during a fast. The matn states that these acts are makruh (disliked) but do not break the fast. The commentator explains that the dislike is because it exposes the fast to potential invalidation. However, if there is a legitimate excuse, such as a woman tasting food for a bad-tempered husband or a mother chewing food for her child, then it is not disliked. The discussion also covers the difference between obligatory (fard) and voluntary (nafl) fasts. According to the narration of Hasan ibn Ziyad, breaking a voluntary fast is permissible even without excuse, so tasting would not be disliked. But the relied-upon position of the madhhab is that even voluntary fasts cannot be broken without excuse, so tasting without excuse is disliked in both types. The narration of Hasan is considered weak (shadhdhah).

Key Terms
الذوق (al-dhawq): Tasting food, typically to check seasoning or quality.
المضغ (al-madhgh): Chewing food, e.g., for a child or for oneself.
العذر (al-'udhr): A legitimate excuse that makes the act permissible and removes dislike.
رواية الحسن (riwayat al-Hasan): A narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad, considered weak (shadhdh) compared to the main narrations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is it not makruh to taste or chew food during a fast?
A) If the food is halal.
B) If there is an excuse, such as a woman tasting for her husband or a mother chewing for her child.
C) If it is done quickly.
D) If the fast is voluntary.

Key Takeaway
Tasting or chewing food without a legitimate excuse during a fast is makruh tanzih but does not break the fast; with an excuse, the dislike is removed.

Ruling on Chewing Mastic Gum During Fasting
Commentary on the ruling of chewing mastic gum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on chewing gum during fasting and distinguish between the apparent narration and the later scholars' position, identifying which types break the fast.

Core Idea
Chewing gum is makruh but not a fast-breaker in the apparent narration; later scholars ruled black or unchewed gum breaks the fast.

The Hanafi school has a nuanced position on chewing gum during fasting. While the earlier authoritative texts (zahir al-riwayah) deem it merely disliked, later scholars refined this based on the physical properties of gum. This lesson unpacks that refinement and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The matn states 'chewing mastic' is disliked. Recognize that the default in the apparent narration is makruh tanzih, not a fast-breaker.
The ruling applies to any type of gum in the apparent narration.
2
Understand the reasoning behind dislike
Two reasons: it threatens the fast (exposes it to corruption) and it invites accusation of breaking the fast. This applies even if it does not actually break.
3
Trace the later scholars' exception
Later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirun) differentiated: if the gum is white and pre-chewed by another, it only melts but does not crumble, so it remains only makruh. If it is black or not pre-chewed, it disintegrates and particles may reach the throat, thus breaking the fast.
The researcher (al-Muhaqqiq) chose this view as mufta bihi.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a specific gum type, determine its color and whether it has been pre-chewed. White pre-chewed: only makruh. Black or unchewed: breaks the fast.
This is the operative position for fatwa.
5
Note gender-based recommendations
For non-fasting individuals, gum is not disliked. For men, it is recommended to avoid it unless needed (e.g., bad breath). For women, it is recommended because it serves as a siwak (tooth-stick).
This highlights the different considerations based on custom and need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومضغ العلك أي ويكره مضغه في ظاهر الرواية لما فيه من تعريض الصوم على الفساد ولأنه يتهم بالإفطار أطلقه فأفاد أنه لا فرق بين علك وعلك في أنه لا يفطر، وإنما يكره وهو ظاهر الرواية. كذا في غاية البيان والمتأخرون قيدوه بأن يكون أبيض وقد مضغه غيره، أما إذا لم يمضغه غيره أو كان أسود مطلقاً يفطره لأنه إذا لم يمضغه غيره يتفتت فيتجاوز شيء منه حلقه، وإذا مضغه غيره لا يتففت إلا أن الأسود يذوب بالمضغ، فأما الأبيض لا يذوب، وإطلاق محمد يدل على أن الكل سواء كذا ذكره الولوالجي في فتاواه، واختار المحقق كلام المتأخرين لأن إطلاق محمد محمول عليه للقطع بأنه معلل بعدم الوصول فإذا فرض في بعض العلك معرفة الوصول منه عادة وجب الحكم فيه بالفساد لأنه كالمتيقن وقال فخر الإسلام وعموم ما قال محمد في الجامع الصغير إشارة إلى أنه لا يكره العلك لغير الصائم ولكن يستحب للرجال تركه إلا لعذر مثل أن يكون في فمه بخر ا هـ . وأما في حق النساء فالمستحب لهن فعله لأنه سواكهن. وفي فتح القدير : والأولى الكراهة للرجال إلا لحاجة لأن الدليل أعني التشبه يقتضيها في حقهم حالياً عن المعارض. وفي
الأظهر، وما تقدم نقله هو الأظهر، وما تأخر على خلاف الأظهر قوله : (وأطلق في الصوم الخ) قال في الإمداد : كذا أطلقه في الهداية والكنز وشرح المختار فشمل النفل لما أنه لا يباح فيه الفطر بلا عذر على المذهب، ومن فيده بالفرض كشمس الأئمة الحلواني ونفى كراهة الذوق في النفل إنما هو على رواية جواز الإفطار في النفل بلا عذر قوله : ( وفيه بحث الخ) قال في النهر : يمكن أن يقال إنما لم يكره في النفل وكـه في الفرض إظهاراً لتفاوت المرتبتين .
His saying: 'And chewing mastic' - i.e., it is disliked to chew it in the apparent narration because it exposes the fast to corruption and because he is accused of breaking the fast. He made it absolute, so it indicates that there is no difference between one mastic and another in that it does not break the fast; rather it is only disliked, and that is the apparent narration. This is in Ghayat al-Bayan. And the later scholars restricted it: that it be white and have been chewed by someone else. As for if it has not been chewed by someone else, or it is absolutely black, it breaks the fast because if it has not been chewed by someone else, it crumbles and something passes his throat. If it has been chewed by someone else, it does not crumble. However, black gum melts with chewing, whereas white does not melt. The absolute statement of Muhammad indicates that all are the same, as al-Walwalji mentioned in his fatwas. And the researcher chose the statement of the later scholars because the absolute statement of Muhammad is interpreted in light of that, for the certainty that it is reasoned by non-reach. So if it is assumed in some mastic that reach from it is known habitually, then the ruling of breaking the fast is obligatory because it is like certainty. And Fakhr al-Islam said: The generality of what Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir indicates that mastic is not disliked for the non-fasting person, but it is recommended for men to leave it except for an excuse such as if he has bad breath. As for women, it is recommended for them to do it because it is their siwak. And in Fath al-Qadir: The preferred view is dislike for men except for need, because the evidence, i.e., resemblance, necessitates it for them apart from opposition. And the most apparent, and what was transmitted earlier is the most apparent, and what is later is contrary to the most apparent. His saying: 'And he made it absolute in fasting' etc. He said in al-Imdad: Similarly, he made it absolute in al-Hidayah, al-Kanz, and Sharh al-Mukhtar, so it includes voluntary because it is not permissible to break the fast without excuse according to the madhhab. And whoever from the obligatory like Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani negated the dislike of tasting in voluntary only according to the narration of permissibility of breaking the voluntary fast without excuse. His saying: 'And there is discussion' etc. He said in al-Nahr: It can be said that it was only not disliked in voluntary, like in obligatory, to show the difference between the two ranks.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling on chewing mastic gum ('ilk) during a fast. The matn states it is makruh (disliked). The commentator explains that in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), chewing gum is disliked but does not break the fast regardless of the type of gum. However, later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) refined this: if the gum is white and has already been chewed by someone else, it only melts but does not disintegrate, so it does not break the fast and is only makruh. But if it is black or has not been pre-chewed, it disintegrates and particles may reach the throat, thus breaking the fast. The commentator notes that Imam Muhammad's general statement is understood in light of this refinement. For non-fasting individuals, gum is not disliked; for men it is recommended to avoid it unless there is a need like bad breath. For women, it is recommended to chew gum as it serves as a siwak (tooth-stick). The passage also clarifies that the discussion about dislike in voluntary fasts is based on a weak narration; the relied-upon position is that it is disliked in both obligatory and voluntary fasts.

Key Terms
العلك (al-'ilk): Mastic gum, a type of chewing gum.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent/well-known transmission from the founding imams, forming the core of the school.
المتأخرون (al-muta'akhkhirun): Later Hanafi scholars who refined rulings based on new evidence or interpretation.
السواك (al-siwak): A tooth-stick used for oral hygiene, recommended for both men and women.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirun), which type of gum is considered to break the fast?
A) White gum that has been chewed by someone else.
B) Black gum or gum that has not been chewed by someone else.
C) Any gum, regardless of type.
D) Only gum that is flavored.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi position: white pre-chewed gum is only makruh; black or unchewed gum breaks the fast.

The Ruling on Tasting and Chewing While Fasting: Dislike (Karāhah) Without Invalidation
Commentary expanding on the matn ruling about disliked actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tasting and chewing during a fast, identify when karāhah applies, and distinguish between obligatory and supererogatory fasts.

Core Idea
Tasting and chewing without excuse do not break the fast but are disliked due to risk of nullification.

In Hanafi fiqh, not every action that risks the fast actually breaks it. This lesson examines two such actions—tasting food and chewing—and clarifies when they are simply disliked (makruh tanzih) versus permitted. The commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq unpacks the nuances, including excuses that remove the dislike, the distinction between obligatory and supererogatory fasts, and the special case of chewing gum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being discussed and its legal classification. The passage states that tasting and chewing without excuse are disliked (makruh) but do not invalidate the fast.
The matn explicitly says 'kariha' (disliked), and the commentary confirms it is not a nullifier.
2
Understand the reasoning and proof
The commentary gives the rationale: 'because it exposes the fast to corruption.' The proof is based on istihsan—avoiding actions that risk nullification, even if they do not actually break the fast. This is a principle of protecting the worship.
No specific hadith is cited; the dalil is the general legal principle.
3
Apply the exception for excuses
The ruling is qualified by 'without excuse.' The commentary provides examples: tasting for an ill-tempered husband or chewing food for an infant when no other option exists removes the dislike. Commercial tasting, however, is not an excuse.
The excuse must be genuine necessity; tasting to test quality for purchase is not excused.
4
Differentiate obligatory vs. supererogatory fasts
The commentary addresses a disagreement: some scholars (based on a weak narration) say tasting is not disliked in voluntary fasts because breaking is permissible. But the relied-upon Hanafi position holds that breaking a voluntary fast without excuse is not allowed, so the dislike remains even in supererogatory fasts.
The distinction is important for practical fatwa: the dislike applies to both types in the mufta bihi view.
