ويكره مضغه في ظاهر الرواية لما فيه من تعريض الصوم على الفساد ولأنه يتهم بالإفطار أطلقه فأفاد أنه لا فرق بين علك وعلك في أنه لا يفطر، وإنما يكره وهو ظاهر الرواية. كذا في غاية البيان والمتأخرون قيدوه بأن يكون أبيض وقد مضغه غيره، أما إذا لم يمضغه غيره أو كان أسود مطلقاً يفطره لأنه إذا لم يمضغه غيره يتفتت فيتجاوز شيء منه حلقه، وإذا مضغه غيره لا يتففت إلا أن الأسود يذوب بالمضغ، فأما الأبيض لا يذوب، وإطلاق محمد يدل على أن الكل سواء كذا ذكره الولوالجي في فتاواه، واختار المحقق كلام المتأخرين لأن إطلاق محمد محمول عليه للقطع بأنه معلل بعدم الوصول فإذا فرض في بعض العلك معرفة الوصول منه عادة وجب الحكم فيه بالفساد لأنه كالمتيقن وقال فخر الإسلام وعموم ما قال محمد في الجامع الصغير إشارة إلى أنه لا يكره العلك لغير الصائم ولكن يستحب للرجال تركه إلا لعذر مثل أن يكون في فمه بخر. وأما في حق النساء فالمستحب لهن فعله لأنه سواكهن. وفي فتح القدير: والأولى الكراهة للرجال إلا لحاجة لأن الدليل أعني التشبه يقتضيها في حقهم حالياً عن المعارض. وفي الأظهر، وما تقدم نقله هو الأظهر، وما تأخر على خلاف الأظهر. قوله: (وأطلق في الصوم الخ) قال في الإمداد: كذا أطلقه في الهداية والكنز وشرح المختار فشمل النفل لما أنه لا يباح فيه الفطر بلا عذر على المذهب، ومن فيده بالفرض كشمس الأئمة الحلواني ونفى كراهة الذوق في النفل إنما هو على رواية جواز الإفطار في النفل بلا عذر. قوله: (وفيه بحث الخ) قال في النهر: يمكن أن يقال إنما لم يكره في النفل وكه في الفرض إظهاراً لتفاوت المرتبتين.
And he disliked tasting something and chewing it without excuse, and chewing gum, not kohl and oil. And chewing without excuse because it exposes fasting to corruption, but his fast is not invalidated because there is no breaking in form and meaning. He restricted it with 'without excuse' because tasting with an excuse is not disliked, as stated in Al-Khaniya: if her husband is ill-tempered or her master, there is no harm in her tasting with her tongue. Tasting at the time of purchase to know good from bad is not an excuse; rather it is disliked as mentioned in Al-Walwaliji and followed in Fath al-Qadir. In Al-Muhit: it can be said that it is not harmful so that he is not cheated. And chewing with an excuse, like if a woman does not find someone to chew food for her child from a menstruating woman or a woman in post-natal bleeding or others who are not fasting, and she does not find cooked food or milk, there is no harm due to necessity. Do you not see that it is permissible for her to break her fast if she fears for the child, so chewing is more appropriate. And he generalized in As-Sawm, so it includes obligatory and supererogatory. They said that the dislike is in obligatory, but in supererogatory fast, tasting and chewing are not disliked because breaking the fast is permissible with or without excuse according to the narration of Al-Hasan. Thus in Al-Tajnis. Followed by Al-Nihaya, Fath al-Qadir, and others. There is a discussion because the school holds that breaking the supererogatory fast is not permissible without excuse, so what exposes it to corruption is disliked because our discussion is in the absence of excuse. As for the narration of Al-Hasan, it is accepted but it is anomalous. His statement: (and chewing gum) i.e., it is disliked in the apparent narration because it exposes the fast to corruption and because he is suspected of breaking the fast. He [Muhammad] made it general, indicating that there is no difference between gum and gum in that it does not break the fast, it is only disliked, and that is the apparent narration. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. The later scholars restricted it to white gum that has been chewed by someone else. As for if it has not been chewed by another, or if it is black absolutely, it breaks the fast because if it has not been chewed by another, it crumbles and something passes through his throat. If it has been chewed by another, it does not crumble except that black dissolves with chewing, while white does not dissolve. The absolute statement of Muhammad indicates that all are equal, as Al-Walwaliji mentioned in his Fatawa. The muhaqqiq chose the statement of the later scholars because Muhammad's absolute statement is interpreted in that context, since it is definitively reasoned by non-reach; so if in some gums it is normally known to reach, it must be judged invalid because it is like certain. And Fakhr al-Islam said: The generality of what Muhammad said in Al-Jami' al-Saghir indicates that gum is not disliked for the non-fasting person, but it is recommended for men to leave it except for an excuse like bad breath in his mouth. As for women, it is recommended for them to do it because it is their siwak. In Fath al-Qadir: The preferable is dislike for men except for need, because the evidence, namely imitation, requires it in their case without countervailing. And the more apparent is what preceded, and what came later is against the more apparent. His statement: (And he generalized in As-Sawm...) He said in Al-Imdad: Thus he made it general in Al-Hidaya, Al-Kanz, and Sharh al-Mukhtar, so it includes supererogatory because breaking it is not permissible without excuse according to the school. And whoever excludes supererogatory like Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani negated dislike in supererogatory only based on the narration of permissibility of breaking supererogatory without excuse. His statement: (And there is a discussion...) He said in Al-Nahr: It can be said that it is not disliked in supererogatory in contrast to obligatory to show the difference between the ranks.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The matn states that tasting food and chewing it without excuse, as well as chewing gum, are disliked (makruh) actions while fasting, while using kohl and oil are not. The commentary first explains the reason for dislike: these actions expose the fast to the risk of corruption, though they do not actually invalidate it because no substance enters the stomach in a meaningful way. The author then restricts the dislike to cases without an excuse; if there is a valid need, like a woman testing food for her ill-tempered husband or a mother chewing food for her infant when no other alternative exists, it is permissible and not disliked. The commentary also addresses the difference between obligatory and voluntary fasts: according to the stronger view, the dislike applies to obligatory fasts only. For voluntary fasts, some narrations allow breaking without excuse, so tasting is not disliked, but the Hanafi school holds that breaking a voluntary fast without excuse is not permissible, so the dislike remains. The passage then discusses gum in detail, noting that in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwaya) all gums are disliked but do not break the fast. However, later scholars distinguished: white gum previously chewed by another is only disliked, while black gum or gum not chewed by another may break the fast because it crumbles or dissolves. The author concludes by preferring the later view. Finally, the commentary addresses the ruling for non-fasting persons: gum is not disliked for them, but it is recommended for men to avoid it unless needed, while for women it is recommended as a substitute for siwak.

Key Terms
ذوق (dhawq): Tasting food with the tongue without swallowing.
مضغ (madgh): Chewing food without swallowing, typically to prepare for an infant or to test taste.
علك (‘ilk): Chewing gum (masticatory substance) that does not dissolve fully when chewed.
عذر (‘udhr): Valid excuse such as necessity, tasting for a family member, or chewing for a child.
كراهة (karāhah): Dislike; in Hanafi fiqh, makruh tanzih means it is better to avoid but no sin and the act remains valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered a valid excuse (udhr) to remove the dislike of tasting during a fast?
A) A woman tasting food for her ill-tempered husband
B) A person tasting food to check its quality for purchase
C) A mother chewing food for her infant when no other alternative exists
D) A woman tasting food for her master

Key Takeaway
Tasting and chewing during a fast are disliked but not invalidating; valid excuses remove the dislike, and the ruling applies to both obligatory and supererogatory fasts in the relied-upon position.

Rulings on Kohl, Oiling Mustache, and Beard Trimming During Fasting
Commentary on the matn stating that kohl and oiling the mustache are not disliked, with a discussion on beard and mustache rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on using kohl and oiling the mustache during fasting, and explain the Hanafi position on beard trimming including the ikhtilaf on obligation.

Core Idea
Kohl and mustache oil are not disliked in fasting; beard trimming beyond a handful is sunnah, not wajib.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that applying kohl and oiling the mustache do not invalidate or even dislike the fast, as they are acts of comfort. The discussion extends to the grooming of the beard, where the author examines whether trimming beyond a handful is obligatory or merely recommended, presenting evidence from hadith and the practice of Ibn 'Umar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
From the commentary, extract the ruling for using kohl (kuhl) and oiling the mustache (duhn al-sharib) during fasting. Note that the matn states they are not disliked.
Also note the caveat about intention: for treatment vs. adornment.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
Find the hadith quoted: 'Trim the mustaches and let the beards grow' and the practice of Ibn 'Umar cutting what exceeded a handful. Identify the type of evidence (hadith ahad) and its thubut/dalala.
The hadith is used primarily for the beard ruling, but the passage also relies on general principle that comfort is not a prohibition.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the command 'اعفوا اللحى' is interpreted to allow trimming beyond a handful, contrasting with shaving entirely. The action of Ibn 'Umar clarifies the meaning.
The sunnah measure (qabdah) is derived from Ibn 'Umar's practice, not directly from the hadith.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the disagreement within the Hanafi school: Al-Nihaya says it is wajib to cut beyond a handful, while Sheikh Ismail and the apparent view of the author consider it mustahabb.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the level of obligation.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Based on the commentary, the author leans towards mustahabb (recommended) because the stronger evidentiary basis (practice of Ibn 'Umar) and the lack of clear prohibition.
The mufta bihi today, according to many later Hanafi scholars, is that it is mustahabb, not wajib.

قوله: (لا كحل ودهن شارب) أي لا يكره. يجوز أن تكون الفاء منهما مفتوحة فيكونان مصدرين من كحل عينيه كحلاً ودهن رأسه دهناً إذا طلاة بالدهن ويجوز أن يكون مضموماً ويكون معناه ولا بأس باستعمال الكحل والدهن كذا في العناية. وفي غاية البيان: الرواية بفتح الكاف والدال. وإنما لم يكرها لما أنه نوع ارتفاق وليس من محظور الصوم وقد ندب إلى الاكتحال يوم عاشوراء وإلى الصوم فيه ولا بأس بالاكتحال للرجال إذا قصدوا به التداوي دون الزينة. ويستحسن دهن الشارب إذا لم يكن من قصده الزينة لأنه يعمل عمل الخضاب، ولا يفعل لتطويل اللحية إذا كانت بقدر المسنون وهو القبضة. كذا في الهداية. وكان ابن عمر يقبض على لحيته فيقطع ما زاد على الكف. رواه أبو داود في سننه. وما في الصحيحين عن ابن عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام "أحفوا الشوارب واعفوا اللحى" فمحمول على إعفائها من أن يأخذ غالبها أو كلها كما هو فعل مجوس الأعاجم من حلق لحاهم فيقع بذلك الجمع بين الروايات، وأما الأخذ منها وهي دون ذلك لم يفعله بعض المغاربة والمخنثة من الرجال فلم يبحه أحد. كذا في فتح القدير. وقد صرح في النهاية بوجوب قطع ما زاد على القبضة بالضم ومقتضاه الإثم بتركه. واعلم أنه لا تلازم بين قصد الجمال وقصد الزينة، فالقصد الأول لدفع الثين وإقامة ما به الوقار وإظهار النعمة شكراً لا فخراً وهو أثر أدب النفس وشهامتها، والثاني أثر ضعفها وقالوا بالخضاب وردت السنة ولم يكن لقصد الزينة ثم بعد ذلك إن حصلت زينة فقد حصلت في ضمن قصد مطلوب فلا يضره إذا لم يكن ملتفتاً إليه. كذا في فتح القدير. ولهذا قال الولوالجي في فتاواه لبس الثياب الجميلة مباح إذا كان لا يتكبر لأن التكبر حرام وتفسيره: قوله: (وقد صرح في النهاية بوجوب قطع ما زاد الخ) قال في النهر: وسمعت من بعض أعزاء الموالي أن قول النهاية "يجب" بالحاء المهملة ولا بأس بها. هـ. قال الشيخ إسماعيل: ولكنه خلاف الظاهر واستعمالهم في مثله يستحب اهـ. وكأنه لهذا - والله تعالى أعلم - لم يعول عليه الشيخ.
His statement: (Not kohl and mustache oil) i.e., not disliked. It is possible that the letter fa in both words is open, so they are verbal nouns: he applied kohl to his eyes, kohl; and oiled his head, oil. It is also possible that it is with dammah, meaning there is no harm in using kohl and oil. Thus in Al-Inaya. In Ghayat al-Bayan: the narration is with fatha on kaf and dal. They are not disliked because they are a type of comfort and are not among the prohibitions of fasting. And it is recommended to apply kohl on Ashura and to fast on that day. There is no harm in applying kohl for men if they intend treatment, not adornment. Oiling the mustache is considered good as long as one does not intend adornment, because it acts like dye. He should not do it to lengthen the beard if it is the amount of the sunnah, which is a handful. Thus in Al-Hidaya. Ibn 'Umar used to grasp his beard and cut off what exceeded the handful. Recorded by Abu Dawud. What is in the Sahihayn from Ibn 'Umar: 'Trim the mustaches and let the beards grow' is interpreted as letting them grow such that one does not take most or all of it, like the practice of the Magians shaving their beards; thus harmony between reports. As for taking from it when it is less than that, some Maghribis and effeminate men have done it, but no one has permitted it. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in Al-Nihaya it is explicitly stated that it is obligatory to cut off what exceeds the handful (qabdah), and the implication is sin for leaving it. Know that there is no necessary connection between intending beauty and intending adornment. The first intention is to remove shame and establish dignity and show the blessing as gratitude, not out of pride; it is a sign of nobility of soul. The second is a sign of weakness. They said: dyeing is established by the sunnah and was not for adornment; then after that if adornment results, it results within the context of a desired goal, so it does not harm if he is not paying attention to it. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. For this reason Al-Walwaliji said in his Fatawa: wearing beautiful clothes is permissible as long as he is not arrogant, because arrogance is haram. And as for its interpretation: His statement: (And in Al-Nihaya it is explicitly stated that it is obligatory to cut off what exceeds...) He said in Al-Nahr: I heard from some noble mawlas that the statement of Al-Nihaya 'yajib' is with an important letter ha, and there is no harm in it. End. Sheikh Ismail said: But it is against the apparent, and their usage in similar situations is 'mustahabb'. End. And it seems for this reason - and Allah knows best - the sheikh did not rely on it.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn. The matn states that using kohl (eyeliner) and oiling the mustache are not disliked during fasting. The commentary explains that these are not from the prohibited actions of fasting; rather they are a form of comfort. It notes that the narration reads with fatha on the first letters, indicating verbal nouns. The dislike is only when the intention is adornment; for medicinal purposes or to prevent harm, it is fine. The passage then shifts to a discussion about trimming the beard. It reports that Ibn 'Umar used to cut off what exceeded a handful, and that the sunnah is to let the beard grow to a handful (qabdah). The hadith 'trim the mustaches and let the beards grow' is interpreted to mean not shaving the beard entirely, but it does not forbid trimming beyond a handful. The author then notes a difference of opinion: Al-Nihaya states that it is wajib (obligatory) to trim what exceeds a handful, implying sin for leaving it, but some scholars like Sheikh Ismail consider it mustahabb (recommended) rather than obligatory. The commentary also discusses the distinction between intending beauty (jamal) and adornment (zina): the former is praiseworthy as it reflects gratitude for blessings, while the latter may be ostentatious. Wearing fine clothes or dyeing the beard is permissible if done without arrogance. This section combines rulings on fasting (kohl and oil are not disliked) with general etiquettes of grooming (beard and mustache).

Key Terms
كحل (kuhl): Antimony powder applied to the eyes; used for treatment or adornment.
دهن الشارب (duhn al-shārib): Oiling the mustache; an act of grooming.
القبضة (al-qabḍah): A handful; the measure of the beard according to sunnah, about the width of the palm.
الاعفاء (al-i‘fā’): Letting the beard grow without shaving or cutting most of it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on using kohl during fasting?
A) It is disliked (makruh) because it touches the eye.
B) It is permissible and not disliked.
C) It invalidates the fast if applied.
D) It is recommended only for women.

Key Takeaway
Kohl and mustache oil are not disliked in fasting; the sunnah beard length is a handful, and trimming beyond it is recommended, not obligatory.

Permissibility of Kohl and Oil During Fasting
Commentary on permissibility of kohl and oil during fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on applying kohl and oil while fasting, and identify the condition that changes the ruling from permissible to disliked.

Core Idea
Kohl and oil are permissible for a fasting person unless intended for adornment.

The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq states that kohl and oil are not disliked for the fasting person. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and conditions, drawing from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Raiq. The key distinction is between using these substances for benefit (like medicine) versus for beautification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The matn says 'لا كحل ودهن' meaning these are not disliked. The commentator clarifies the default ruling is permissibility (ibaha).
The linguistic analysis (fatha/damm) is not needed for the ruling.
2
Apply the condition for men
The text adds: for men it is permissible if intended for treatment, not adornment. This shows the ruling is contingent on intent.
For women, adornment may be permissible? Not specified here.
3
Note the recommended use on Ashura
Applying kohl on Ashura is recommended (mustahabb) because it is a sunnah of that day, but the general ruling remains mubah.
4
Understand the underlying reason
These acts are not from the prohibitions of fasting; they are mere benefits. The fast is only invalidated by things that enter the body or are explicitly forbidden.
This principle applies to other similar acts as well.

قوله : (لا كحل ودهن شارب) أي لا يكره. يجوز أن تكون الفاء منهما مفتوحة فيكونان مصدرين من كحل عينيه كحلاً ودهن رأسه دهناً إذا طلاة بالدهن ويجوز أن يكون مضموماً ويكون معناه ولا بأس باستعمال الكحل والدهن كذا في العناية. وفي غاية البيان: الرواية عمر بفتح الكاف والدال. وإنما لم يكرها لما أنه نوع ارتفاق وليس من محظور الصوم وقد ندب إلى الاكتحال يوم عاشوراء وإلى الصوم فيه ولا بأس بالاكتحال للرجال إذا قصدوا به التداوي دون الزينة. ويستحسن دهن الشارب إذا لم يكن من قصده الزينة لأنه يعمل عمل الخضاب، ولا يفعل لتطويل اللحية إذا كانت بقدر المسنون وهو القبضة. كذا في الهداية. وكان ابن عمر يقبض على لحيته فيقطع ما زاد على الكف . رواه أبو داود في سننه . وما في الصحيحين عن ابن عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أحفوا الشوارب واعفوا اللحى» (۱) فمحمول على إعفائها من أن يأخذ غالبها أو كلها كما هو فعل مجوس الأعاجم من حلق لحاهم فيقع بذلك الجمع بين الروايات، وأما الأخذ منها وهي دون ذلك لم يفعله بعض المغاربة والمخنثة من الرجال فلم يبحه أحد. كذا في فتح القدير : وقد صرح في النهاية بوجوب قطع ما زاد على القبضة بالضم ومقتضاه الإثم بتركه . واعلم أنه لا تلازم بين قصد الجمال وقصد الزينة، فالقصد الأول لدفع الثين وإقامة ما به الوقار وإظهار النعمة شكراً لا فخراً وهو أثر أدب النفس وشهامتها، والثاني أثر ضعفها وقالوا بالخضاب وردت السنة ولم يكن لقصد الزينة ثم بعد ذلك إن حصلت زينة فقد حصلت في ضمن قصد مطلوب فلا يضره إذا لم يكن ملتفتاً إليه . كذا في فتح القدير. ولهذا قال الولوالجي في فتاواه لبس الثياب الجميلة مباح إذا كان لا يتكبر لأن التكبر حرام وتفسيره : قوله : (وقد صرح في النهاية بوجوب قطع ما زاد الخ قال في النهر : وسمعت من بعض أعزاء الموالي أن قول النهاية يجب» بالحاء المهملة ولا بأس بها .هـ. قال الشيخ إسماعيل : ولكنه خلاف الظاهر واستعمالهم في مثله يستحب اهـ. وكأنه لهذا - والله تعالى أعلم - لم يعول عليه الشيخ (1) رواه أحمد في مسنده (٢/ ٥٢). ثم قال: (وسواك) وقبلة أن أمن أي لا يكرهان، وقد تقدم حكم القبلة، وأما السواك فلا بأس به للصائم. أطلقه فشمل الرطب واليابس والمبلول وغيره وقبل الزوال وبعده العموم قوله الا الله والولا أن أشق على أمتي لأمرتهم بالسواك عند كل وضوء وعند كل صلاة لتناوله الظهر والعصر والمغرب، وقد تقدم أحكامه في سنن الطهارة فارجع إليها. ولم يتعرض لسنة السواك للصائم ولا شك فيه كغير الصائم صرح به النهاية والله أعلم .
His statement: (No kohl, oil, mustache) meaning not disliked. It is possible that the fa' in both is fatha, making them verbal nouns from 'he applied kohl to his eyes' and 'he oiled his head' when he applied oil. Or it could be damm, meaning there is no harm in using kohl and oil, as in al-Inayah. In Ghayat al-Bayan: the narration is 'amr' with fat-ha on kaf and dal. They were not disliked because it is a type of benefit and not prohibited for fasting. It is recommended to apply kohl on Ashura and to fast on it. There is no harm for men applying kohl for medicinal purposes, not for adornment. Oiling the mustache is commendable if not intended for adornment, as it acts like dye. It is not done to lengthen the beard if it is at the recommended length of a fistful. This is in al-Hidayah. Ibn Umar would grasp his beard and cut what exceeded the fist. Reported by Abu Dawud. What is in the Sahihain from Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Trim the mustaches and let the beards grow' is interpreted to mean leaving them from taking most or all, as done by the Persian magi who shave their beards, thus reconciling narrations. As for taking from them when they are less than that, done by some Moroccans and effeminate men, no one has permitted it. In Fath al-Qadir: al-Nihayah explicitly states the obligation to cut what exceeds a fistful (with damm), implying sin in leaving it. Know that there is no connection between the intention of beauty and the intention of adornment; the first is to remove dirt and establish dignity and show blessing as gratitude not pride, and is a trace of noble character; the second is a trace of weakness. They said dyeing is recommended by sunnah not for adornment. Then if adornment results incidentally, it does not harm if one does not intend it. In Fath al-Qadir. Hence al-Walwaliji said wearing beautiful clothes is permissible if not arrogant, for arrogance is haram. His statement: (And al-Nihayah explicitly states obligation to cut what exceeds...) Al-Nahr: I heard from some friends that the word 'yajib' in al-Nihayah is with a ha' (meaning 'thabata'? Actually it says 'mustahabb'?). Sheikh Isma'il said it is against the apparent and their usage in such cases is 'yustahabb'. Therefore the shaykh did not rely on it. (Hadith). Then he said: (Miswak) and kissing if safe meaning not disliked. The ruling on kissing has preceded. As for miswak, there is no harm for the fasting person. It is general including wet, dry, moistened, etc., before and after noon due to the generality of his saying 'if it were not a burden on my Ummah, I would have commanded them to use miswak at every wudu and every prayer' which includes zuhr, asr, maghrib. Its rulings have preceded in the sunan of taharah. He did not address the sunnah of miswak for the fasting person, but there is no doubt it is the same as for a non-fasting person as stated in al-Nihayah. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage expands on the rulings of actions that are not disliked for a fasting person. The commentator begins by explaining the matn 'لا كحل ودهن شارب وسواك وقبلة إن أمن' (no kohl, oil, mustache, miswak, and kissing if safe). He clarifies that these are not disliked. He provides linguistic analysis of the words. He then discusses the optional nature of kohl and oil, noting that they are permissible because they are benefits not prohibited. Men may use kohl for medicine. Oiling the mustache is commendable if not for adornment. The discussion then digresses into a detailed treatment of beard length. The commentator cites the hadith of Ibn 'Umar on trimming the beard to a fistful. He reconciles conflicting hadiths: the command to let the beard grow means not to shave it completely. Cutting it to less than a fistful is not permitted. Al-Nihayah states it is obligatory to cut what exceeds a fistful, but some scholars interpret 'obligation' as 'strong recommendation'. The commentator then returns to miswak and kissing. He confirms that miswak is permissible at all times of the day for a fasting person, based on the general command from the Prophet. Kissing is also not disliked if one is safe from desire. The passage ends by noting that the sunnah of miswak during fasting is the same as for non-fasting.

Key Terms
كحل (kuhl): Antimony or kohl applied to the eyes
دهن (duhn): Oil applied to the head or body

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is applying kohl makruh (disliked) for a fasting man?
A) When used for medicinal purposes
B) When used on Ashura
C) When used for adornment (zīnah)
D) When used in Ramadan

Key Takeaway
Applying kohl or oil externally does not break the fast and is permissible provided it is not for prohibited adornment (for men).

Sawm — Fasting

Rulings on Miswak and Kissing During Fasting
Commentary on miswak and kissing during fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using miswak and kissing while fasting, and explain the evidence for miswak's permissibility at all times and the condition for kissing.

Core Idea
Miswak is recommended at all times for the fasting person; kissing is permissible if one is safe from desire.

The matn mentions two actions often questioned by fasting persons: using the miswak and kissing one's spouse. The commentary affirms that both are not disliked, with a detailed proof for miswak from a prophetic hadith, and a condition for kissing to avoid invalidating the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions and their default ruling
The matn says 'وسواك وقبلة أن أمن' meaning they are not disliked. The commentator confirms this.
The condition 'إن أمن' applies only to kissing.
2
Analyze the proof for miswak
The commentator cites the hadith 'لولا أن أشق على أمتي لأمرتهم بالسواك عند كل وضوء وعند كل صلاة'. This general command includes all prayer times, so it applies to zuhr and asr prayers which occur during fasting.
The hadith is from Sahihain, indicating its authenticity.
3
Apply the generality to the fast
Since the hadith encourages miswak at every prayer, and fasting does not prohibit it, the ruling is the same as for a non-fasting person. The commentator notes that Al-Nihayah confirms this.
This overrules the Shafi'i view that miswak is makruh after noon.
4
Understand the condition for kissing
Kissing is not disliked only if one is safe from desire (أمن). If desire leads to arousal or emission, it may invalidate the fast or make it makruh.
The commentator refers to a previous discussion on kissing.

The Hanafi Ruling on Beard Length
Commentary on beard length and trimming

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on trimming the beard, including the recommended fistful length and the prohibition of shaving or trimming excessively.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school requires (or strongly recommends) cutting the beard to a fistful and prohibits removing more than that.

A digression in the commentary addresses a common question: what is the proper length of the beard? The passage reconciles the hadith 'اعفوا اللحى' (let the beards grow) with the practice of the Companions, concluding that a fistful is the sunnah, and shaving the beard is forbidden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and initial hadith
The hadith in Sahihain says 'أحفوا الشوارب واعفوا اللحى' (trim mustaches and let beards grow). This is interpreted in light of other narrations.
2
Reconcile with the practice of Ibn Umar
Ibn Umar used to grasp his beard and cut what exceeded the fist. This action is reported by Abu Dawud and shows a practical interpretation of 'letting grow'.
Ibn Umar was known for strict adherence to sunnah.
3
Determine the ruling from the commentary
The commentator states that taking less than a fistful is not permitted by anyone, while shaving the beard is like the action of Magians and is forbidden. Al-Nihayah explicitly says it is obligatory to cut what exceeds a fistful.
There is a difference of opinion on whether cutting to the fist is obligatory (wajib) or recommended (mustahabb).
4
Resolve the obligation vs recommendation
Some later scholars (like Sheikh Isma'il) interpret 'yajib' in Al-Nihayah as 'yustahabb' (recommended) to reconcile with other texts. The relied-upon position is that it is a strong sunnah, not an obligation.
The commentator notes the weight of the obligation view but does not fully adopt it.
5
Understand the distinction between beauty and adornment
The passage also distinguishes between legitimate grooming (beauty for dignity) and prohibited adornment (for pride). Trimming the beard for neatness is permissible, but shaving for fashion is not.

Source Text

قوله : (لا كحل ودهن شارب) أي لا يكره. يجوز أن تكون الفاء منهما مفتوحة فيكونان مصدرين من كحل عينيه كحلاً ودهن رأسه دهناً إذا طلاة بالدهن ويجوز أن يكون مضموماً ويكون معناه ولا بأس باستعمال الكحل والدهن كذا في العناية. وفي غاية البيان: الرواية عمر بفتح الكاف والدال. وإنما لم يكرها لما أنه نوع ارتفاق وليس من محظور الصوم وقد ندب إلى الاكتحال يوم عاشوراء وإلى الصوم فيه ولا بأس بالاكتحال للرجال إذا قصدوا به التداوي دون الزينة. ويستحسن دهن الشارب إذا لم يكن من قصده الزينة لأنه يعمل عمل الخضاب، ولا يفعل لتطويل اللحية إذا كانت بقدر المسنون وهو القبضة. كذا في الهداية. وكان ابن عمر يقبض على لحيته فيقطع ما زاد على الكف . رواه أبو داود في سننه . وما في الصحيحين عن ابن عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أحفوا الشوارب واعفوا اللحى» (۱) فمحمول على إعفائها من أن يأخذ غالبها أو كلها كما هو فعل مجوس الأعاجم من حلق لحاهم فيقع بذلك الجمع بين الروايات، وأما الأخذ منها وهي دون ذلك لم يفعله بعض المغاربة والمخنثة من الرجال فلم يبحه أحد. كذا في فتح القدير : وقد صرح في النهاية بوجوب قطع ما زاد على القبضة بالضم ومقتضاه الإثم بتركه . واعلم أنه لا تلازم بين قصد الجمال وقصد الزينة، فالقصد الأول لدفع الثين وإقامة ما به الوقار وإظهار النعمة شكراً لا فخراً وهو أثر أدب النفس وشهامتها، والثاني أثر ضعفها وقالوا بالخضاب وردت السنة ولم يكن لقصد الزينة ثم بعد ذلك إن حصلت زينة فقد حصلت في ضمن قصد مطلوب فلا يضره إذا لم يكن ملتفتاً إليه . كذا في فتح القدير. ولهذا قال الولوالجي في فتاواه لبس الثياب الجميلة مباح إذا كان لا يتكبر لأن التكبر حرام وتفسيره : قوله : (وقد صرح في النهاية بوجوب قطع ما زاد الخ قال في النهر : وسمعت من بعض أعزاء الموالي أن قول النهاية يجب» بالحاء المهملة ولا بأس بها .هـ. قال الشيخ إسماعيل : ولكنه خلاف الظاهر واستعمالهم في مثله يستحب اهـ. وكأنه لهذا - والله تعالى أعلم - لم يعول عليه الشيخ (1) رواه أحمد في مسنده (٢/ ٥٢). ثم قال: (وسواك) وقبلة أن أمن أي لا يكرهان، وقد تقدم حكم القبلة، وأما السواك فلا بأس به للصائم. أطلقه فشمل الرطب واليابس والمبلول وغيره وقبل الزوال وبعده العموم قوله الا الله والولا أن أشق على أمتي لأمرتهم بالسواك عند كل وضوء وعند كل صلاة لتناوله الظهر والعصر والمغرب، وقد تقدم أحكامه في سنن الطهارة فارجع إليها. ولم يتعرض لسنة السواك للصائم ولا شك فيه كغير الصائم صرح به النهاية والله أعلم .
His statement: (No kohl, oil, mustache) meaning not disliked. It is possible that the fa' in both is fatha, making them verbal nouns from 'he applied kohl to his eyes' and 'he oiled his head' when he applied oil. Or it could be damm, meaning there is no harm in using kohl and oil, as in al-Inayah. In Ghayat al-Bayan: the narration is 'amr' with fat-ha on kaf and dal. They were not disliked because it is a type of benefit and not prohibited for fasting. It is recommended to apply kohl on Ashura and to fast on it. There is no harm for men applying kohl for medicinal purposes, not for adornment. Oiling the mustache is commendable if not intended for adornment, as it acts like dye. It is not done to lengthen the beard if it is at the recommended length of a fistful. This is in al-Hidayah. Ibn Umar would grasp his beard and cut what exceeded the fist. Reported by Abu Dawud. What is in the Sahihain from Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Trim the mustaches and let the beards grow' is interpreted to mean leaving them from taking most or all, as done by the Persian magi who shave their beards, thus reconciling narrations. As for taking from them when they are less than that, done by some Moroccans and effeminate men, no one has permitted it. In Fath al-Qadir: al-Nihayah explicitly states the obligation to cut what exceeds a fistful (with damm), implying sin in leaving it. Know that there is no connection between the intention of beauty and the intention of adornment; the first is to remove dirt and establish dignity and show blessing as gratitude not pride, and is a trace of noble character; the second is a trace of weakness. They said dyeing is recommended by sunnah not for adornment. Then if adornment results incidentally, it does not harm if one does not intend it. In Fath al-Qadir. Al-Nahr: I heard from some friends that the word 'yajib' in al-Nihayah is with a ha' (meaning 'thabata'? Actually it says 'mustahabb'?). Sheikh Isma'il said it is against the apparent and their usage in such cases is 'yustahabb'. Therefore the shaykh did not rely on it. (Hadith). Then he said: (Miswak) and kissing if safe meaning not disliked. The ruling on kissing has preceded. As for miswak, there is no harm for the fasting person. It is general including wet, dry, moistened, etc., before and after noon due to the generality of his saying 'if it were not a burden on my Ummah, I would have commanded them to use miswak at every wudu and every prayer' which includes zuhr, asr, maghrib. Its rulings have preceded in the sunan of taharah. He did not address the sunnah of miswak for the fasting person, but there is no doubt it is the same as for a non-fasting person as stated in al-Nihayah. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage expands on the rulings of actions that are not disliked for a fasting person. The commentator begins by explaining the matn 'لا كحل ودهن شارب وسواك وقبلة إن أمن' (no kohl, oil, mustache, miswak, and kissing if safe). He clarifies that these are not disliked. He provides linguistic analysis of the words. He then discusses the optional nature of kohl and oil, noting that they are permissible because they are benefits not prohibited. Men may use kohl for medicine. Oiling the mustache is commendable if not for adornment. The discussion then digresses into a detailed treatment of beard length. The commentator cites the hadith of Ibn 'Umar on trimming the beard to a fistful. He reconciles conflicting hadiths: the command to let the beard grow means not to shave it completely. Cutting it to less than a fistful is not permitted. Al-Nihayah states it is obligatory to cut what exceeds a fistful, but some scholars interpret 'obligation' as 'strong recommendation'. The commentator then returns to miswak and kissing. He confirms that miswak is permissible at all times of the day for a fasting person, based on the general command from the Prophet. Kissing is also not disliked if one is safe from desire. The passage ends by noting that the sunnah of miswak during fasting is the same as for non-fasting.

Key Terms
القبضة (al-qabḍa): A fistful; the recommended length of the beard
اللحية (al-liḥya): Beard

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nihayah as quoted in the commentary, what is the ruling on cutting what exceeds a fistful of the beard?
A) Mustahabb (recommended)
B) Wajib (obligatory)
C) Mubah (permissible)
D) Haram (unlawful)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that shaving the beard is haram, trimming to a fistful is a strong sunnah (or wajib according to some), and trimming less than a fistful is not permitted.

The Eight Legal Excuses for Breaking the Fast (عوارض الصوم)
Introduction to legal excuses for breaking the fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight excuses that permit breaking a fast in the Hanafi school and explain the basic ruling that breaking without excuse is sinful.

Core Idea
Breaking the fast without a valid excuse is a sin; eight excuses remove the sin.

This passage introduces a new section on the legal excuses (عوارض) that allow a fasting person to break the fast without incurring sin. The author first establishes the general rule: breaking the fast without an excuse is prohibited and sinful, as per the Quranic injunction 'Do not invalidate your actions.' Then he lists the eight recognized excuses, noting that the list may be expanded by later scholars. Understanding these excuses is essential for correctly applying the rulings of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The text states that breaking a fast without an excuse is sinful (حرام) and with an excuse there is no sin. This is the baseline ruling.
Quranic proof: 'ولا تبطلوا أعمالكم' (Muhammad 47:33).
2
Parse the term 'عارض'
The author gives a linguistic definition: anything that confronts you. In legal context, it means a legitimate obstacle.
Plural is عوارض.
3
Extract the list of eight excuses
The text explicitly says 'وهي هنا ثمانية' and enumerates them: مرض, سفر, إكراه, حبل, رضاع, جوع, عطش, كبر سن.
Memorize these eight core excuses.
4
Note the ninth addition
The commentator adds a ninth excuse: fear of the enemy in battle (خوف العدو), based on later Hanafi works.
This expands the list.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
If a Muslim breaks his fast due to one of these excuses, he does not sin. If he breaks without any excuse, he commits a sin and must make up the day.
Distinguishes valid from invalid breaking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العوارض
اعلم أن لفساد الصوم أحكاماً بعضها يعم الصيامات كلها، وبعضها يخص البعض دون البعض، فالذي يعم الكل الإثم إذا أفسده بغير عذر لأنه أبطل عمله من غير عذر وإبطال العمل من غير عذر حرام لقوله تعالى ولا تبطلوا أعمالكم :[محمد : ٣٣] على ما سيأتي في صوم التطوع، وإن كان بعذر لا يأثم وإذا اختلف الحكم بالعذر فلا بد من معرفة الإعذار المسقطة للإثم والمؤاخذة فلهذا ذكرها في فصل على حدة. كذا في مختصر البدائع. وأخرها حرية بالتأخير. والعوارض جمع عارض وهو في اللغة كل ما استقبلك قال الله تعالى عارض ممطرنا ﴾ [الأحقاف : ٢٤] وهو السحاب الذي يستقبلك والعارض الناب أيضاً، والعارضان شقا الفم والعارض الخد يقال أخذ من عارضيه من الشعر، وعرض له عارض أي آفة من كبر أو من مرض كذا في ضياء الحلوم مختصر شمس العلوم. وهي هنا ثمانية :
المرض والسفر الإكراه والجبل والرضاع والجوع والعطش وكبر السن. كذا في البدائع.

فصل في العوارض
قوله : (وهي هنا ثمانية الخ نظمها المقدسي في بيت واحد فقال :
سقم وإكراه وحمل وسفر رضع وجوع وعطش وكبر انتهى والأولى إنشاده خالياً من الضرورة هكذا :
مرض وإكراه رضاع والسفر حبل كذا عطش وجوع والـكـبـر ويزاد تاسع وهو تال العدو فإن الغازي إذا خاف العجز عن القتال له الفطر ولو مقيماً كما يأتي قريباً وقد زدت ذلك فقلت :
Section on Excuses. Know that breaking the fast has rulings: some apply to all fasts, some to specific ones. That which applies to all is sin if broken without excuse because he invalidated his action without excuse, and invalidating action without excuse is haram due to Allah's saying 'Do not invalidate your actions' [Muhammad 33]. If with an excuse, he does not sin. Since the ruling differs with excuse, it is necessary to know the excuses that remove sin and liability, hence they are mentioned in a separate chapter. This is in Mukhtasar al-Bada'i. 'Awārid is plural of 'ārid which linguistically means everything that meets you. Allah said: 'a cloud giving us rain' [Ahqaf 24] meaning the cloud that meets you. 'Ārid also means canine tooth. The two 'āridāni are the two sides of the mouth. 'Ārid is the cheek. It is said: 'he took from his facial hair'. A 'ārid occurs meaning an affliction of old age or illness. Here they are eight: illness, travel, coercion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and old age. This is in al-Bada'i. ... (then poetry) ... and a ninth is added: fear of the enemy, for the warrior who fears inability to fight may break his fast even if a resident, as will come soon. And I added that and said:
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the legal excuses (أعذار) that permit a fasting person to break his fast. The author explains that breaking a fast without excuse is sinful based on the Quranic prohibition (ولا تبطلوا أعمالكم). With an excuse, there is no sin. Therefore, it is essential to know the excuses. The term عوارض (sing. عارض) is defined linguistically and technically. The eight excuses listed are: illness, travel, coercion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and old age. These are the classical Hanafi list from al-Bada'i. The commentator adds a ninth excuse: fear of the enemy in battle, even if not traveling. The passage includes poetic versions by al-Maqdisi and a personal composition by the commentator. The discussion also touches on the obligation of cutting the beard beyond a fistful (a tangent from the previous section). This serves as an introduction to the detailed rulings of each excuse that will follow in the subsequent lessons.

Key Terms
عارض (‘āriḍ): A legal excuse or obstacle that permits breaking the fast
عذر (‘udhr): A valid excuse that removes sin for breaking fast
المرض (al-maraḍ): Illness; a valid excuse if it is severe or prolonged
السفر (al-safar): Travel; a journey of sufficient length (approx. 48 miles) permits breaking fast
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Coercion; being forced to break the fast under duress
الحبل (al-ḥabl): Pregnancy; a woman who fears for herself or child
الرضاع (al-riḍā‘): Breastfeeding; if the mother fears harm to child or herself
الجوع (al-jū‘): Extreme hunger; such that one fears death or severe harm
العطش (al-‘aṭash): Extreme thirst; such that one fears death or severe harm
كبر السن (kibar al-sinn): Old age; when the person is unable to fast without hardship

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Seven
B) Eight
C) Nine
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
Eight excuses permit breaking the fast without sin: illness, travel, coercion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and old age; a ninth, fear of enemy, is added by later scholars.

Ruling on Siwak for the Fasting Person
Explaining the ruling of siwak for a fasting person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on siwak for the fasting person and identify the evidence (dalil) that establishes it.

Core Idea
Siwak is a confirmed sunnah for the fasting person at all times—no prohibition or dislike applies.

This lesson examines the ruling of using the siwak (tooth stick) while fasting. A common concern is whether it breaks the fast or is disliked after noon. The Hanafi school, based on the hadith 'at every prayer,' affirms that siwak remains sunnah regardless of the time or condition of the stick. We will explore the evidence and clarify why it is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being discussed? (siwak). What is its hukm level for the fasting person? The passage states 'لا بأس به' (no harm) and it is sunnah muakkadah.
Sunnah muakkadah means a confirmed sunnah, not wajib.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited? The hadith of the Prophet ﷺ: 'لولا أن أشق على أمتي لأمرتهم بالسواك عند كل صلاة' (Were it not that I would burden my community, I would have ordered them to use siwak at every prayer).
The hadith is ahad (single-chain) but accepted for action.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this hadith prove the ruling? The phrase 'عند كل صلاة' (at every prayer) includes all prayers—Dhuhr, Asr, Maghrib, etc.—even those performed while fasting. The imperative implies recommendation.
In Hanafi usul, al-amr yufidu al-nadb (command indicates recommendation) unless there is a qarina for obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Do other schools disagree? Some early scholars disliked siwak after noon fearing it might break the fast or reduce reward. The Hanafi school rejects this because the hadith is general and no authentic evidence supports karahah.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وسواك) وقبلة أن أمن أي لا يكرهان، وقد تقدم حكم القبلة، وأما السواك فلا بأس به للصائم. أطلقه فشمل الرطب واليابس والمبلول وغيره وقبل الزوال وبعده العموم قوله لولا أن أشق على أمتي لأمرتهم بالسواك عند كل وضوء وعند كل صلاة لتناوله الظهر والعصر والمغرب، وقد تقدم أحكامه في سنن الطهارة فارجع إليها. ولم يتعرض لسنة السواك للصائم ولا شك فيه كغير الصائم صرح به النهاية والله أعلم.
His saying: (and siwak) and kissing if one is safe, meaning neither is disliked. The ruling of kissing has preceded. As for siwak, there is no harm in it for the fasting person. He made it absolute, so it includes fresh, dry, moistened, and other types, before noon and after noon, generally. His saying: except that were it not that I would burden my community, I would have ordered them to use siwak with every ablution and with every prayer, because it includes Dhuhr, Asr, and Maghrib. Its rulings have preceded in the Sunan of purification, so refer to them. And he did not address the sunnah of siwak for the fasting person, and there is no doubt that it is like for the non-fasting person; this is stated explicitly in al-Nihayah. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the use of siwak (tooth stick) while fasting. The author, Ibn Nujaym, states that siwak is not disliked (makruh) for the fasting person, neither before nor after noon, and it is permissible in all forms—wet, dry, or moist. He supports this with the hadith narrated by Abu Hurairah (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'Were it not that I would burden my community, I would have ordered them to use siwak at every prayer.' Since this hadith includes all prayers, it applies to the fasting person as well. The author notes that the matn (original text) did not specifically mention the sunnah of siwak for the fasting person, but it is clear that it remains sunnah just as for a non-fasting person, as stated in al-Nihayah. The ruling is general and applies regardless of the time of day.

Key Terms
سواك (siwāk): A tooth stick or brush used to clean the teeth, recommended for the fasting and non-fasting person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on using a fresh, moist siwak for a fasting person after noon according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is makruh (disliked) and should be avoided.
B) It is permissible and not disliked, and it is sunnah.
C) It is only permissible if used before noon.
D) It is haram (forbidden) because it may break the fast.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits siwak for the fasting person at any time of day, in any state (dry, wet, etc.), and it remains a sunnah act.

The Sickness Exemption: Proof and Requirement of Preponderant Thought
Quranic proof and condition for valid fear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic basis for the sickness exemption for fasting and explain the Hanafi requirement that fear of harm must be based on preponderant thought (ghalabat al-zann).

Core Idea
Fear of increased illness permits breaking the fast only when based on preponderant thought, not mere delusion.

The Hanafi school derives the sickness exemption for fasting from Quran 2:184. However, not every fear justifies breaking the fast; the fear must be genuine, based on signs, experience, or reliable medical advice. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the critical distinction between valid preponderant thought and baseless delusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that one who fears increased illness may break the fast. This is a permission (rukhsah), not an obligation, unless harm is certain.
The ruling is mubah (permissible) but can become wajib if there is risk of death.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites Quran 2:184: 'But whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then an equal number of other days.' This is the primary evidence.
The verse's general permission for the sick is the basis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The permission is linked to the presence of hardship (haraj). The fear of increased illness constitutes hardship, so the exemption applies via the illah (effective cause) of removing hardship.
Hanafi usul: the 'illah is derived from the wisdom behind the ruling.
4
Identify the condition for valid fear
The passage emphasizes that ijtihad (determining fear) is not mere delusion (wahm) but preponderant thought (ghalabat al-zann) from signs, experience, or a Muslim physician not openly sinful.
The condition applies to both the patient and the physician's report.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on physician reliability
Some Hanafi scholars require the physician to be 'adl (upright), but the relied-upon position only requires that he is not openly sinful.
The tarjih follows the easier condition to facilitate the exemption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم فصل في العوارض لمن خاف زيادة المرض الفطر وللمسافر وصومه أحب إن لم يضره ولا قضاء إن قوله : ( لمن خاف زيادة المرض الفطر) لقوله تعالى فمن كان منكم مريضاً أو على سفر فعدة من أيام أخر ﴾ [البقرة : ١٨٤] فإنه أباح الفطر لكل مريض لكن القطع بأن شرعية الفطر فيه إنما هو لدفع الحرج وتحقيق الحرج منوط بزيادة المرض، أو إبطاء البرء أو إفساد عضو، ثم معرفة ذلك باجتهاد المريض والاجتهاد غير مجرد الوهم بل هو غلبة الظن عن أمارة أو تجربة أو بإخبار طبيب مسلم غير ظاهر الفسق . وقيل عدالته شرط فلو برأ من المرض لكن الضعف باق وخاف أن يمرض سأل عنه القاضي الإمام فقال : الخوف ليس بشيء. كذا في فتح القدير وفي التبيين والصحيح الذي يخشى أن يمرض بالصوم فهو كالمريض، ومراده بالخشية غلبة الظن كما أراد المصنف بالخوف إياها وأطلق الخوف ابن الملك في شرح المجمع وأراد الوهم حيث قال : لو خاف من المريض لا يفطر. وفي فتح القدير: الأمة إذا ضعفت عن العمل وخشيت الهلاك بالصوم جاز لها الفطر، وكذا الذي ذهب به متوكل السلطان إلى العمارة في الأيام الحارة والعمل الحثيث إذا خشي الهلاك أو نقصان العقل. وقالوا: الغازي إذا كان يعلم يقيناً أنه يقاتل العدو في شهر رمضان ويخاف الضعف إن لم يفطر يفطر قبل الحرب، مسافراً كان أو مقيماً. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية والولوالجية : للأمة أن تمتنع من امتثال أمر المولى. إذا كان ذلك يعجزها عن إقامة الفرائض لأنها مبقاة على أصل الحرية في حق الفرائض. أطلق في المرض فشمل ما إذا مرض قبل طلوع الفجر أو بعده بعد ما شرع بخلاف السفر فإنه ليس بعذر في اليوم الذي أنشأ السفر فيه ولا يحل له الإفطار وهو عذر في سائر الأيام. كذا في الظهيرية. وأشار باللام إلى أنه مخير بين الصوم والفطر لكن الفطر حبل والرضاع وإكراه سفر مرض جهاد جوعه عطش كبر قال في النهر : ويرد عليه أن السفر من الثمانية مع أنه يبيح الفطر إنما يبيح عدم الشروع في الصوم، ومنها كبر السن، وفي عروضه في الصوم ليكون مبيحاً للفطر ما لا يخفى فالأولى أن يراد بالعوارض ما يبيح عدم الضوم ليطرد في الكل قوله : ( وفي فتح القدير الأمة إذا ضعفت الخ) قال الرملي قال في جامع الفتاوى : ولو ضعف عن الصوم لاشتغاله بالمعيشة فله أن يفطر ويطعم لكل يوم نصف صاع ا هـ . وأقول : هذا إذا لم يدرك عدة من أيام أخر يمكنه الصوم فيها، أما إذا أمكنه يجب القضاء، وعلى هذا الحصاد في شهر رمضان إذا لم يقدر عليه مع الصوم ويهلك الزرع بالتأخير لا شك في جواز الفطر والقضاء إذا أدرك عدة من أيام أخر والله تعالى أعلم قوله : (للأمة أن تمتنع الخ) أي لا يجب عليها طاعته في ذلك، وانظر هل يجوز لها إطاعته أم لا ؟ والظاهر الثاني تأمل إطاعته إلا أن يقال : إن قوله ولها معناه أنه يحل لها مخالفة أمره إن أمكنها، وقوله قبله بخلاف الأمة محمول على ما إذا فعلت بغير اختيارها بدليل التعليل تأمل
Book of Fasting, Chapter on Impediments: For one who fears increased illness, breaking the fast is permissible. And for the traveler, his fasting is preferable if it does not harm him, and there is no makeup [if he breaks?]... His saying: (For one who fears increased illness, breaking the fast) due to the Almighty's saying: 'But whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then an equal number of other days' [2:184]. For He permitted breaking the fast for every sick person, but the determination that the legality of breaking the fast in this case is only to remove hardship, and the realization of hardship is dependent on increase of illness, delay of recovery, or corruption of a limb. Then knowledge of that is by the ijtihad of the patient, and ijtihad is not mere delusion but rather preponderant thought from a sign, experience, or the information of a Muslim physician who is not evidently sinful. And it was said that his uprightness is a condition. So if he recovered from illness but weakness remains and he fears becoming ill, al-Qadi al-Imam was asked and said: Fear is nothing. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and al-Tabyin: The correct is that one who fears becoming ill by fasting is like the sick person. And he meant by 'fear' preponderant thought, as the author meant by 'khawf' that, but Ibn al-Malik in Sharh al-Majma' took khawf as delusion [wahn] where he said: If he fears from the sick person, he does not break the fast. And in Fath al-Qadir: The slave woman (umm walad) if she becomes weak from work and fears destruction by fasting, it is permissible for her to break, and likewise the one sent by the sultan's deputy to construction in hot days and intense work if he fears destruction or loss of intellect. And they said: The warrior, if he knows with certainty that he will fight the enemy in Ramadan and fears weakness if he does not break, he breaks before the battle, whether traveler or resident. And in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya and al-Walwaljiyya: The slave woman may refrain from obeying the master's command if that would incapacitate her from performing the obligatory duties, because she is retained upon the original freedom with respect to obligatory duties. He gave absolute license for illness, so it includes if he became ill before dawn or after it after having commenced, unlike travel, for it is not an excuse on the day he initiated the travel and it is not permissible for him to break the fast, but it is an excuse on other days. Thus in al-Zahiriyya. And he indicated by the letter 'lam' that he has a choice between fasting and breaking, but breaking [includes] pregnancy, breastfeeding, coercion, travel, illness, jihad, hunger, thirst, old age. Al-Nahr said: It is objected that travel is among the eight, and although it permits breaking the fast, it only permits not commencing the fast. And among them is old age, and its occurrence during fasting to be a permissive for breaking is not hidden. So the better is that by 'impediments' is meant what permits not fasting, so that it applies uniformly. His saying: (And in Fath al-Qadir: the slave woman if she becomes weak...). Al-Ramli said: In Jami' al-Fatawa: If one becomes weak from fasting due to being occupied with livelihood, he may break and feed for each day half a sa'. I say: This is if he does not reach a number of other days in which he can fast. But if he can, then he must make up. And likewise the harvester in Ramadan if he is not able with fasting and the crop perishes by delay, there is no doubt about the permissibility of breaking and making up if he reaches other days. And Allah knows best. His saying: (The slave woman may refrain...). That is, she is not obligated to obey him in that. Consider: Is it permissible for her to obey him or not? The apparent is the second. Reflect. Except that it may be said: His saying 'and she may' means that it is permissible for her to disobey his command if she can, and his earlier saying 'unlike the slave woman' is carried on when she did it involuntarily due to the reasoning. Reflect.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran and expanded commentary from multiple Hanafi sources. The author begins by establishing the general rule: one who fears increased illness due to fasting may break the fast, citing Quran 2:184. The commentary clarifies that the fear must be based on preponderant thought (ghalabat al-zann), not mere delusion. This preponderant thought can come from personal signs, experience, or a Muslim physician who is not openly sinful. There is a difference of opinion on whether the physician must be upright ('adl). The text then addresses various cases: a person who recovers but still fears illness (fear is not considered if weakness remains? Wait, the text says the Qadi al-Imam said 'fear is nothing', but then the correct view in Fath al-Qadir and al-Tabyin is that one who fears becoming ill by fasting is like a sick person. The author reconciles by noting that the term 'khawf' can be ambiguous: Ibn al-Malik took it as delusion, while the author takes it as preponderant thought. Other cases follow: a slave woman (umm walad) who becomes weak from work may fast, and a worker sent by the sultan to strenuous labor in hot weather may break if he fears harm. A warrior preparing for battle may break before fighting if he knows he will fight and fears weakness. The slave woman may disobey her master if his command prevents her from performing obligatory duties, because she retains freedom in religious obligations. The text also discusses the timing: illness is an excuse whether it occurs before dawn or after starting the fast, but travel is only an excuse on days after the travel is initiated, not on the day travel begins. The passage lists the eight recognized excuses for not fasting: pregnancy, breastfeeding, coercion, travel, illness, jihad, hunger, thirst, old age. However, al-Nahr objects that travel only permits not starting the fast, not breaking it, so it differs from the others. The better definition of impediments is what permits not fasting at all. Finally, the commentary addresses a person who gives up fasting due to weakness from work: if he cannot make up days later, he must feed half a sa' per day; otherwise he must make up. The slave woman's case is concluded with a reflection on whether she may choose to obey her master or not.

Key Terms
الخوف (al-khawf): Fear of harm from fasting, which must be based on preponderant thought (ghalabat al-zann) not mere delusion (wahm).
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): The dominant likelihood based on signs, experience, or reliable medical advice that fasting will cause harm.
العدالة (al-'adalah): Uprightness, a condition for testimony in Islamic law; some Hanafi scholars require it for the physician's report.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum level of certainty for fear of illness to permit breaking the fast in Ramadan?
A) Mere suspicion (wahm)
B) Preponderant thought (ghalabat al-zann) based on signs, experience, or a Muslim physician
C) Absolute certainty (yaqin)
D) A doctor's note regardless of the doctor's religion

Key Takeaway
Fear of increased illness permits breaking the fast only when it is preponderant thought (ghalabat al-zann), not mere delusion.

Extended Exemptions: Specific Cases and the List of Eight Impediments
Expansion of exemptions and list of impediments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight recognized impediments for not fasting in the Hanafi school and apply the exemption to specific cases like the slave woman, worker, and warrior.

Core Idea
Exemptions from fasting extend beyond sickness to include pregnancy, breastfeeding, coercion, travel, jihad, hunger, thirst, and old age.

The passage expands the sickness exemption to other situations where fasting causes hardship. It discusses specific cases: a slave woman who weakens from work, a worker sent by the sultan to strenuous labor, a warrior preparing for battle, and then lists eight recognized impediments. However, there is a critical note from al-Nahr questioning whether travel truly belongs among those that permit breaking the fast after starting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific cases discussed
The text mentions: (1) a slave woman (umm walad) who becomes weak from work, (2) a worker sent by the sultan's deputy to construction in hot days, (3) a warrior who knows he will fight, and (4) general weakness from occupation with livelihood.
These are examples of extreme hardship.
2
Extract the ruling for each case
All these cases permit breaking the fast if there is fear of harm (e.g., death, loss of intellect). The slave woman may also refuse the master's command if it prevents obligatory duties.
The ruling is based on the principle of necessity (darura).
3
List the eight impediments
The passage lists: pregnancy (hubl), breastfeeding (rada'), coercion (ikrah), travel (safar), illness (marad), jihad, hunger (ju'), thirst (atash), and old age (kibar).
The OCR may have issues: 'حبل والرضاع وإكراه سفر مرض جهاد جوعه عطش كبر'
4
Analyze the objection by al-Nahr
Al-Nahr points out that travel only permits not starting the fast, not breaking it after starting, unlike the other impediments. Therefore, the definition of 'impediments' should be 'what permits not fasting' rather than 'what permits breaking'.
This critique refines the classification.
5
Apply to the harvester scenario
The commentary adds: a harvester in Ramadan who cannot fast without damaging his crop may break and make up later, provided he can fast on other days.
This shows the principle extends to livelihood necessities.

الغزو (al-ghazw): Military expedition; the warrior may break fast before battle if he fears weakness.

Physician Reliability and the Umm Walad Case: Two Intra-Hanafi Disputes
Two intra-Hanafi disputes on conditions for exemption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two intra-Hanafi disputes in this passage: the condition of the physician's uprightness and whether the umm walad must obey her master regarding fasting.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school has internal differences on whether a physician must be 'adl and whether a slave woman must obey her master if fasting harms her.

This passage contains two distinct intra-Hanafi debates. The first concerns the reliability of the physician whose report may justify breaking the fast: some require 'adalah (uprightness), while the relied-upon view only requires not being openly sinful. The second dispute is about the slave woman (umm walad) who fears harm from fasting: may she disobey her master's command? The passage presents both positions and offers a reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first dispute: physician's 'adalah
The text states: 'by the information of a Muslim physician who is not evidently sinful. And it was said that his uprightness is a condition.' This indicates a difference on whether the physician must be 'adl or just not fasiq.
The default condition in the text is the easier one.
2
Identify the second dispute: umm walad's obedience
The passage says: 'For the umm walad to refrain from obeying the master's command if that would incapacitate her from performing the obligatory duties.' The commentary adds a reflection on whether she may choose to obey even if it prevents her obligatory duties.
The question: is it permissible or obligatory for her to disobey?
3
Extract the positions
First dispute: some say physician must be 'adl; others say only not openly sinful. Second dispute: the slave woman may refrain from obeying (permissible), but the commentator wonders if she may still obey (apparent: she cannot obey if it prevents obligations).
The text gives the ruling, but the commentary opens further discussion.
4
Apply the tarjih mechanism
For the physician, the mufta bihi is the easier view (not openly sinful) because it facilitates the exemption. For the umm walad, the relied-upon view appears to be that she must disobey if her obligations are compromised, but the commentary leaves room for doubt.
The author says: 'the apparent is the second [i.e., she cannot obey]' but then says 'reflect'.

كتاب الصوم فصل في العوارض لمن خاف زيادة المرض الفطر وللمسافر وصومه أحب إن لم يضره ولا قضاء إن قوله : ( لمن خاف زيادة المرض الفطر) لقوله تعالى فمن كان منكم مريضاً أو على سفر فعدة من أيام أخر ﴾ [البقرة والصحيح الذي يخشى أن يمرض بالصوم فهو كالمريض، ومراده بالخشية غلبة الظن كما أراد المصنف بالخوف إياها وأطلق الخوف ابن الملك في شرح المجمع وأراد الوهم حيث قال : لو خاف من المريض لا يفطر. وفي فتح القدير: الأمة إذا ضعفت عن العمل وخشيت الهلاك بالصوم جاز لها الفطر، وكذا الذي ذهب به متوكل السلطان إلى العمارة في الأيام الحارة والعمل الحثيث إذا خشي الهلاك أو نقصان العقل. وقالوا: الغازي إذا كان يعلم يقيناً أنه يقاتل العدو في شهر رمضان ويخاف الضعف إن لم يفطر يفطر قبل الحرب، مسافراً كان أو مقيماً. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية والولوالجية : للأمة أن تمتنع من امتثال أمر المولى. إذا كان ذلك يعجزها عن إقامة الفرائض لأنها مبقاة على أصل الحرية في حق الفرائض. أطلق في المرض فشمل ما إذا مرض قبل طلوع الفجر أو بعده بعد ما شرع بخلاف السفر فإنه ليس بعذر في اليوم الذي أنشأ السفر فيه ولا يحل له الإفطار وهو عذر في سائر الأيام. كذا في الظهيرية. وأشار باللام إلى أنه مخير بين الصوم والفطر لكن الفطر حبل والرضاع وإكراه سفر مرض جهاد جوعه عطش كبر قال في النهر : ويرد عليه أن السفر من الثمانية مع أنه يبيح الفطر إنما يبيح عدم الشروع في الصوم، ومنها كبر السن، وفي عروضه في الصوم ليكون مبيحاً للفطر ما لا يخفى فالأولى أن يراد بالعوارض ما يبيح عدم الضوم ليطرد في الكل قوله : ( وفي فتح القدير الأمة إذا ضعفت الخ) قال الرملي قال في جامع الفتاوى : ولو ضعف عن الصوم لاشتغاله بالمعيشة فله أن يفطر ويطعم لكل يوم نصف صاع ا هـ . وأقول : هذا إذا لم يدرك عدة من أيام أخر يمكنه الصوم فيها، أما إذا أمكنه يجب القضاء، وعلى هذا الحصاد في شهر رمضان إذا لم يقدر عليه مع الصوم ويهلك الزرع بالتأخير لا شك في جواز الفطر والقضاء إذا أدرك عدة من أيام أخر والله تعالى أعلم قوله : (للأمة أن تمتنع الخ) أي لا يجب عليها طاعته في ذلك، وانظر هل يجوز لها إطاعته أم لا ؟ والظاهر الثاني تأمل إطاعته إلا أن يقال : إن قوله ولها معناه أنه يحل لها مخالفة أمره إن أمكنها، وقوله قبله بخلاف الأمة محمول على ما إذا فعلت بغير اختيارها بدليل التعليل تأمل
Book of Fasting, Chapter on Impediments: For one who fears increased illness, breaking the fast is permissible. And for the traveler, his fasting is preferable if it does not harm him, and there is no makeup [if he breaks?]... His saying: (For one who fears increased illness, breaking the fast) due to the Almighty's saying: 'But whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then an equal number of other days' [2:184]. For He permitted breaking the fast for every sick person, but the determination that the legality of breaking the fast in this case is only to remove hardship, and the realization of hardship is dependent on increase of illness, delay of recovery, or corruption of a limb. Then knowledge of that is by the ijtihad of the patient, and ijtihad is not mere delusion but rather preponderant thought from a sign, experience, or the information of a Muslim physician who is not evidently sinful. And it was said that his uprightness is a condition. So if he recovered from illness but weakness remains and he fears becoming ill, al-Qadi al-Imam was asked and said: Fear is nothing. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and al-Tabyin: The correct is that one who fears becoming ill by fasting is like the sick person. And he meant by 'fear' preponderant thought, as the author meant by 'khawf' that, but Ibn al-Malik in Sharh al-Majma' took khawf as delusion [wahn] where he said: If he fears from the sick person, he does not break the fast. And in Fath al-Qadir: The slave woman (umm walad) if she becomes weak from work and fears destruction by fasting, it is permissible for her to break, and likewise the one sent by the sultan's deputy to construction in hot days and intense work if he fears destruction or loss of intellect. And they said: The warrior, if he knows with certainty that he will fight the enemy in Ramadan and fears weakness if he does not break, he breaks before the battle, whether traveler or resident. And in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya and al-Walwaljiyya: The slave woman may refrain from obeying the master's command if that would incapacitate her from performing the obligatory duties, because she is retained upon the original freedom with respect to obligatory duties. He gave absolute license for illness, so it includes if he became ill before dawn or after it after having commenced, unlike travel, for it is not an excuse on the day he initiated the travel and it is not permissible for him to break the fast, but it is an excuse on other days. Thus in al-Zahiriyya. And he indicated by the letter 'lam' that he has a choice between fasting and breaking, but breaking [includes] pregnancy, breastfeeding, coercion, travel, illness, jihad, hunger, thirst, old age. Al-Nahr said: It is objected that travel is among the eight, and although it permits breaking the fast, it only permits not commencing the fast. And among them is old age, and its occurrence during fasting to be a permissive for breaking is not hidden. So the better is that by 'impediments' is meant what permits not fasting, so that it applies uniformly. His saying: (And in Fath al-Qadir: the slave woman if she becomes weak...). Al-Ramli said: In Jami' al-Fatawa: If one becomes weak from fasting due to being occupied with livelihood, he may break and feed for each day half a sa'. I say: This is if he does not reach a number of other days in which he can fast. But if he can, then he must make up. And likewise the harvester in Ramadan if he is not able with fasting and the crop perishes by delay, there is no doubt about the permissibility of breaking and making up if he reaches other days. And Allah knows best. His saying: (The slave woman may refrain...). That is, she is not obligated to obey him in that. Consider: Is it permissible for her to obey him or not? The apparent is the second. Reflect. Except that it may be said: His saying 'and she may' means that it is permissible for her to disobey his command if she can, and his earlier saying 'unlike the slave woman' is carried on when she did it involuntarily due to the reasoning. Reflect.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran and expanded commentary from multiple Hanafi sources. The author begins by establishing the general rule: one who fears increased illness due to fasting may break the fast, citing Quran 2:184. The commentary clarifies that the fear must be based on preponderant thought (ghalabat al-zann), not mere delusion. This preponderant thought can come from personal signs, experience, or a Muslim physician who is not openly sinful. There is a difference of opinion on whether the physician must be upright ('adl). The text then addresses various cases: a person who recovers but still fears illness (fear is not considered if weakness remains? Wait, the text says the Qadi al-Imam said 'fear is nothing', but then the correct view in Fath al-Qadir and al-Tabyin is that one who fears becoming ill by fasting is like a sick person. The author reconciles by noting that the term 'khawf' can be ambiguous: Ibn al-Malik took it as delusion, while the author takes it as preponderant thought. Other cases follow: a slave woman (umm walad) who becomes weak from work may fast, and a worker sent by the sultan to strenuous labor in hot weather may break if he fears harm. A warrior preparing for battle may break before fighting if he knows he will fight and fears weakness. The slave woman may disobey her master if his command prevents her from performing obligatory duties, because she retains freedom in religious obligations. The text also discusses the timing: illness is an excuse whether it occurs before dawn or after starting the fast, but travel is only an excuse on days after the travel is initiated, not on the day travel begins. The passage lists the eight recognized excuses for not fasting: pregnancy, breastfeeding, coercion, travel, illness, jihad, hunger, thirst, old age. However, al-Nahr objects that travel only permits not starting the fast, not breaking it, so it differs from the others. The better definition of impediments is what permits not fasting at all. Finally, the commentary addresses a person who gives up fasting due to weakness from work: if he cannot make up days later, he must feed half a sa' per day; otherwise he must make up. The slave woman's case is concluded with a reflection on whether she may choose to obey her master or not.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-ummah): A slave woman; in this context, an umm walad (mother of her master's child) who has certain rights.
العدالة (al-'adalah): Uprightness, a condition for testimony; some require it for the physician's report.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mufta bihi, what condition must a Muslim physician meet for his advice to justify breaking the fast?
A) He must be 'adl (upright).
B) He must be Muslim and not openly sinful.
C) He must be a specialist in the illness.
D) He must be recommended by another physician.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school eases conditions for exemptions: the physician need not be 'adl, and a slave woman may refuse her master's command to protect her religious obligations.

Kafarah for Breaking Fast on a False Presumption: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafarah for false presumption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on expiation for breaking fast based on an unverified presumption and identify the intra-madhhab difference between the majority and Qadikhan's views.

Core Idea
Breaking fast on a false presumption of fever or menses incurs kafarah according to the majority, but Qadikhan excuses it based on istihsan.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a subtle point in the fiqh of fasting: what if someone breaks their fast because they expect a fever or battle, but the expectation does not materialize? The text presents the majority view that expiation is due, but also notes a contrary opinion from Qadikhan. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply istihsan in Hanafi fiqh and recognizes the breadth of ikhtilaf within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
Consider a person who breaks his Ramadan fast because he expects a periodic fever (nawbat al-humma) at a certain time, but the fever does not actually come. The initial ruling from Jami' al-Fusulayn is that he owes kafarah. However, there is a dissenting view that he does not.
The act is breaking the fast before actual excuse materializes.
2
Distinguish the special case of the fighter
The text contrasts this with a fighter who breaks his fast presuming he will fight but no battle occurs. The fighter is not liable for kafarah because his need (quwwa for battle) is immediate and recognized by Shari'ah as a valid reason to break early.
The fighter's case is an exception, not the rule.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
The author cites Qadikhan who holds that even the fever patient is not liable for kafarah, and similarly a woman who breaks fast on the presumption of menstruation. Qadikhan extends the leniency based on istihsan (juristic preference) because the person acted on a strong probable indication.
This is a genuine ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Understand the basis of the dispute
The majority relies on qiyas: the person broke the fast without a genuine excuse, so kafarah is due, just like a woman who mistakenly thinks she is menstruating. Qadikhan uses istihsan: the person did not intend to break without excuse; they relied on a credible sign, so the Shari'ah should not punish.
The difference is between strict analogy and equitable consideration.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The dominant fatwa follows the majority: kafarah is required unless there is a clear need recognized by Shari'ah (like fighting). However, Qadikhan's view is noted for its leniency and may be applied in accommodating circumstances.
The mufta bihi is the majority, but the ikhtilaf is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كان عليه الكفارة) قال في جامع الفصولين : وقيل لا، ولو أفطر على ظن أنه يقاتل أهل الحرب فلم يتفق القتال لا يكفر، والفرق أي بين هذا وبين من له نوبة حمى أن القتال يحتاج إلى تقديم الإفطار ليتقوى بخلاف المرض ا هـ. وحاصله أن المقاتل محتاج إلى تقديم الأكل فصار مأذوناً فيه قبل وجود حقيقة العذر بخلاف المريض فلذا يلزمه الكفارة إذا لو يوجد عذره بعد الأكل لكن قدمنا عن قاضيخان في شرح الجامع سقوطها عنه أيضاً، وكذا عمن ظنت أنه يوم حيضها
His saying: (He owes expiation). He said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: And it was said: No. And if one breaks the fast presuming that he will fight the people of war, but no fighting occurs, he is not liable for expiation. The difference between this and the case of one who has a fever episode is that fighting requires advancing the breaking of the fast to gain strength, unlike illness. So the gist is that the fighter needs to advance eating, so he becomes permitted before the actual existence of the excuse, unlike the sick person, so he is liable for expiation if his excuse does not materialize after eating. But we previously cited from Qadikhan in Sharh al-Jami' that expiation is dropped from him as well, and likewise for one who presumed it was her menstruation day.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about expiation (kafarah) when someone breaks the fast based on a presumption that later turns out false. The specific case discussed is a person who has a periodic fever (nawbah al-humma) and eats before the fever actually appears. The general rule from Jami' al-Fusulayn is that he owes expiation, but there is a minority view that he does not. The text then contrasts this with a fighter who breaks his fast presuming he will fight but then no battle occurs. The fighter is excused from expiation because he needs to eat early to gain strength for war. The effective cause is that the fighter's need is immediate and the excuse is likely, so he is permitted in advance. However, the author notes that Qadikhan in Sharh al-Jami' has a different view that even the fever patient is not liable for expiation. Similarly, a woman who breaks her fast thinking it is her menstruation day but later finds it is not also falls under this principle. The dispute centers on whether one who acts on a reasonable presumption without actual justification is excused or not.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kafarah): The expiatory penalty for intentionally invalidating a Ramadan fast without valid excuse: freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people.
نوبة الحمى (nawbat al-humma): A periodic fever attack that the person expects at a certain time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a person who breaks his fast presuming his periodic fever will occur, but it does not?
A) He must only make up the day
B) He must pay kafarah and make up the day
C) He is excused from both qada and kafarah
D) He must only pay kafarah

Key Takeaway
Breaking fast on a false presumption generally incurs kafarah, except when the presumption is tied to an immediate need recognized by Shari'ah, like preparing for battle.
