Exemptions from Fasting: Wet Nurse, Snakebite, and Baker
Rulings on fasting exemptions for specific professions and emergencies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a wet nurse, a snakebite victim, and a baker may break their fast in Hanafi fiqh, and apply the principle of necessity to distinguish valid excuses from invalid ones.

Core Idea
Necessity permits breaking fast for wet nurse and snakebite, but a baker cannot deliberately cause weakness.

Fasting in Ramadan is a fundamental obligation, but the Shari'ah recognizes genuine necessities that permit breaking the fast. This lesson examines three specific cases from the Hanafi tradition: a wet nurse who needs medication to save an infant, a snakebite victim requiring medicine, and a baker who claims weakness from his work. Understanding the conditions for these permissions is crucial to applying the principle that necessity overrides prohibition only within its proper limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
The passage addresses three distinct exemptions: the wet nurse (al-dha'r), the snakebite victim (al-maldūgh), and the baker (al-khubbāz). Note the specific circumstances of each.
2
Analyze the condition for the wet nurse
The wet nurse may break her fast only if skilled physicians (especially Muslim) advise that she take medicine during the day to cure the nursing infant. The author adds a caution: the physician should be Muslim, citing a hadith about non-Muslims intending harm.
This condition reflects the Hanafi concern for the reliability of the advisor.
3
Examine the rule for the snakebite victim
A person bitten by a snake may break his fast to take medicine if it is beneficial. No further condition is mentioned; the necessity is self-evident.
4
Understand the baker's restriction
The baker is not permitted to deliberately bake so much that he becomes weak and then break his fast. He should adjust his work to half a day. The text refutes his claim of insufficiency, arguing that even a short winter day suffices for earnings.
This highlights that one cannot create a necessity through one's own actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (برئ الصغير وتماثل) قال في القاموس في مادة م ث ل تماثل العليل قارب البرء قوله : (وفيه إشارة إلى أن المريض يجوز له الخ) : قال الدر المختار وفيه كلام لأن عندهم نصح المسلم كفر فأنى يتطبب بهم اهـ. قال محشية وأيده شيخنا بما نقله عن الدر المنثور للعلامة السيوطي من قوله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم «ما خلا كافر بمسلم إلا عزم على قتله قوله : ( وفي القنية لا يجوز للخباز (الخ قال الرملي : ما قدمناه عن جامع الفتاوى يدخل فيه الخباز وغيره، وقوله هو كاذب الخ فيه نظر فإن طول النهار وقصره لا دخل له في الكفاية فقد يظهر صدقه في قوله لا يكفيني فيفوض إليه حملاً لحاله في الصلاح تأمل ا هـ .
His saying: (The infant recovered and recovered). He said in al-Qamus under the root m-th-l: The sick person recovered means approaching recovery. His saying: (And in it is an indication that the patient is permitted...). Al-Durr al-Mukhtar said: There is discussion in it, because according to them, advising a Muslim is disbelief, so how can one be treated by them? The commentator said: Our teacher supported it with what he transmitted from al-Durr al-Manthur of al-Suyuti from his saying, peace be upon him: 'No non-Muslim has ever been alone with a Muslim but he intended to kill him.' His saying: (And in al-Qunyah: It is not permissible for the baker...). Al-Ramli said: What we presented earlier from Jami' al-Fatawa includes the baker and others. And his saying 'he is lying' etc. is questionable, because the length or shortness of the day has nothing to do with sufficiency; his truthfulness in saying 'it does not suffice me' might appear, so it is referred to him, assuming his state of integrity. Reflect.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage addresses three distinct cases of fasting exemptions. First, the case of a nursing infant (radi') who is sick and needs medicine: if skilled physicians say the wet nurse should take a medicine in the daytime to cure the infant, she may break her fast. The text notes that this relies on the word of 'skillful physicians' (atibba' al-hudhdhaq). The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq adds a caution: this applies only if the physician is Muslim, not a non-Muslim, because a non-Muslim might intend to corrupt the fast. He draws an analogy with a person who performs tayammum and a non-Muslim promises to bring water but might intend to invalidate the prayer. Second, the case of a person bitten by a snake who needs to take medicine during the day: it is permissible if it benefits him. Third, the case of a baker (khubbaz) who bakes bread and becomes weak: it is not permissible for him to deliberately bake bread that will cause him weakness so that he can break his fast. Rather, he should work half the day and rest half. If he claims his earnings are insufficient, the text says he is lying, because even the shortest winter day would suffice. There is a discussion about whether the baker's need is genuine.

Key Terms
الظئر (al-dha'r): The wet nurse, a woman who breastfeeds an infant that is not her own.
الخباز (al-khubbaz): A baker; in this context, one who bakes bread for a living and fears weakness if he fasts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it permissible for a wet nurse to break her fast to take medicine during Ramadan?
A) Whenever she feels uncomfortable
B) When skilled physicians (especially Muslim) say the medicine will cure the nursing infant
C) Only if the infant is critically ill
D) Only if she is the mother

Key Takeaway
Fasting may be broken for genuine medical necessity, but only under the guidance of a trustworthy Muslim physician, and one cannot deliberately bring about weakness to excuse breaking the fast.

Fasting vs. Breaking Fast: The Hanafi Principle of Azimah and Rukhsah
States the general Hanafi principle on fasting vs. breaking fast for those with excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on fasting and breaking fast for a traveler or sick person, and identify when breaking becomes obligatory.

Core Idea
Fasting is the stronger obligation (azimah) and better unless harm is certain — then breaking becomes wajib.

This lesson examines a concise ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, a foundational Hanafi text. The author establishes the default preference for fasting (the azimah) for those with valid excuses like travel or illness, but clarifies that this changes when fasting leads to actual harm. Understanding this balance is essential for applying the ruling correctly in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories: rukhsah and azimah
Recognize that the text distinguishes between a concession (rukhsah) and the stronger original ruling (azimah). The act of breaking fast is a concession, while fasting is the original ruling.
2
Determine the default ruling
The text states that fasting is azimah, meaning it is the stronger and more virtuous act. Therefore, for someone with a valid excuse who can fast safely, fasting is better (afdal).
3
Identify the exception
The exception is when one fears destruction (khawf al-halak) — that is, when fasting would cause significant harm or danger to health.
4
Apply the exception
In the case of fear of destruction, breaking fast ceases to be merely a concession and becomes obligatory (wajib). Preserving life takes precedence over the virtue of fasting.
5
Note the authority
The text cites al-Bada'i' (al-Bada'i' al-Sana'i') as the source, confirming this is the standard Hanafi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رخصة والصوم عزيمة فكان أفضل إلا إذا خاف الهلاك فالإفطار واجب. كذا في البدائع.
It is a concession, and fasting is a determination, so it is better, except if one fears destruction, then breaking fast is obligatory. Thus in al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from Kanz al-Daqa'iq being explained by the commentator. It establishes that for those with valid excuses (like sickness or travel), the azimah (the stronger obligation) is to fast, making it more virtuous. However, if fasting would lead to actual harm or destruction, then it becomes obligatory (wajib) to break the fast, because preserving life takes precedence. The citation to al-Bada'i' (al-Bada'i' al-Sana'i') confirms this as the standard Hanafi position.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsah): A concession or dispensation from the original ruling due to necessity.
عزيمة (azimah): The original, stronger ruling that is generally recommended to follow.
خاف الهلاك (khaf al-halak): Fear of destruction or severe harm to oneself or another (e.g., unborn child, nursing infant).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is breaking fast obligatory (wajib) for a person with a valid excuse?
A) Whenever the person is traveling
B) When the person is sick
C) When the person fears destruction (harm)
D) Always, because it is a rukhsah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds fasting as the stronger and more virtuous act (azimah) for those with excuses, unless harm is certain, in which case breaking becomes obligatory to preserve life.

Combining Fasting and Prayer When Standing is Impossible
Rules on combining fasting and prayer when standing is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a fasting person who cannot stand for prayer and explain the principle of combining acts of worship.

Core Idea
Preserve both fasting and prayer by adjusting posture — combining acts of worship takes priority over abandoning one.

Imagine you are fasting in Ramadan but feel so weak that you cannot stand for fard prayer. Should you break your fast to pray properly, or can you pray sitting? The Hanafi school provides a clear answer based on a principle of maximising devotion. Let's examine the ruling from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting scenario
Note the person: he is fasting in Ramadan, and fasting makes it impossible for him to stand in prayer. However, if he were not fasting, he could stand.
The conflict is between two obligations: preserving the fast and performing prayer with standing (which is normally required for a capable person).
2
State the two possible responses
One could break the fast to regain strength and pray standing, or one could keep fasting and pray sitting. The author chooses the latter.
Breaking a fard fast without a valid excuse is prohibited, so the option to keep fasting is preferred.
3
Apply the principle: combine both acts (jama bayn al-ibadatayn)
The ruling orders the person to fast and pray sitting, thereby performing both obligations simultaneously. This is because both acts are worship, and losing any part of either is undesirable when possible.
The basis is maximising reward and fulfilling duties to the extent possible.
4
Derive the general principle
When two obligatory acts conflict, the Hanafi school attempts to preserve both by modifying the less critical element (here, prayer posture) rather than discarding one entirely.
This is consistent with the axiom 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (actions are judged by intentions) but here it's about feasibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : رجل لو صام في شهر رمضان لا يمكنه أن يصلي قائماً وإذا أفطر يمكنه أن يصلي قائماً فإنه يصوم ويصلي قاعداً جمعاً بين العبادتين
And in al-Zahiriyyah: A man who, if he fasts in the month of Ramadan, cannot pray standing, and if he breaks fast he can pray standing, then he should fast and pray sitting, combining both acts of worship.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This is a ruling from the Dhahiriya (a Hanafi work) addressing a conflict between fasting and performing salah properly. The case is a man who, if he fasts, becomes too weak to stand in prayer, but if he breaks his fast he regains strength to stand. The ruling is that he should keep the fast and pray sitting, because both deeds are acts of worship and preserving both is better than sacrificing one for the other. The principle is that one should try to combine all possible devotions rather than jettison any.

Key Terms
العبادتين (al-ibadatayn): Refers to the two acts of worship: fasting and prayer.
الظهيرية (al-Zahiriyyah): A Hanafi legal work by Zahir al-Din al-Bukhari (d. 619/1222), containing fatwas and rulings.
جمعاً بين العبادتين (jam'an bayn al-ibadatayn): Combining both acts of worship by performing both simultaneously when conflict arises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in al-Zahiriyya, what should a fasting person do if standing for prayer is impossible due to the fast?
A) Break the fast and pray standing
B) Fast and pray sitting
C) Delay the prayer until after iftar
D) Fast and perform a different act of worship instead

Key Takeaway
When fasting hinders standing for prayer, the Hanafi school instructs to continue fasting and pray sitting, preserving both obligatory acts through permissible concession.

Breaking a Fast Based on an Unrealized Excuse: Kaffarah or Qada?
Ruling on breaking fast based on anticipation of a valid excuse that does not materialize.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when kaffarah is required versus only qada for breaking a fast based on an anticipated excuse that does not materialize.

Core Idea
If you break a fast after intending it, kaffarah is due; if before intention, only qada.

Al-Khulasa presents a precise ruling distinguishing the consequence of breaking a fast based on an anticipated but unrealized excuse. The key factor is whether the fast had already been intended (niyyah) and entered into. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is breaking a Ramadan fast. The ruling depends on whether the fast was already intended and started.
The text presents two scenarios.
2
Note the condition of anticipation
The person breaks the fast anticipating a valid excuse (fever or menses) that does not occur.
If the excuse had actually occurred, no penalty would apply.
3
Distinguish intention status
If the person had made the intention to fast (e.g., at dawn) and commenced the fast, then broke it: kaffarah + qada. If they had not made the intention: only qada.
Intention is what activates the fast and makes its violation a major offense.
4
Recognize the consequence
Kaffarah (expiation) is required only when a valid fast was in progress. Qada is always required for the missed day.
Kaffarah: fasting 60 consecutive days or feeding 60 poor.
5
Consider analogous cases
The text equates the fever case with a woman who breaks her fast thinking she is menstruating.
Both share the same principle: a valid excuse was anticipated but did not come.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الخلاصة : لو كان له نوبة حمى فأكل قبل أن تظهر يعني في يوم النوبة لا بأس، فإن لم يحم فيه كان عليه الكفارة كما لو أفطرت على ظن أنه يوم حيضها فلم تحض كان عليها الكفارة لوجود الإفطار في يوم ليس فيه شبهة الإباحة. وهذا إذا أفطر بعد ما نوى الصوم وشرع فيه أما لو لم ينو كان عليه القضاء هذا دون الكفارة.
In al-Khulasa: If he has a periodic fever and eats before it appears, meaning on the day of the episode, there is no harm. But if he does not develop (fever), kaffarah is obligatory upon him, just as if a woman broke her fast thinking it was the day of her menses, and she did not menstruate, kaffarah is upon her because there is breaking of the fast on a day that has no semblance of permissibility. This is if he broke part after intending and starting the fast, but if he did not intend, then only qada is upon him, not kaffarah.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This ruling from al-Khulasa clarifies the consequence of breaking the fast based on an anticipated excuse that fails to materialize. The case of a person with periodic fever: if he eats on the day the fever typically occurs, he assumes he will be excused, but if the fever does not come, he has broken a valid fast without a genuine excuse, so kaffarah (expiation) is obligatory. The same applies to a woman who breaks her fast thinking she is menstruating, but her period does not start. However, if he had not yet made the intention to fast (i.e., he had not started fasting that day), then only qada (makeup) is due, not kaffarah, because the fast was not yet in effect. This distinguishes between breaking a fast in progress and simply not fasting.

Key Terms
الكفارة (kaffarah): Expiation required for intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast without a valid excuse.
القضاء (qada): Making up a missed fast on another day.
شبهة الإباحة (shubbat al-ibahah): Semblance of permissibility; a doubt or indication that an act may be allowed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman breaks her fast thinking it is the first day of her menses, but she does not menstruate. According to the Hanafi school, what must she do?
A) Only qada (make up the day)
B) Qada and kaffarah
C) Nothing, because she had a valid doubt
D) Sadaqah (charity) but no fasting

Key Takeaway
Breaking a Ramadan fast based on an anticipated excuse that does not occur requires kaffarah if the fast had been intended; otherwise, only qada is due.

The Traveler's Fasting: When to Fast and When to Break
Commentary on the matn regarding the traveler's fasting and its preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the traveler's fasting and apply the conditions under which breaking fast becomes better or obligatory.

Core Idea
A traveler should fast if it does not harm him; otherwise, breaking fast is better or even obligatory.

Fasting in Ramadan is a pillar of Islam, but Allah has granted concessions to travelers due to the inherent hardship of travel. The Hanafi school balances the virtue of fasting in the best month with the mercy of the concession. This lesson unpacks the ruling from the matn and its commentary, clarifying when fasting is preferred, when breaking fast is better, and the rare cases where breaking fast becomes obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
The text states that the traveler is permitted to break fast because travel is not free from hardship. This is a general concession (rukhsa).
The default is permissibility, not obligation.
2
Determine the preferred action
Fasting is more beloved (ahabb) if it does not harm the traveler. The primary dalil is Quran 2:184 and the virtue of performing the act in its optimal time.
This is a recommendation, not an obligation.
3
Apply the condition of harm
If fasting causes harm or extreme difficulty, breaking fast becomes better (afdal). The hadith 'It is not righteousness to fast while traveling' supports this.
Harm includes bodily harm and, in some cases, financial harm from shared expenses.
4
Recognize when breaking fast is obligatory
If fasting would lead to fear of destruction (e.g., death), breaking fast becomes wajib. Similarly, coercion with threat of death makes breaking fast obligatory.
This is distinct from the normal recommendation; here, fasting would be sinful.
5
Note the exceptions and disputes
The text discusses specific scenarios: entering a city, shared expenses, coercion of family members, and the dispute with Ali and Ibn Abbas about initiating travel in Ramadan.
The Hanafi school holds that initiating travel in Ramadan is permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم فصل في العوارض
قوله : (وللمسافر وصومه أحب إن لم يضره) أي جاز للمسافر الفطر لأن السفر لا يعرا المشقة فجعل نفسه عذراً بخلاف المرض لأنه قد يخفف بالصوم فشرط كونه مفضياً إلى الحرج، وإنما كان الصوم أفضل إن لم يضره لقوله تعالى {وإن تصوموا خير لكم} [البقرة: 184] ولأن رمضان أفضل الوقتين فكأن فيه الأداء أولى ولا يرد علينا القصر في الصلوات فإنه واجب حتى يأثم بالإتمام لأن القصر هو العزيمة وتسميتهم له رخصة إسقاط مجاز، وقول صاحب غاية البيان أن القصر أفضل تسامح. ولو قال المصنف «وصومهما أحب إن لم يضرهما» لكان أولى لشموله قيد بقوله إن لم يضره لأن الصوم إن ضره بأن شق عليه فالفطر أفضل لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام ليس من البر الصيام في السفر قاله لرجل صائم يصب عليه الماء. وفي المحيط : ولو أراد المسافر أن يقيم في مصر أو يدخل مصره كره صوم ذلك اليوم، لأنه يجتمع في اليوم المبيح وهو السفر والمحرم وهو الإقامة فرجحنا المحرم احتياطاً. وصرح في الخلاصة بكراهة الصوم إن أجهده وأطلق الضرر ولم يقيده بضرر بدنه لأنه لو لم يضره الصوم لكن كان رفقاؤه أو عامتهم مفطرين والنفقة مشتركة بينهم فالإفطار أفضل - كذا في الخلاصة والظهيرية - لأن ضرر المال كضرر البدن . وأشار إلى أن إنشاء السفر في شهر رمضان جائز لإطلاق النص خلافاً لعلي وابن عباس كذا في المحيط. وفي الولوالجية والسفر الذي يبيح الفطر هو الذي يبيح القصر لأن كلاهما قد ثبتت رخصته. وأطلق السفر فشمل سفر الطاعة والمعصية لما عرف وأراد بالضرر الضرر الذي ليس فيه خوف الهلاك لأن ما فيه خوف الهلاك بسبب الصوم فالإفطار في مثله واجب لا أنه أفضل. كذا في البدائع. ومنه ما إذا أكره المريض والمسافر فإن الإفطار واجب ولا يسعه الصوم حتى لو امتنع من الإفطار فقتل يأثم كالإكراه على أكل الميتة بخلاف ما إذا كان صحيحاً مقيماً فأكره بقتل نفسه فإنه يرخص له الفطر والصوم أفضل حتى لو امتنع من الإفطار حتى قتل يثاب عليه لأن الوجوب ثابت حالة الإكراه وأثر الرخصة بالإكراه في سقوط الإثم بالترك لا في سقوط الواجب كالإكراه على الكفر كذا في البدائع وقيدنا بكونه أكره بقتل نفسه لأنه لو قيل له لتفطرن أو لأقتلن ولدك فإنه لا يباح له الفطر كقوله لتشربن الخمر أو لأقتلن ولدك فصار كتهديده بالحبس كذا في النهاية. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان المسافر إذا تذكر شيئاً قد نسيه في منزله فدخل فأفطر ثم خرج - قال عليه الكفارة قياساً لأنه مقيم عند الأكل حيث رفض سفره بالعود إلى منزله وبالقياس نأخذ ا هـ .
Book of Fasting, Section on Excuses. His statement: (And for the traveler, his fasting is more beloved if it does not harm him) meaning it is permissible for the traveler to break the fast because travel is not free from hardship, so it is made an excuse in itself unlike illness because illness may be alleviated by fasting, so it is conditioned that it leads to distress. However, fasting is better if it does not harm him due to His statement {And that you fast is better for you} [2:184] and because Ramadan is the best of the two times, so performing it in its time is better. The shortening of prayers is not a counterexample because it is obligatory, so one sins by completing them; shortening is the resolved determination and calling it a concession is a metaphorical simplification. The statement of the author of Ghayat al-Bayan that shortening is better is lax. If the author had said 'and the fasting of both is more beloved if it does not harm them' it would have been more appropriate for comprehensiveness. He restricted with 'if it does not harm him' because if fasting harms him, such that it is difficult for him, then breaking fast is better due to his statement peace be upon him: 'It is not righteousness to fast while traveling' said to a man who was fasting and water was being poured on him. In al-Muhit: If a traveler intends to stay in a city or enters his city, it is disliked for him to fast that day because the permissible (travel) and the prohibited (residence) come together in the day, so we tip the scale to the prohibited as a precaution. In al-Khulasa it is explicitly stated that fasting is disliked if it exhausts him, and harm is left unrestricted and not limited to bodily harm because even if fasting does not harm him bodily but his companions or most of them are breaking fast and expenses are shared, then breaking fast is better. So in al-Khulasa and al-Zahiriyya because harm to wealth is like harm to body. It is indicated that initiating travel in Ramadan is permissible due to the absolute text, contrary to Ali and Ibn Abbas, as in al-Muhit. In al-Walwalijiyya: The travel that permits breaking fast is that which permits shortening, because both concessions are established. Travel is left unrestricted so it includes travel of obedience and disobedience. He meant by harm the harm that does not involve fear of destruction because what involves fear of destruction due to fasting, breaking fast is obligatory, not merely better. So in al-Bada'i. Among that is when the sick and the traveler are coerced: breaking fast is obligatory and he is not permitted to fast, such that if he refrains from breaking fast and is killed, he sins like coercion to eat carrion, unlike if a healthy resident is coerced by killing: then breaking fast is permissible and fasting is better, such that if he refrains from breaking fast until killed, he is rewarded because obligation is established during coercion and the effect of the concession due to coercion is only to remove sin for leaving, not to remove the obligation, like coercion to disbelief. So in al-Bada'i. We restricted it to coercion by killing because if it is said to him 'Break fast or I will kill your son' then breaking fast is not permissible for him, like saying 'Drink wine or I will kill your son', so it becomes like threatening him with imprisonment. So in al-Nihaya. In Fatawa Qadikhan: A traveler who remembers something he forgot in his home, so he enters and breaks fast then goes out: he said the expiation is due by analogy because he is a resident at the time of eating since he abandoned his travel by returning to his home, and we adopt the analogy. End.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement regarding the traveler's fasting. The author explains that the traveler is permitted to break fast because travel inherently involves hardship, making it an excuse in itself. Unlike illness, which may be mild, travel is assumed to have hardship. Despite this, fasting is preferred (more beloved) if it does not cause harm to the traveler. The proof is the Quranic verse and the fact that Ramadan is the best time. The commentator then clarifies that the shortening of prayers is not analogous because it is obligatory (azima), not a concession. The author of Ghayat al-Bayan's statement that shortening is better is an error. The commentator then discusses various scenarios: if fasting harms the traveler (makes it extremely difficult), breaking fast is better, based on the hadith. The rest of the passage covers rulings from al-Muhit, al-Khulasa, al-Zahiriyya, al-Walwalijiyya, al-Bada'i, al-Nihaya, and Fatawa Qadikhan. Key rulings include: it is disliked for a traveler to fast if he intends to stay in a city or enters his city; breaking fast may be better if companions break fast due to shared expenses; coercion to break fast is obligatory if there is fear of death; a traveler who returns home, breaks fast, and then goes out again must make expiation. The overall theme is the traveler's concession and its limits.

Key Terms
السفر (al-safar): Travel, defined as a journey of sufficient length to permit shortening of prayers (approx. 48 miles).
المشقة (al-mashaqqa): Hardship; travel inherently involves hardship, making it an excuse for breaking fast.
الرخصة (al-rukhsa): Concession; permissible action due to an excuse, like breaking fast while traveling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is breaking fast better for the traveler?
A) Always, because travel is an excuse.
B) When fasting would cause him harm or difficulty.
C) Only if he is afraid of death.
D) When his companions are breaking fast and expenses are shared.

Key Takeaway
A traveler should fast if no harm; if harm, breaking fast is better; if fear of destruction, breaking fast is obligatory.

No Qada for Sick or Traveler Who Dies Before Making Up
Explanation of a matn ruling on qada for missed fasts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for missed Ramadan fasts when death occurs before the excuse ends, and distinguish when a will for fidya becomes necessary.

Core Idea
No make-up for missed fasts if death comes before the excuse lifts.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a key exception in the obligation to make up missed Ramadan fasts. When a person dies while still sick or on travel, the condition for make-up—being able to fast on other days—never materialized. Therefore, no qada is owed. However, if the person recovered or returned home and had time but died before fasting, a will for fidya becomes binding.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
What is the ruling for a sick/traveler who dies before recovery/return? The text says: no make-up required.
2
Isolate the condition (shart)
What must be present for qada to become obligatory? The text: 'they did not reach a number of other days' — meaning they never had the ability to fast on other days (health/home).
The condition is derived from Quran 2:184: 'iddatun min ayyamin ukhar'.
3
Apply the condition to the ruling
Why does absence of this condition remove obligation? Because the very basis of qada depends on being able to fast on other days; if death prevents that opportunity, no duty attaches.
4
Identify the exception (if excuse ends)
What changes if the sick person recovers or the traveler returns home? The condition is now satisfied.
5
Extract the ruling for the exception
Then a will for fidya becomes wajib. Why? Because the person was able but neglected to fast, so the obligation transforms into a financial duty upon the estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا قضاء إن مانا عليهما) أي ولا قضاء على المريض والمسافر إذا ماتا قبل الصحة والإقامة لأنهما لم يدركا عدة من أيام أخر فلم يوجد شرط الأداء وجوب فلم يلزم القضاء. قيد به لأنه لو صح المريض أو أقام المسافر ولم يقض حتى مات لزمه الإيصاء بقدره
His statement: (And no make-up if they die upon the two) meaning no make-up is required of the sick and the traveler if they die before recovery and return home because they did not reach 'a number of other days', so the condition for obligatory performance was not present, so make-up is not binding. He restricted it with this because if the sick person recovers or the traveler returns home and does not make up until death, then it is binding upon him to bequeath its equivalent.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage explains a matn ruling: If a sick person dies while still sick, or a traveler dies while still on travel, they are not required to make up missed fasts. The reason is they never experienced the condition for make-up: being physically able and present at home to fast other days. They never 'reached' the opportunity to fast those days, so no obligation. However, if they become healthy or return from travel and have time to make up but die before doing so, then they must leave a will (وصية) for expiation (fidya). This is a straightforward ruling of fiqh.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada): Make-up of missed obligatory fasts.
الإيصاء (al-isa): Bequeathing; here it means leaving a will to pay fidya (expiation) for missed fasts.
عدة من أيام أخر (iddatun min ayyamin ukhar): A number of other days; the condition for make-up.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it necessary for a sick person who missed Ramadan fasts to leave a will for fidya?
A) If he dies while still sick.
B) If he recovers but dies before making up the fasts.
C) Only if he was a traveler.
D) Only if he had the means to pay fidya.

Key Takeaway
No qada is owed for missed fasts if death occurs before the excuse ends; if the excuse ends and death occurs before make-up, a will for fidya is required.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Vow to Fast: Does the Will Cover the Entire Month or Only Capable Days?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on will scope for vowed fasts after partial recovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the scope of a will for a vowed fast when only partial capability existed, explain their respective proofs, and evaluate the practical consequence (thamar al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A vow creates an obligation, but its scope depends on actual capability: the two Imams say full month, Muhammad says only days of ability.

This passage addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: a sick person vows to fast an entire month, recovers for a single day, and then dies. What must his will cover? The answer differs between Imam Muhammad and the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Understanding this dispute requires careful attention to the cause (sabab) and evidence (dalil) for each position, as well as the practical outcome (thamar al-khilaf). This lesson will guide you through the reasoning of each side and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Contested Ruling
Recognize the scenario: a sick person vows to fast a full month, recovers for one day (becomes capable of fasting that day), then dies. The question is: what must be covered by his will?
The dispute hinges on whether the obligation is tied to the vow's binding nature or to actual capability.
2
State Imam Muhammad’s Position and Its Basis
Imam Muhammad holds that only the day(s) of actual capability must be covered. His reasoning: obligation arises from actual ability; one cannot be obliged for what is impossible.
This is the position later preferred by Quduri and al-Hidayah.
3
State the Position of the Two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Their Basis
The two Imams hold that the entire month must be covered. They argue that the vow itself is a binding cause (sabab) that creates an immediate obligation on the substitute (the will), so the full month remains due once the vow is made.
They draw an analogy with a healthy person who vows a specific month and then recovers a day.
4
Analyze Tahawi’s Refinement
Al-Tahawi introduced a refinement: if the person actually fasted on the day of recovery, then nothing is owed (because that day is fulfilled); if he did not fast, then the dispute remains as stated.
This refinement helps reconcile the positions: it shows that actual performance of the fast extinguishes the obligation for that day.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence and the Mufta Bihi
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that according to the two, the will must cover the full month unless the capable day was fasted; according to Muhammad, only the capable days (and if fasted, nothing). The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is Muhammad's view as preferred by Quduri and al-Hidayah.
Thus, in most cases, the will need only cover the days the vower was actually capable of fasting.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الطحاوي أن هذا قول محمد، وعندهما يلزمه قضاء الكل، وغلطه القدوري وتبعه في الهداية قال : والصحيح أنه لا يلزمه إلا بقدره عند الكل. وإنما الخلاف في النذر بأن يقول المريض لله هذا علي صوم الشهر فصح يوماً ثم مات يلزمه قضاء جميع الشهر عندهما، وعند محمد قضاء ما فيه . والفرق لهما أن النذر سبب فظهر الوجوب في حق الخلف، وفي هذه المسألة السبب إدراك العدة فيتقدر بقدر ما أدرك فيه. وإنما لم يلزمه القضاء قبل الصحة ليظهر في الإيضاء لأنه معلق بالصحة وإن لم يذكر أداة التعليق تصحيحاً لتصرف المكلف ما أمكن فينزل عن الصحة . وأجاب عنه في غاية البيان بأن الجماعة الذي أنكروا الخلاف نشؤوا بعد الطحاوي بكثير من الزمان باعتبار أن الخلاف لم يبلغهم وهو ليس بحجة عليه لأن جهل الإنسان لا يعتبر حجة على غيره، وقد ذكره بعد ما ثبت عنده وهو ممن لا يتهم لأوصافه الجميلة. والحاصل أن الصحيح لو نذر صوم شهر معين ثم مات قبل مجيء الشهر لا يلزمه شيء بلا خلاف، وإن صح يوماً يلزمه الإيصاء بالجميع عندهما، وعند محمد بقدر ما صح. وفصل الطحاوي فقال إن لم يصم اليوم الذي صح فيه لزمه الكل وإن صامه لا يلزمه شيء كالمريض في رمضان إذا صح يوماً فصامه ثم مات لا يلزمه شيء اتفاقاً لأنه بالصوم تعين أنه يصلح فيه قضاء يوم آخر بخلاف ما إذا لم يصمه حيث لا يلزمه الكل كما قدمناه على قول الطحاوي، لأن ما قدر فيه صالح لقضاء اليوم الأول والوسط والأخير فلما قدر على قضاء البعض فكأنه قدر على قضاء الكل إليه أشار في البدائع وغاية البيان.
Al-Tahawi mentioned that this is the saying of Muhammad, and according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) he must make up the whole. Al-Quduri declared him mistaken and was followed by al-Hidayah, saying: The correct view is that he is only obliged for the amount he was capable of, according to all. The difference (between the two positions) is in the vow: when a sick person says, 'For the sake of Allah, I owe the fast of this month,' then he recovers for a day and dies, according to the two he must make up the entire month, and according to Muhammad he makes up only the day he was capable of. Their distinction is that the vow is a cause, so obligation appears in the substitute, whereas in this case the cause is the attainment of the waiting period (iddah), which is limited by what was attained. He is not required to make up before recovery so that it appears in the will, because it is conditional upon recovery even if the conditional particle is not mentioned, to correct the action of the obligated person as much as possible, so it is interpreted as conditional on recovery. He answered in Ghayat al-Bayan that the group who denied the difference arose long after al-Tahawi, considering that the difference did not reach them, and that is not a proof against him because a person's ignorance is not considered a proof against others, and he mentioned it after it was established with him, and he is above suspicion due to his fine qualities. The summary: A healthy person who vows to fast a specific month then dies before the month arrives owes nothing without dispute; if he recovers for a day, he must make a will for the whole according to the two, and according to Muhammad for what he was capable of. Al-Tahawi refined, saying: If he did not fast the day he recovered, the whole is required; if he fasted it, nothing is required, like a sick person in Ramadan who recovers for a day and fasts it then dies, nothing is required by agreement, because by fasting it becomes clear that it is suitable for making up another day, unlike if he did not fast it, where the whole is not required as we previously stated according to al-Tahawi, because what he was capable of is suitable for making up the first, middle, and last day, so when he was capable of some, it is as if he was capable of the whole, as indicated in al-Bada'i' and Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a sick person who makes a vow to fast an entire month, then recovers for a single day and dies. The core question is: what must he leave in his will? Imam Muhammad holds that only the day he was actually capable of (the one day of health) must be covered. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two) hold that he must make a will for the entire month. The reasoning for the two is that a vow (nadhr) is a binding cause (sabab) that creates an obligation in the substitute (the will), and therefore the whole month is owed once the vow is made, even if only one day was possible. Muhammad's argument is that the obligation arises only from the actual ability to fast, so it is limited to what was possible. The text also reports Tahawi's refinement: if the person actually fasted on the day of recovery, then nothing is owed (because that day is then used for the current Ramadan or another obligation), and the principle is that capability for one day does not imply capability for the whole. The commentator supports Muhammad's view, citing Quduri and al-Hidayah, and refutes those who dismissed Tahawi's report as mistaken. The passage concludes with the analogous case of a healthy person who vows a specific month: if he dies before the month, no will is needed; if he recovers a day, the dispute is similar.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship (here, fasting) that becomes obligatory upon fulfillment of a condition (explicit or implied).
إيصاء (īṣā'): Making a will; in this context, a testamentary instruction to feed or compensate for missed fasts after death.
قضاء (qaḍā'): Making up a missed obligatory fast after its time has passed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, what must a sick person's will cover if he vowed to fast a month, recovered for one day, and then died without fasting that day?
A) The entire month
B) Only that one day
C) Nothing
D) Half the month

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves this dispute by tying obligation to actual ability, so the will need only cover days the vower could have fasted.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Feeding for Missed Fasts: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on guardian feeding for missed fasts with intra-madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the obligation of a guardian to feed on behalf of a deceased who missed fasts, distinguishing between the method of Al-Hakim (Muhammad and Zufar) and the method of fatwa, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A vow is binding immediately on the liability — so the entire month's feeding is required, regardless of ability to perform.

This lesson examines a detailed commentary from Al-Bahr al-Raiq on the ruling when a sick person or traveler dies having missed fasts. The matn states that the guardian must feed for each day like fitra if the deceased made a will. The commentary reveals an important intra-Hanafi dispute between the method of Al-Hakim (attributed to Muhammad and Zufar) and the method of fatwa (the relied-upon position). Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly determining the obligation of feeding when vows are involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The matn states: the guardian feeds for each day like fitra by will. This is the starting point for the dispute.
The ruling applies to anyone who died with missed obligatory fasts, not just sick/traveler.
2
Extract the condition of the will
The feeding is only obligatory if the deceased made a will. Without a will, the heirs are not obliged, similar to zakah.
The will ensures the choice of the deceased; otherwise it is a right of Allah that does not transfer automatically.
3
Understand the quantity comparison
Feeding is like fitra in quantity: half a sa' of wheat or raisins, or a sa' of dates or barley. But it is not identical to fitra in nature because mere permission (ibaha) suffices here, whereas fitra requires ownership (tamlik).
The comparison is only in amount, not in legal category.
4
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The dispute: If a person made a vow to fast a month and had some days of ability but did not fast them, then died, does the guardian feed for the entire month or only for the days he was capable?
Muhammad and Zufar hold the method of Al-Hakim: only capable days. The method of fatwa (mufta bihi) holds the entire month.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference appears when the person fasted some of the capable days. On the method of Al-Hakim, no feeding for the remaining days; on the method of fatwa, the entire month's feeding is still required.
This is because the method of fatwa sees the vow as binding immediately in the liability, regardless of ability to perform.

كتاب الصوم فصل في العوارض
ماتا عليهما ويطعم وليهما لكل يوم كالفطرة بوصية وقضيا ما قدرا بلا شرط ولاء فإذا لا
الطحاوي فقال : إن لم يصم اليوم الذي صح فيه لزمه الكل، وإن صامه لا يلزمه شيء كالمريض في رمضان إذا صح يوماً فصامه ثم مات لا يلزمه شيء اتفاقاً لأنه بالصوم تعين أنه يصلح فيه قضاء يوم آخر بخلاف ما إذا لم يصمه حيث لا يلزمه الكل كما قدمناه على قول الطحاوي، لأن ما قدر فيه صالح لقضاء اليوم الأول والوسط والأخير فلما قدر على قضاء البعض فكأنه قدر على قضاء الكل إليه أشار في البدائع وغاية البيان. وفي الولوالجية: ولو أوجب على نفسه اعتکاف شهر وهو مريض ثم مات قبل أن يصح لم يجب عليه لأنه لم يجب عليه أداء الأصل فلا يجب أداء البدل، ولو أوجب على نفسه اعتكاف شهر وهو صحيح فعاش عشرة أيام ثم مات أطعم عنه الشهر كله لأن الاعتكاف مما لا يتجزى
قوله : (ويطعم وليهما لكل يوم كالفطرة (بوصية أي يطعم ولي المريض والمسافر عنهما عن كل يوم أدركاه كصدقة الفطر إذا أوصيانه لأنهما لما عجزا عن الصوم الذي هو في ذمتهما التحقا بالشيخ الفاني دلالة لا قياساً فوجب عليهما الإيصاء بقدر ما أدركا فيه عدة من أيام أخركما في الهداية. ولو قال ويطعم ولي من مات وعليه قضاء رمضان» لكان أشمل لأن فعندهما عقيبه وأنما التأخير لتسهيل الأداء إلا أنه لا بد من التمكن من الأداء لئلا يلزم تكليف ما لا يطاق، ولهما على طريقة الفتاوى أن اللزوم إذا لم يظهر في حق الأداء يظهر في خلفه وهو الإطعام، فإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إذا نذر شهراً غير معين ثم أقام بعد النذر أياماً قادراً على الصوم فلم يصم يلزمه الوصية لجميع الشهر على كلا الطريقتين. وقال محمد وزفر لقدر ما قدر؛ وجه قولهما على طريق الحاكم أن ما أدركه صالح لصوم كل يوم من أيام النذر فإذا لم يصم جعل كالقادر على الجميع فوجب الإيصاء، وعلى طريقة الفتاوى النذر ملزم في الذمة الساعة ولا يشترط إمكان الأداء، وفائدة الخلاف إذا صام ما أدرك فعلى الأول لا يجب الإيصاء بالباقي، وعلى الثاني يجب. ومثله لو نذر ليلاً صوم شهر غير معين ومات في الليل لا يجب الإيصاء على الأول لعدم الإدراك، ويجب على الثاني، ولو أوجب على نفسه صوم رجب ثم أقام أياماً ولم يصم فقد اهـ. ما في السراج ملخصاً. علم وجه الفرق بين النذر المعين والمطلق. ثم قال في السراج: مريض لا يقدر على الصوم نذر صوم رجب ثم دخل رجب وهو مريض ثم صح بعده يوماً أو يومين فلم يصم ثم مرض ومات، فعليه بجميع الشهر، أما على طريقة الفتاوى فظاهر، وكذا على طريقة الحاكم لأن بخروج الشهر الإيصاء كما مر فيه وجب عليه الإيصاء بجميع الشهر، وبه الشهر، المعين وصحته بعده وجب عليه صوم شهر مطلق، فإذا لم يصم في النذر المطلق إذا بقي يوماً أو يومين فلم يصم ثم مرض ومات، فعليه الإيصاء بجميع أما على طريقة الفتاوى فظاهر وكذا على طريقة الحاكم لأن بخروج الشهر المعين وصحته بعده وجب عليه صوم شهر مطلق، فإذا لم يصم فيه وجب عليه الإيصاء بجميع الشهر كما في النذر المطلق إذا بقي يوماً أو يومين يقدر على الصوم ولم يصم ثم مات ا هـ . قوله : (لكان أشمل الخ) أجاب في النهر
البحر الرائق/ ج ٢/ م ٣٢
هذا الحكم لا يخص المريض والمسافر ولا من أفطر لعذر بل يدخل فيه من أفطر متعمداً ووجب القضاء عليه، بل أراد بالولي من له ولاية التصرف في ماله بعد موته فيدخل وصيهما، وأراد بتشبيهه بالفطرة كالكفارة التشبيه من جهة المقدار بأن يطعم عن صوم كل يوم نصف صاع من بر أو زبيب أو صاعاً من تمر أو شعير لا التشبيه مطلقاً لأن الإباحة كافية هنا، ولهذا عبر بالإطعام دون الإيتاء دون صدقة الفطر فإن الركن فيها التمليك ولا تكفي الإباحة. وقيد بالوصية لأنه لو لم يأمر لا يلزم الورثة شيء كالزكاة لأنها من حقوق الله تعالى، ولا بد فيها من الإيصاء ليتحقق الاختيار إلا إذا مات قبل أن يؤدي العشر فإنه يؤخذ من تركته من غير إيصاء لشدة تعلق العشر بالعين. كذا في البدائع من كتاب الزكاة في مسألة إذا باع صاحب المال ماله قبل أداء الزكاة، ومع ذلك لو تبرع الورثة أجزأه إن شاء الله تعالى. وكذا كفارة اليمين والقتل إذا تبرع الوارث بالإطعام والكسوة يجوز ولا يجوز التبرع بالإعتاق لما فيه من إلزام الولاء للميت بغير رضاه. وأشار بالوصية إلى أنه . ثلث ماله قاضيخان في فتاواه وإلى أن الصلاة كالصوم بجامع أنهما من حقوقه تعالى بل أولى لكونها كذا معتبر من صرح به بأن من أفطر متعمداً فوجوبها عليه بالأولى على أن الفصل معقود للعوارض قوله : (بل أراد بالولي) في بعض النسخ وفي بعضها بدون «بل» قوله : (وكذا كفارة اليمين والقتل الخ) كذا في الزيلعي والدرر . قال في الشرنبلالية أقول : لا يصح تبرع الوارث في كفارة القتل بشيء لأن الواجب فيها ابتداء عتق رقبة مؤمنة ولا يصح إعتاق الوارث عنه كما ذكره ، والصوم فيها بدل عن الإعتاق لا يصح فيه الفدية كما يأتي ا.هـ. ومثله في العزمية معترضاً على صاحب الدرر والزيلعي، وادعى أن الزيلعي وهم في فهم كلام الكافي وعبارة الكافي على ما في شرح الشيخ إسماعيل : على معسر كفارة يمين أو قتل وعجز عن الصوم لم تجز الفدية كمتمتع عجز عن الدم والصوم لأن الصوم هنا بدل ولا بدل للبدل، فإن مات وأوصى بالتكفير صح من : ثلثه التبرع في الكسوة والإطعام لأن وصح الإعتقاء بلا إيصاء إلزام الولاء على الميت ولا إلزام في الكسوة والإطعام انتهت. وأنت خبير بأنها نص فيما قاله الزيلعي، وأما ما ادعاه في العزمية من أن الموضوع في كلام الكافي هو الكفارة مطلقاً ولما وقع في سياق كلامه ذكر كفارة يمين أو قتل وهما قد اشتركا في مسألة الإعتاق، ذهل الزيلعي عن حقيقة الحال فساق كلامه على تعلق هذه المسألة بهما وقال ما قال ا.هـ. فبعيد، ولا ينافي ذلك ما سيأتي في شرح قوله وللشيخ الفاني من أنه لو وجبت عليه كفارة يمين أو قتل لا تجوز له الفدية لأن الصوم هنا بدل عن غيره فإن ذاك في الحي وما هنا فيما إذا تبرع الولي فيصح لعدم إمكان الأصل لعدم إمكان الإعتاق لما فيه من الإلزام كما بسطه الشيخ إسماعيل في الجواب عن الدرر . وفي الإمداد في فصل إسقاط الصلاة ولزم عليه يعني من أفطر في رمضان الوصية بما قدر عليه وبقي في ذمته حتى أدركه الموت وأوصى بفدية ما عليه من صيام فرض رمضان، وكذا صوم يمين وقتل خطأ وظهار وجناية على إحرام وقتل محرم صيد أو صوم منذور، فيخرج عنه وليه من عنه كفارة
Book of Fasting, Section on Impediments. Both (the sick and traveler) die and their guardian feeds for each day like fitra by will, and they make up what they were able to without condition of succession. So if... At-Tahawi said: If he did not fast the day in which he became healthy, then the entire (month) is incumbent; if he fasted it, nothing is incumbent, like the sick person in Ramadan who becomes healthy for a day and fasts it then dies, nothing is incumbent by agreement because by fasting it becomes determined that it is suitable for making up another day, contrary to if he did not fast it where the entire is not incumbent as we have mentioned based on At-Tahawi's statement, because that which he was able to is suitable for making up the first, middle and last day, so when he was able to make up some, it is as if he was able to make up all. He indicated that in Al-Bada'i and Ghayat al-Bayan. And in Al-Walwaljiyya: If one obligated upon himself i'tikaf for a month while sick and then died before becoming healthy, it is not obligatory because the original act was not obligatory, so the substitute is not obligatory. If he obligated upon himself i'tikaf for a month while healthy and he lived ten days then died, feeding is done for the entire month because i'tikaf is indivisible. His statement: 'And their guardian feeds for each day like fitra by will' means the guardian of the sick and traveler feeds for each day they caught up, like sadaqat al-fitr if they willed it because since they were unable to fast which was in their liability, they become like the elderly non-fasting person by indication not by analogy, so it is obligatory upon them to will according to what they caught up with days other than those. As in Al-Hidayah. And if he said 'the guardian of whoever died with a missed Ramadan fast feeds' it would be more comprehensive because... This ruling does not exclusively apply to the sick and traveler or those who broke the fast due to an excuse, but rather includes one who intentionally broke the fast and owed a make-up. Rather, by 'guardian' he means one who has authority to dispose of his wealth after death, which includes his executor. And by comparing to fitra like kaffarah, the comparison is in terms of quantity: he feeds for each fast half a sa' of wheat or raisins or a sa' of dates or barley, not an absolute comparison because permissibility suffices here, hence he used 'feeding' not 'giving', unlike zakat al-fitr where the pillar is ownership and permissibility does not suffice. And it is restricted to a will because if he does not order, the heirs are not obliged, like zakah, because it is from the rights of Allah, and a will is necessary to actualize choice, except if he dies before paying the tithe (ushr), then it is taken from his estate without a will due to the strong attachment of ushr to the asset. Thus in Al-Bada'i from the book of zakah... Likewise, the expiation for oath and killing: if the heir voluntarily gives food and clothing, it is permissible, but voluntary freeing of a slave is not permissible because it imposes wala' on the deceased without his consent. And he indicated by the will that it is from one-third of his wealth. Qadi Khan in his Fatawa... And that prayer is like fasting in that both are rights of Allah, rather more so... He said: (بل أراد بالولي) in some copies... He said: (وكذا كفارة اليمين والقتل الخ) thus in al-Zayla'i and al-Durar. He said in al-Sharnabulaliyya: I say: voluntary action by the heir in expiation for killing is not valid for anything because the obligation therein initially is freeing a believing slave, and the heir cannot free a slave on his behalf as mentioned, and fasting therein is a substitute for freeing, and redemption (fidya) is not valid for it as will come. Similarly in al-‘Azmiyya objecting to the author of al-Durar and al-Zayla'i, claiming that al-Zayla'i misunderstood the statement of al-Kafi... And you are aware that it is text regarding what al-Zayla'i said, but what is claimed in al-‘Azmiyya that the subject in the statement of al-Kafi is expiation absolutely, and since in the context he mentioned expiation for oath and killing, and they both share the issue of freeing, al-Zayla'i was heedless of the reality... This does not contradict what will come in the explanation of his statement 'and for the old non-fasting person' that if expiation for oath or killing is obligatory upon him, redemption (fidya) is not permissible for him because fasting here is a substitute for something else; that is regarding the living, and here it is when the guardian voluntarily does it, so it is valid due to impossibility of the original... And in al-Imdad in the section on dropping prayer...
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on the chapter of fasting, specifically the section on impediments ('awarid). The author explains the ruling when a sick person or traveler dies having missed fasts. The matn states that the guardian must feed for each day like fitra if the deceased made a will. The commentary opens by discussing a dispute attributed to At-Tahawi regarding whether the entire month's feeding is obligatory or only the days the person was capable of fasting. The author then cites Al-Walwaljiyya regarding i'tikaf. Next, the commentator explains the phrase 'feeding like fitra by will', clarifying that the feeding is like sadaqat al-fitr in quantity (half sa' of wheat or raisins, or a sa' of dates or barley) but not in nature because here mere permission (ibaha) suffices whereas fitra requires ownership (tamlik). The condition of a will (wasiyya) is necessary because otherwise the heirs are not obliged, similar to zakah. The commentary then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute: Muhammad and Zufar hold that feeding is only required for the days he was capable of fasting, while the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) on the method of fatwa is that the entire month's feeding is required because the vow (nadhr) becomes binding immediately in the liability, regardless of ability to perform. The practical consequence is that if he fasted some days, on the first view no feeding is required for the remaining, on the second it is. The author also discusses the difference between a specified vow (mu'ayyan) and an unspecified vow (mutlaq). Later, the commentator addresses the definition of 'wali' (guardian) and notes that the ruling applies not only to sick and travelers but also to anyone who intentionally broke the fast. The passage concludes with a discussion about kaffarah (expiation) for oath and killing, where the heir may give food or clothing but cannot free a slave because that would impose wala' on the deceased without consent. There is a citation of a dispute in Al-‘Azmiyya regarding the correct understanding of a passage in Al-Kafi. The overall theme is the obligation of feeding as a substitute for missed fasts and the conditions surrounding it.

Key Terms
الفطرة (al-fitrah): Sadaqat al-fitr, the charity given at the end of Ramadan. Here it refers to the quantity of food given as compensation for missed fasts.
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A will or bequest. Here it is required from the deceased to obligate the guardian to feed on his behalf.
الولي (al-wali): The guardian or executor of the deceased's estate. He is the one who has authority over the deceased's property after death.
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow. In this context, a person vows to fast a month. The vow creates a liability in the dhimmah.
طريقة الفتاوى (tariqat al-fatawa): The method of fatwa, i.e., the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, as opposed to the method of Al-Hakim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On the method of fatwa (mufta bihi), if a person made a vow to fast a month, then had some days of ability but did not fast them, then died, what is the obligation?
A) The guardian must feed for the entire month.
B) The guardian must feed only for the days he was capable of fasting.
C) Nothing is required because the vow is invalid.
D) The guardian must fast for the deceased.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view requires the guardian to feed for the entire month of missed fasts if the deceased made a will, because a vow is binding immediately in the liability regardless of ability to perform.

Voluntary Feeding by Heir for Deceased's Kaffarah: The Valid and the Invalid
Commentary on the ruling that the guardian must feed for missed fasts, comparing to kaffarah and zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why voluntary feeding by an heir is valid for the deceased's kaffarah of killing, while emancipation is not, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on this issue.

Core Idea
Voluntary feeding by heir is valid; emancipation imposes wala' without consent and is invalid.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a person dies owing kaffarah for killing (or oath) and the heir wishes to perform the expiation voluntarily. The Hanafi school largely permits feeding and clothing but not emancipation. We will explore the reasoning and the intra-madhhab disagreement, drawing from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the heir is doing (feeding/emancipation) and whether it is valid or invalid according to the text.
The act of feeding for kaffarah of killing is ruled permissible and valid if done voluntarily by the heir; emancipation is ruled invalid.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof given in the text: emancipation imposes wala' (clientage) on the deceased without his consent, which is not permissible. Feeding and clothing do not impose such a relationship.
The proof is based on the principle that no one can impose a legal relationship on another without consent, even for the deceased.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the proof yields the ruling: because emancipation would create wala' which is a harm (darar) and invalid, while feeding does not, so feeding is valid.
The key is the absence of imposition; feeding is a simple transfer of benefit without legal consequences.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the opposing view: al-Sharnabulali and al-'Azmiyya argue that kaffarah of killing originally requires emancipation, and feeding is a substitute that cannot be performed by the heir because the substitute cannot stand in for the original after death.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; al-Zayla'i and al-Durar permit feeding, while the others restrict it.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq supports al-Zayla'i, citing al-Kafi and Shaykh Isma'il that because emancipation is impossible after death (due to wala' imposition), the substitute feeding becomes valid.
The tarjih is based on the impossibility of the original act, making the substitute the only viable option.

This ruling does not specifically apply to the sick and traveler nor to one who broke fast for an excuse, but rather includes one who broke fast intentionally and must make it up. Rather, he intended by 'guardian' one who has authority over his property after his death, so it includes their executor. And he intended by his comparison to fitrah and kaffarah a comparison in terms of quantity: that he feeds for each day of fasting half a sa' of wheat or raisins or a sa' of dates or barley; not an absolute comparison because permission is sufficient here. For this reason he expressed 'feeding' without 'giving', unlike sadaqat al-fitr where the pillar is ownership and permission is not sufficient. And he restricted it by will because if he does not command, nothing is binding on the heirs, like zakat, because it is from the rights of Allah. And following a will is necessary to realize choice except if he dies before paying 'ushr, for it is taken from his estate without a will due to the strong attachment of 'ushr to the asset. As stated in al-Bada'i' from the book of zakat in the issue: if the owner of property sells his property before paying zakat, and even if the heirs voluntarily give it, it suffices if Allah wills. Likewise, the kaffarah of oath and killing: if the heir voluntarily gives food and clothing, it is permissible, but voluntary emancipation is not permissible because it would impose wala' on the deceased without his consent. And he indicated by the will that it is from one-third of his property (Qadikhan in his fatawa). And that prayer is like fasting in that both are from the rights of Allah, rather more so because it is considered from one who explicitly stated that one who broke fast intentionally, its obligation is a fortiori since the chapter is devoted to impediments. His statement: 'Rather he intended by al-wali' - in some copies, and in some without 'bal'. His statement: 'Likewise kaffarah of oath and killing etc.' as in al-Zayla'i and al-Durar. He said in al-Sharnabulaliyya: I say: voluntary giving by the heir in kaffarah of killing is not valid for anything because the obligation initially is to free a believing slave, and the heir's freeing on his behalf is not valid as he mentioned. And fasting in it is a substitute for emancipation, and redemption is not valid for it as will come. And similar is in al-'Azmiyya objecting to the author of al-Durar and al-Zayla'i, claiming that al-Zayla'i erred in understanding the words of al-Kafi. The wording of al-Kafi as in the commentary of Shaykh Isma'il: 'One who is in difficulty owes kaffarah of oath or killing and is unable to fast, redemption is not permissible, like a mutamatti' unable to offer blood and fast, because fasting here is a substitute and there is no substitute for a substitute. If he dies and bequeaths expiation, it is valid from one-third; voluntary giving in clothing and food [is valid] because emancipation without a will imposes wala' on the deceased, and there is no imposition in clothing and food.' End. And you are aware that it is explicit regarding what al-Zayla'i said. As for what he claimed in al-'Azmiyya that the subject in the words of al-Kafi is kaffarah in general, and because in the context he mentioned kaffarah of oath or killing, and they both share in the issue of emancipation, al-Zayla'i was unaware of the true state of affairs and applied his words to both issues and said what he said. That is far-fetched. And this does not contradict what will come in the commentary on his statement 'and for the elderly shaykh' that if one owes kaffarah of oath or killing, redemption is not permissible for him because fasting here is a substitute for something else, for that is concerning the living, and here is where the guardian voluntarily gives, so it is valid due to the impossibility of the original due to the impossibility of emancipation because of the imposition [of wala'] as elaborated by Shaykh Isma'il in his response to al-Durar. And in al-Imdad in the chapter on abandonment of prayer: 'And it is incumbent upon him, meaning one who broke fast in Ramadan, to bequeath what he can, and what remains in his liability until death overtakes him, and he bequeaths redemption for the obligatory fasts of Ramadan, and likewise fast of oath, accidental killing, zihar, transgression during ihram, killing a game in ihram, or vowed fast, then his guardian should [feed] on his behalf.'
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the ruling regarding feeding on behalf of a deceased person for missed fasts. The author clarifies that the ruling applies to anyone who missed fasting, not just those with excuses. He explains that the comparison to fitrah and kaffarah is only in the quantity (half sa' of wheat or raisins, or a sa' of dates/barley), not in the nature of the obligation, because feeding (it'am) is sufficient here without requiring ownership. The guardian (wali) referred to is the executor (wasi) who has authority over the deceased's property. A will is required for obligations like zakat to ensure choice, except for 'ushr which is attached to the asset itself. The passage then discusses kaffarah of oath and killing: voluntary feeding and clothing by the heir are valid, but emancipation is not because it imposes wala' (clientage) on the deceased without consent. A scholarly dispute is presented: al-Zayla'i and al-Durar permit voluntary feeding; al-Sharnabulali and al-'Azmiyya object, arguing that kaffarah of killing requires emancipation initially and feeding is a substitute not valid by heir. The commentary supports al-Zayla'i by citing al-Kafi and Shaykh Isma'il, concluding that voluntary feeding by the guardian is valid because emancipation is impossible due to imposition of wala'. Finally, it quotes al-Imdad listing obligations for which the deceased should bequeath redemption: missed Ramadan fasts, oath fasts, accidental killing, zihar, ihram transgressions, etc., and the guardian executes.

Key Terms
ولى (wali): Guardian with authority over the deceased's property, specifically the executor (wasi).
إطعام (it'am): Feeding, here a substitute for missed fasts, done by giving food (half sa' etc.) without requiring ownership transfer.
فدية (fidya): Redemption payment for missed fasts when unable to fast, usually feeding.
ولاء (wala'): Clientage relationship established by emancipation, where the freed slave's inheritance and allegiance go to the emancipator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is voluntary emancipation by the heir invalid for the deceased's kaffarah of killing?
A) Because emancipation is not mentioned in the Quran for kaffarah.
B) Because it imposes wala' on the deceased without his consent.
C) Because the heir cannot own the slave.
D) Because emancipation requires the deceased's personal action.

Key Takeaway
Voluntary feeding by the heir for the deceased's kaffarah of killing is valid; emancipation is invalid because it imposes wala' without consent.

Qada of Ramadan: Delay Allowed and Concession for Pregnant/Nursing Women
Ruling on sequence of qada and concession for pregnant/nursing women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that qada of Ramadan is on tarkhi (delay) and apply the concession for pregnant/nursing women to break fast and pay fidya.

Core Idea
Qada of Ramadan is not immediate; delay is allowed, and pregnant/nursing women may break fast for fear of harm.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains two key Hanafi rulings: the timing of making up missed Ramadan fasts and the special concession for pregnant and nursing women. Understanding the principle of tarkhi (delay) versus fawr (immediacy) is essential for correctly applying the school's position on voluntary fasting before completing qada. The passage also clarifies that pregnant/nursing women who fear harm to themselves or the child may break their fast and must compensate with fidya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First, note that qada of Ramadan is wajib but the timing is on delay (tarkhi). Second, note that pregnant and nursing women may break their fast if they fear harm to themselves or the child, and they must pay fidya.
2
Locate the primary dalil
For qada timing, the Quranic verse 'fiddatun min ayyamin ukhar' (2:184) is cited as absolute, implying tarkhi. For the concession, a hadith about Allah lifting fasting from the traveler and from the pregnant/nursing woman is mentioned.
The verse is from Surah al-Baqarah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'a number of other days' is unrestricted, so it does not require immediate performance. The hadith explicitly permits breaking the fast for pregnant/nursing women in case of fear.
The qira'ah of Ubayy adding 'consecutive' is not mutawatir and thus not binding.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that qada is immediate (fawr) because the original fast was time-specific. The Hanafi school responds that the command is not bound to a specific time.
This ikhtilaf affects whether voluntary fasts before qada are disliked.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
In the Hanafi school, it is not makruh to fast voluntarily before completing qada. In the Shafi'i school, it is makruh. This is a practical difference arising from the disagreement on tarkhi vs. fawr.
Also, if death occurs before completing qada, the Hanafi ruling on fidya differs.

(وقضيا ما قدرا بلا شرط ولاء) أي لا يشترط التتابع في القضاء لإطلاق قوله تعالى فعدة من أيام أخر ﴾ [البقرة: ١٨٤] والذي في قراءة أبي «فعدة من أيام أخر متتابعه غير مشهور لا يزاد بمثله بخلاف قراءة ابن مسعود في كفارة اليمين فإنها مشهورة فيزاد. كذا في النهاية والكافي. لكن المستحب التتابع. وأشار بإطلاقه إلى أن القضاء على التراخي لأن الأمر فيه مطلق وهو على التراخي كما عرف في الأصول. ومعنى التراخي عدم تعين الزمن الأول للفعل ففي أي وقت شرع فيه كان ممتثلاً ولا إثم عليه بالتأخير، ويتضيق عليه الوجوب في آخر عمره في زمان يتمكن فيه من الأداء قبل موته ولهذا قال أصحابنا : إنه لا يكره لمن عليه قضاء رمضان أن يصوم متطوعاً ولو كان الوجوب على الفور يكره له التطوع قبل القضاء لأنه يكره له تأخير الواجب عن وقته المضيق، ولهذا إذا أخر قضاء رمضان حتى دخل آخر فلا فدية عليه لكونها تجب خلفاً عن الصوم عند العجز ولم يوجد لقدرته على القضاء ولهذا قال: (فإذا جاء رمضان آخر قدم الأداء على القضاء لأنه في وقته وهو لا يقبل غيره ويصوم القضاء بعده ، وهذا بخلاف قضاء الصلوات فإنها على الفور ولا يباح التأخير إلا بعذر. ذكره بذلك، وحينئذ فلا مانع من التوفيق بما مر والله تعالى أعلم. وبه يندفع ما في حاشية مسكين عن الأقصر أي من أن مرادهم بالقتل قتل الصيد لا قتل
When the next Ramadan comes, the performance of the current fast is given precedence over making up missed ones. And for the pregnant and nursing woman if they fear for the child or themselves, one of them fasts (?) and he pays for each day half a sa' because it is fard according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. As in Ghayat al-Bayan. And each prayer is considered equivalent to a day's fast according to the sound opinion. And that all other rights of Allah are likewise, whether financial or physical: whether a pure act of worship or one with the meaning of expense like sadaqat al-fitr, or its opposite like 'ushr, or pure expense like alimony, or with the meaning of punishment like kaffarat. And that the guardian does not fast nor pray on his behalf due to the hadith of al-Nasa'i: 'No one fasts for anyone, and no one prays for anyone.' And we restricted it to those who have survived a number of other days; if they die before that, they are not obliged to make a will as we have presented, but if they do make a will, it is valid because its validity does not depend on obligation. As in al-Bada'i'. And he also indicated that if one obligated i'tikaf upon himself and then died, food is given on his behalf for each day half a sa' of wheat because there is despair of [his performing] it; feeding is like fasting in prayer. As mentioned by al-Walwaliji in his fatawa. So the result is that whatever is a physical act of worship, the executor feeds on his behalf after his death for every obligation like sadaqat al-fitr. Whatever is a financial act of worship like zakat, he pays the amount due from his estate. Whatever is composed of both like hajj, a man performs hajj on his behalf from the deceased's wealth. ... His statement: 'And he makes up what he can without the condition of continuity' meaning continuity is not required in making up fasts because of the generality of His statement: 'a number of other days' [2:184]. And in the reading of Ubayy: 'a number of other days consecutive' is not well-known, so we do not add it, unlike the reading of Ibn Mas'ud regarding kaffarah of an oath because it is well-known, so we add it. As in al-Nihaya and al-Kafi. But consecutive is recommended. And his unrestricted statement indicates that making up is on delay (tarkhi), because the command is absolute and it is on delay as known in usul. The meaning of delay is that the first time is not specified for the action; whenever he begins it he complies and there is no sin for delay. But the obligation becomes urgent at the end of his life when he can perform it before death. For this reason our companions said: it is not disliked for one who has to make up Ramadan fasts to fast voluntarily. If the obligation were immediate, it would be disliked to volunteer before making up because it would entail delaying the obligatory beyond its immediate time. For this reason, if one delays making up Ramadan until the next Ramadan, there is no fidya on him because fidya is a substitute for fasting when unable, and here he is able to make up. That is why he said: 'When the next Ramadan comes, he gives precedence to the performance over making up because its time is present and it accepts no substitute, and he fasts the make-up after it.' This differs from making up prayers which are immediate and delay is not permitted except for an excuse. Mentioned by al-Walwaliji. His statement: 'And for the pregnant and nursing woman if they fear for the child or themselves' means they may break the fast to avoid hardship, and because of his statement: 'Indeed Allah has lifted from the traveler fasting and half of prayer, and from the pregnant and nursing woman two-thirds of what he left.' So it is textually established the permissibility of bequeathing that. Therefore there is no obstacle to reconciling it with what has passed. And Allah knows best. And by this the objection in the margin of Miskin from al-Aqsar is refuted, namely that their intent by 'killing' is killing game, not killing.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for making up missed Ramadan fasts. The author explains that when a new Ramadan arrives, one must perform the current fast first even if qada is pending, because the current fast is time-specific. Qada is not required to be consecutive; it can be delayed because the Quranic verse 'a number of other days' is absolute. However, it is recommended to be consecutive. The qada is on delay (tarkhi), meaning no immediate sin for postponing, but it becomes urgent near the end of life. Interestingly, one may volunteer fasts before completing qada, unlike if qada were immediate. If one delays qada until the next Ramadan, no fidya is required because fidya is only for permanent inability. For pregnant and nursing women, if they fear harm to themselves or the child, they may break the fast and must give fidya (half sa' per day) according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The author also distinguishes between different types of worship: physical acts (like fasting) require feeding after death through the executor; financial acts (like zakat) are taken directly from the estate; combined acts (like hajj) are performed by proxy. A hadith from al-Nasa'i is cited: 'No one fasts for anyone and no one prays for anyone,' so the guardian cannot fast or pray on behalf of the deceased. The passage concludes by reconciling apparent contradictions regarding bequests for fidya.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Making up missed obligatory fasts after Ramadan.
تراخي (tarakhi): The legal principle that an obligation is not immediately due but may be performed later.
فدية (fidya): Compensation for missed fast when unable to fast, usually feeding a poor person per day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone delays making up Ramadan fasts until the next Ramadan arrives, must they pay fidya?
A) Yes, fidya is wajib for each day delayed.
B) No, fidya is only for those permanently unable to fast.
C) Yes, but only if they delayed without excuse.
D) No, because the current Ramadan fast has precedence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that qada of Ramadan is on tarkhi (delay) and does not require consecutiveness; pregnant/nursing women may break fast for fear of harm and must pay fidya.
