Fidya for the Elderly: Rulings and Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Explains fidya rulings and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on the amount of fidya for the elderly and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Fidya is a substitute for fasting; the relied-upon view is half a sa' per day per Abu Yusuf.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author explores the rulings of fidya for those who are permanently unable to fast, such as the elderly. The key principle is that fasting here acts as a substitute (badal) for feeding the poor, so fidya is only allowed when one cannot expiate with wealth. The passage also presents a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute on the required amount per day.

Guided Path
1
1. Understand the principle of badal
Recognise that the passage starts by stating that fasting in this context is a substitute for feeding the poor. This principle governs the entire discussion: fidya is only valid when the original expiation (feeding) is impossible.
Badal means substitute; it has specific rules.
2
2. Identify the ruling on fidya for the elderly
The passage explicitly states that the decrepit old man may give fidya (monetary compensation) for each missed fast. It also notes that this can be given all at once at the beginning or end of Ramadan.
Timing flexibility is allowed.
3
3. Compare the two Hanafi positions on the amount
Abu Hanifa required one full sa' per day, analogising to the expiation for an oath. Abu Yusuf allowed half a sa' per day. The passage quotes Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad adopting Abu Yusuf's view as the relied-upon position.
The text says 'wa bihi na'khudh' meaning 'we take this'.
4
4. Recognise the scope of the dispute
The dispute is limited to the amount; both imams agree that fidya is wajib. The passage also notes a narration allowing one sa' for two days under Abu Yusuf, indicating two narrations.
The mufta bihi is half a sa' per day.
5
5. Connect the analogy with kafarat al-yamin
Abu Hanifa compares fidya to the expiation for an oath (kafarat al-yamin), where a full sa' is required. The passage rejects this analogy implicitly by endorsing Abu Yusuf's view.
The qiyas is clear: Abu Hanifa uses qiyas with the oath expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم فصل في العوارض تجوز له الفدية لأن الصوم هنا بدل عن غيره ولذا لا يجوز المصير إلى الصوم إلا عند العجز عما يكفر به من المال. كذا في فتح القدير وفي فتاوى قاضيخان وغاية البيان: وكذا لو حلق رأسه وهو محرم عن أذى ولم يجد نسكاً يذبحه ولا ثلاثة آصع حنطة يفرقها على ستة مساكين وهو فان لا يستطيع الصيام فأطعم عن الصيام لم يجز لأنه بدل. وفي القنية : ولو تصدق الشيخ الفاني بالليل عن صوم الفدية يجزئه . وفي فتاوى أبي حفص الكبير : إن شاء أعطى الفدية في أول رمضان بمرة وإن شاء أعطاها في آخره بمرة. وعن أبي يوسف: لو أعطى نصف صاع من بر عن يوم واحد لمساكين يجوز . قال الحسن : وبه نأخذ وإن أعطى مسكيناً صاعاً عن يومين فعن أبي يوسف روايتان. وعند أبي حنيفة لا يجزئه كالإطعام في كفارة اليمين. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية : استشهاداً لكون البدل لا بدل له. وذكر الصدر الشهيد رأسه مجروحاً فربط الجبيرة لم يجب عليه أن يمسح لأنَّ المسح هنا أصل منصوص عليه لا بدل عن غيره ا هـ .
The book of fasting, chapter on excuses. Fidya is permissible for him because fasting here is a substitute for something else, so it is not permissible to resort to fasting except when unable to expiate with wealth. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and in Fatawa Qadikhan and Ghayat al-Bayan: Similarly, if he shaved his head while in ihram due to harm and did not find a sacrificial animal nor three sa's of wheat to distribute to six poor people, and he is a decrepit old man who cannot fast, so he fed others instead of fasting, it is not permissible because it is a substitute. In al-Qunyah: If the decrepit old man gives charity at night for the fidya of a day's fast, it suffices him. In Fatawa Abi Hafs al-Kabir: If he wishes, he may give the fidya at the beginning of Ramadan at once, or at the end at once. From Abu Yusuf: If he gives half a sa' of wheat for one day to poor people, it is permissible. Al-Hasan said: And with this we take. And if he gives a poor person a sa' for two days, from Abu Yusuf there are two narrations. According to Abu Hanifa, it does not suffice him, like feeding in the expiation of an oath. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah: as a proof that a substitute does not have a substitute. And al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned that if his head is wounded and he ties a bandage, he is not obligated to wipe over it because wiping here is an original textual ruling, not a substitute for something else.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on fidya (monetary compensation) for those who are permanently unable to fast, such as the elderly. The key principle: since fasting here acts as a substitute for feeding the poor, it is only permitted when one cannot expiate with wealth. The passage then gives examples and disagreements within the Hanafi school. Abu Yusuf permits giving half a sa' of wheat per day, while Abu Hanifa requires a full sa' per day (by analogy with the expiation of an oath). The relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's view, as stated by Hasan ibn Ziyad. The final analogy with wiping over a bandage reinforces that a primary ruling (here, the original obligation) cannot be replaced by a substitute if the substitute itself has no substitute.

Key Terms
الفدية (al-fidyah): A monetary or food compensation given by a person unable to fast permanently, in place of each missed fast.
البدل (al-badal): A substitute that takes the place of the original obligation when the original cannot be performed.
صاع (sa'): A unit of volume, approximately 2.5 kg of wheat (but varies; Hanafis consider it about 3.5 kg). Half a sa' is about 1.75 kg.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, what is the minimum amount of fidya for one missed fast?
A) One full sa' of wheat
B) Half a sa' of wheat
C) Two mudd of dates
D) Feeding ten poor people

Key Takeaway
Fidya is wajib on those permanently unable to fast; the mufta bihi amount is half a sa' per day.

Breaking a Voluntary Fast Without Excuse: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-madhhab dispute with practical applications on excuses and permissions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on breaking a voluntary fast without excuse, explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Yusuf and the apparent narration, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to scenarios involving hospitality and oaths.

Core Idea
A voluntary faster may break his fast without excuse but must make it up if the fast was intentional.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a detailed discussion on voluntary fasting. A key point is whether a voluntary faster may break his fast without an excuse. The Hanafi school has two narrations: the apparent narration prohibits it, while Abu Yusuf permits it, with the latter being the preferred view. The passage also explores hospitality as an excuse, the effect of oaths, and the permissions required for slaves, women, and employees. By understanding this dispute and its resolution, you will grasp how the school handles intra-madhhab ikhtilaf and applies principles like tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What is the ruling on breaking a voluntary fast without an excuse? The passage presents two narrations: the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) prohibits it, while Abu Yusuf permits it.
The preferred view is Abu Yusuf's.
2
Understand the two positions
According to the apparent narration, a voluntary faster may only break with an excuse. Abu Yusuf says he may break even without an excuse, but must make up the fast if it was intentional (qasdi).
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Examine the tarjih
The author prefers Abu Yusuf's view because 'the evidences support it'. Scholars like al-Marghinani in Fath al-Qadir adopted this as mufta bihi.
The later school relies on Abu Yusuf's narration.
4
Apply to excuses: hospitality
The passage discusses whether hospitality (diyafah) is a valid excuse. The strongest view is that it depends on the host's feelings and the guest's ability to make up the fast.
Multiple opinions within the school.
5
Extend to permissions and oaths
The passage also covers oaths (if someone swears by divorce for a voluntary faster to break, he should break to avoid sin) and who needs permission to fast voluntary (slaves, employees, women under conditions).
These are practical applications of the general rule.

ومن له الحق له أن يفطره وفي الولوالجية : وابنة الرجل وقرابته تتطوع بدون إذنه لأنه لا يفوّت حقه ا هـ . وقيد في المحيط والولوالجية كراهة صوم المرأة بأن يضر بالزوج، أما إذا كان لا يضره بأن كان صائماً أو مريضاً فلها أن تصوم وليس له منعها لأنه ليس فيه إبطال حقه بخلاف العبد والمدبر وأم الولد والأمة فإنه ليس لهم الصوم بغير إذن المولى وإن لم يضر به لأنه منافعهم مملوكة للمولى بخلاف المرأة فإن منافعها غير مملوكة للزوج وإنما له حق الاستمتاع بها وتقضي المرأة إذا أذن لها الزوج أو بانت منه، ويقضي العبد إذا أذن له المولى أو أعتق. وقيد كراهة صوم الأجير أيضاً بكون الصوم يضر بالمستأجر في الخدمة فإن كان لا يضر فله أن يصوم بغير إذنه ا هـ . وفي البزازية قالوا : يباح الفطر لأجل المرأة أي لا يمنع صوم النفل صحة الخلوة. وفي النظم الأفضل أن يفطر للضيافة ولا يقول أنا صائم لئلا يقف على سره أحد. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : لا يصوم المملوك تطوعاً إلا بإذن المولى إلا إذا كان غائباً ولا ضرر له في ذلك اهـ. وهو خلاف ما في المحيط. وإن أحرمت المرأة تطوعاً بغير إذن الزوج قالوا له أن يحللها. والأجير إذا كان يضره الخدمة وكذا في الصلوات. كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان فالحاصل أن الصوم والحج والصلاة سواء، هذا كله إطلاق ما في الظهيرية في المرأة والعبد لأن الصوم يضر ببدن المرأة ويهزل وإن لم يكن الزوج الآن يطؤها والعبد منافعه مملوكة للمولى فليس له الصوم مطلقاً. قوله : (فإذا كان قبل الزوال صار شارعاً المراد به قبل الضحوة الكبرى ومفهومه أنه إذا كان بعد الزوال أي بعد نصف النهار لا يجب عليه القضاء إذا قطعه سواء قطعه في الحال أو بعد ساعة، ظاهر . قاله بعض الفضلاء . قوله : (والأظهر من هذا كله الخ قال في النهر : وعندي أن إحالة المنع على الضرر وعدمه على
His statement: 'And for the voluntary faster without an excuse, according to one narration, he breaks and makes up.' That is, he may break his fast with or without an excuse, and if he breaks he makes it up if it was an intentional voluntary fast. This narration is from Abu Yusuf, while the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) is that he may not break except due to an excuse. He (the author) corrected this in al-Muhit. He only confined himself to this narration because it is stronger in terms of evidence, and for this reason the investigator in Fath al-Qadir chose it and said: The evidences support it and it is more proper. Then the scholars differed regarding the apparent narration: Is hospitality an excuse or not? It was said yes, it was said no, and it was said it is an excuse before noon but not after, except if not breaking after noon would cause disobedience to one's parents and no one else, such that even if a man swore by triple divorce that he should break, he would not break. And it was said: If the host is satisfied with mere presence even if he does not eat, breaking is not permitted, and if he is hurt by that, he breaks. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And not anything as you see. In al-Kafi: the most apparent is that it is an excuse, and Qadikhan corrected in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir that hospitality is an excuse. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah they said: The correct position in the school is that he looks at this: if the host is one who is satisfied with mere presence and is not hurt by the breaking of the fast, he does not break. Shams al-Aimmah al-Hulwani said: The best that has been said in this chapter is that if he trusts himself to make it up, he breaks to ward off harm from his Muslim brother, and if he does not trust, he does not break, even if not breaking harms his Muslim brother. And regarding the issue of an oath, the answer must be based on this detail. In another place: If he is fasting a make-up for Ramadan, it is disliked for him to break because it has the ruling of Ramadan. For this reason, if a man swore by divorce that he should break, he does not break. In al-Nihayah: the most apparent is that hospitality is an excuse. In al-Bazzaziyyah: If he swore by divorcing his wife that he must break, if it is a voluntary fast he breaks, if it is a make-up he does not, and the reliance is that he breaks in both and does not break his oath. And if we say that hospitality is an excuse in voluntary fasts, it is an excuse for both the guest and the host. Thus in Sharh al-Wiqayah. And he made absolute regarding the make-up of a voluntary fast that it includes if his breaking was intentional or not, such as the occurrence of menstruation for a woman fasting voluntarily in the most correct of the two narrations. Thus in al-Nihyah. And we restricted the voluntary fast to being intentional because if he began thinking it was obligatory and then learned it is not, he is a voluntary faster; the best is to complete it, and if he breaks there is no make-up upon him. Thus in al-Muhit and others. The author of al-Hidayah in al-Tajnīs restricted it that no hour should pass from the time it becomes clear that nothing is upon him; if an hour passes and then he breaks, he must make it up because after an hour passes it is as if he intended during that hour. So if it is before noon, he becomes a performer of voluntary fast, so it becomes obligatory upon him. Then he said: If he intends the make-up fast after dawn so that his intention for make-up does not correct, he becomes a faster, and if he breaks the make-up is required, just as if he originally intended a voluntary fast. And this refutes an issue concerning the case of presumption. It has been discussed earlier at his statement 'And what remains is not valid except with a specific intention.' In al-Bada'i: If he begins the expiatory fast and then becomes wealthy during it, there is no make-up upon him. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah: It is disliked for a slave, an employee, or a woman to voluntarily fast unless the one who has a right over them permits; and the one who has the right may break it. In al-Walwaljiyyah: The daughter of a man and his relatives may voluntarily fast without his permission because it does not cause loss to his right. In al-Muhit and al-Walwaljiyyah, the dislike for a woman's fast is conditioned on it harming her husband; if it does not harm him, e.g., he is fasting or sick, she may fast and he cannot prevent her because it does not nullify his right. This is unlike a male slave, a mudabbar, an umm walad, and a female slave: they may not fast without the permission of the owner even if it does not harm him, because their benefits are owned by the master. Unlike a woman, whose benefits are not owned by her husband, he only has the right to enjoy her. A woman makes it up if her husband permits or she separates from him, and a slave makes it up if his master permits or he is freed. The dislike of fasting for an employee is also conditioned on the fast harming the employer in service; if it does not harm, he may fast without permission. In al-Bazzaziyyah they said: Breaking is permitted for the sake of the woman, i.e., voluntary fasting does not prevent the validity of seclusion. In al-Nuzum: It is better to break for hospitality and not say 'I am fasting' so that no one discovers his secret. In Fatawa Qadikhan: A slave does not fast voluntarily except with the master's permission, unless he is absent and there is no harm in it. This contradicts what is in al-Muhit. If a woman enters ihram voluntarily without the husband's permission, they said the husband may annul it. Likewise for an employee if fasting harms his service. Thus in Fatawa Qadikhan. The conclusion is that fasting, Hajj, and prayer are the same. All this is the absolute statement in al-Zahiriyyah regarding women and slaves because fasting harms a woman's body and makes her thin, even if the husband is not currently having intercourse with her, and the slave's benefits are owned by the master, so he may not fast at all. His statement: 'If it was before noon he becomes a performer (of voluntary fast)': what is meant is before the major forenoon (al-dahwa al-kubra). Its implication is that if it is after noon, i.e., after midday, he is not obligated to make it up if he breaks it, whether he breaks it immediately or after an hour, apparently. This was said by some scholars. His statement: 'And the most apparent of all this...' He said in al-Nahr: In my view, referring the prohibition to harm and its absence upon...
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on voluntary fasting (salat al-tatawwu'). It begins by presenting the two narrations within the Hanafi school: according to Abu Yusuf, a voluntary faster may break his fast even without an excuse, but must make it up if the fast was intentional; according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), he may only break with an excuse. The author indicates that the preferred position is Abu Yusuf's, as it is supported by evidence. The passage then covers several subtopics: (1) whether hospitality (diyafah) is an excuse to break a voluntary fast, with multiple opinions; the strongest view is that it depends on the host's feelings and the guest's ability to make up the fast. (2) The case of oaths: if someone swears by divorce for a voluntary faster to break, the faster should break to avoid sin. (3) The ruling for a woman who menstruates during a voluntary fast: according to the most correct narration, she breaks and must make up. (4) The condition of intention (qasd): if a person starts fasting thinking it is obligatory (e.g., Ramadan) and then learns it is not, the ruling depends on whether time has passed. If no hour has passed, no make-up; if an hour passes before noon, the fast becomes voluntary and must be completed or made up if broken. (5) The expiatory fast (kaffarah) and becoming wealthy: no make-up required. (6) The permission required for slaves, employees, and women to fast voluntarily: they should not fast without permission if it harms the rights of the owner, husband, or employer. The passage ends with commentary clarifications about the timing of becoming a performer of voluntary fast (before the major forenoon) and a cross-reference to another discussion.

Key Terms
التطوع (al-tatawwu'): A voluntary act of worship that is not obligatory; here specifically voluntary fasting beyond the prescribed fasts.
الضيافة (al-diyafah): Hospitality; being a guest invited to eat. The text discusses whether it is a valid excuse to break a voluntary fast.
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up a missed fast; required when a voluntary fast is broken intentionally if it was a qasdi (intentional) voluntary fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a voluntary faster breaks his fast intentionally without an excuse?
A) He is sinful and must make up the fast
B) He is not sinful but must make up the fast if it was an intentional voluntary fast
C) He is not sinful and no make-up is required
D) He must fast two consecutive months

Key Takeaway
A voluntary faster may break his fast without excuse according to the preferred Hanafi view, but must make up if the fast was intentional; hospitality can be a valid excuse depending on circumstances.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Voluntary Fasting of Women, Slaves, and Employees
Rulings on permission and harm for voluntary fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for voluntary fasting of women, slaves, and employees and distinguish between cases of harm and no harm.

Core Idea
A voluntary fast that harms another's right is makruh; permission matters, but the basis is harm.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses when voluntary fasting is disliked for women, slaves, and employees. The key is balancing personal devotion with the rights of others. The rulings are nuanced: harm to the husband, ownership of benefits, and service to the employer all affect the legal classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
The passage treats three distinct categories: women (wife), slaves (including mudabbar, umm walad, amah), and employees (ajir). Each has a different relationship with the one who has a right over them.
A man's daughter or relative is treated differently—no permission needed.
2
Determine the condition of harm
For women, the dislike (karahah) is conditional upon the fast causing harm to the husband. If the husband is fasting or sick, no harm, so the fast is permissible. For employees, the condition is harm to the employer's service. For slaves, there is no condition—even without harm, the fast is not permissible without permission.
The husband's right is limited to enjoyment; the slave's benefits are owned by the master.
3
Note the differences in permission
A woman may fast without permission if no harm; a slave may not fast at all without the master's permission, even if no harm. An employee may fast if no harm to service. The one with the right can also make the person break the fast.
There is a difference within Hanafi sources: al-Muhit allows a slave to fast if no harm; Fatawa Qadikhan prohibits absolutely.
4
Understand the scope of the right
The husband can prevent only voluntary acts (tatawwu), not obligatory ones like qada of Ramadan. Similarly for slaves, except kaffarah of zihar due to the wife's right.
The passage also notes that breaking a fast for hospitality is recommended to avoid showing off.

أسلم كافر أمسك يومه ولم يقض شيئاً ولو نوى المسافر الإفطار ثم قدم ونوى الصوم . بغير إذنه. ولو كان المولى غائباً فإنه لم يكن مبقي على أصل الحرية في العبادات إلا في الفرائض، وأما في النوافل فلا وفي القنية : وللزوج أن يمنع زوجته عن كل ما كان الإيجاب من جهتها كالتطوع والنذر واليمين دون ما كان من جهته تعالى كقضاء رمضان، وكذا العبد إلا إذا ظاهر من امرأته لا يمنعه من كفارة الظهار بالصوم لتعلق حق المرأة به. ثم أن اعلم افساد الصوم أو الصلاة بعد الشروع فيها مكروه - نص عليه في غاية البيان - وليس بحرام لأن الدليل ليس قطعي الدلالة كما أوضحه في فتح القدير.

And we restricted the voluntary to being intentional because if he started thinking it was obligatory then knew there is nothing on him, he is a volunteer, and it is best to complete it, and if he breaks the fast there is no qada on him. So in al-Muhit and others. And the author of al-Hidayah in al-Tajnis restricted it that not even an hour passes from the time it becomes clear that there is nothing on him; if an hour passes then he breaks the fast, qada is due on him because when an hour passed, it became as if he intended in that hour. So if it is before zawal, he becomes a starter of a voluntary fast and it becomes obligatory on him. Then he said: If he intends the fast for qada after dawn so that his intention for qada does not become valid, he becomes a faster, and if he breaks the fast, qada is binding on him as if he initially intended voluntary. And this presents an objection to the issue of conjecture. End. And the discussion on it has preceded at his saying: 'And what remains is not valid except with a specific intention.' In al-Bada'i: If he starts a kaffarah fast then becomes wealthy in the middle, no qada on him. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah: It is disliked for a slave, an employee, or a woman to volunteer fasting unless the one who has a right over them permits, and the one with the right has the right to make him break it. In al-Walwaljiyyah: A man's daughter and his relative may volunteer without his permission because it does not cause loss of his right. End. And in al-Muhit and al-Walwaljiyyah, the dislike of a woman's fast is restricted to when it harms the husband; but if it does not harm him, such as if he is himself fasting or sick, she may fast and he cannot prevent her because it does not nullify his right, unlike a slave, a mudabbar, a umm walad, and an amah: they may not fast without the master's permission even if it does not harm him because their benefits are owned by the master, unlike the woman whose benefits are not owned by the husband; he only has the right to enjoyment. And the woman makes up [the fast] if her husband permits or she separates, and the slave makes up if his master permits or he is freed. And the dislike of an employee's fast is also restricted to when the fast harms the employer in service; if it does not harm, he may fast without his permission. End. In al-Bazaziyyah they said: Breaking the fast for the sake of the woman is permissible, i.e., a voluntary fast does not prevent a valid seclusion. In al-Nazm: It is better to break the fast for hospitality and not say 'I am fasting' so that no one knows his secret. In Fatawa Qadikhan: A slave may not [fast] voluntarily except with the master's permission, unless the master is absent and there is no harm in it. End. And that contradicts what is in al-Muhit. If a woman starts ihram voluntarily without the husband's permission, they said he may make her break it. And the employee if service harms him, and similarly in prayers. So in Fatawa Qadikhan. The conclusion is that fasting, hajj, and prayer are the same. All this is from the absoluteness of what is in al-Zahiriyyah regarding the woman and the slave, because fasting harms the woman's body, and the more apparent is that it weakens her even if the husband is not now having intercourse with her, and the slave's benefits are owned by the master, so he may not fast at all. His saying: 'If it is before zawal he becomes (starter)' — the intended meaning is before the greater forenoon, and its understood meaning is that if it is after zawal, i.e., after midday, qada is not obligatory on him if he breaks it, whether he breaks it immediately or after an hour. This is apparent. Said by some virtuous ones. And his saying: 'And the more apparent from all this, etc.' He said in al-Nahr: In my view, referring prohibition to harm or its absence on A disbeliever who became Muslim abstains on his day and does not make up anything even if the traveller intended to break then arrived and intended fast. Without his permission. If the master is absent, he is not remaining on the original freedom in acts of worship except in obligations; as for supererogatory acts, no. In al-Qunyah: The husband may prevent his wife from everything that is obligatory from her side, like voluntary, vow, and oath, but not from what is from Allah's side like making up Ramadan; similarly the slave except if he has declared  zihar from his wife, he is not prevented from the kaffarah of zihar by fasting because of the right of the woman attached to it. Then know that spoiling a fast or prayer after starting it is disliked — stated in Ghayat al-Bayan — and not forbidden because the evidence is not definitive in indication, as explained in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings regarding voluntary fasting for individuals who have obligations toward others—specifically women, slaves, and employees. The author begins by discussing a scenario where a person starts a fast thinking it is obligatory (e.g., due to some conjecture) then discovers it is not. According to the most sound narration, if a woman intending a voluntary fast begins her fast and then menstruates, the ruling is that her fast is disrupted. The text then clarifies that a voluntary fast is defined as intentional; if someone starts fasting under a mistaken belief of obligation, they become a volunteer. It is best to complete the fast, and if they break it, no qada is required. However, Imam al-Hidayah in al-Tajnis adds a condition: if an hour passes after realizing there is no obligation, then breaking the fast necessitates qada, because passing an hour makes the intention effective. The discussion distinguishes between before and after zawal (midday). If the realization occurs before zawal, the person is considered to have started a voluntary fast and it becomes binding; if after zawal, breaking does not require qada. The text then addresses cases of kaffarah fasts and financial ease, quoting al-Bada'i that no qada is due if one becomes wealthy during a kaffarah fast. The central ruling from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah states that it is disliked (makruh) for a slave, an employee, or a woman to observe a voluntary fast without the permission of the one who has a right over them (master, employer, or husband). The one with the right even has the authority to invalidate the fast. The dislike for a woman is conditional upon causing harm to the husband; if the husband is himself fasting or ill, and thus not harmed, she may fast without permission. This is because the husband's right is only to enjoyment, not ownership of her benefits, unlike a slave whose benefits are owned by the master. Therefore, a slave may not fast even without harm. For the employee, the dislike depends on whether fasting harms the employer's service. The passage also notes that a woman's voluntary fast does not prevent valid seclusion (khalwa) with her husband, and it is better to break a fast for hospitality to avoid pride. The text compares fasting, hajj, and prayer, and concludes that the prohibition is based on harm. The latter part clarifies the meaning of 'before zawal' as before the greater forenoon (approximately mid-morning), and that after zawal (midday) breaking does not require qada. The passage then continues with rulings on a disbeliever who becomes Muslim or a child who reaches puberty during the day, but that is a separate topic. Overall, this section provides detailed guidance on when voluntary fasting is permissible or disliked for individuals whose actions affect others' rights, emphasizing the balance between personal devotion and the rights of spouses, masters, and employers.

Key Terms
الضحوة الكبرى (al-dahwah al-kubra): The greater forenoon, typically around mid-morning, approximately halfway between sunrise and noon, used to define the time before which a fast becomes binding upon realization in voluntary fasts.
صوم التطوع (sawm al-tatawwu'): Voluntary fasting, supererogatory fasts that are not obligatory, such as fasting on Mondays and Thursdays. In this text, it refers to any fast that is not obligatory (e.g., not Ramadan, not qada, not kaffarah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling for a slave who voluntarily fasts without the master's permission even if the fast does not harm the master?
A) Permissible because no harm.
B) Makruh tanzih, same as a woman.
C) Not permissible because the slave's benefits are owned by the master.
D) Haram and the fast is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Voluntary fasting for women, slaves, and employees is assessed based on harm to the other's right, with slaves being an exception where permission is always required.

Ruling on One Who Becomes Mukallaf During the Day (Fasting)
Ruling and its proof with intra-Hanafi distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a child who reaches puberty or a disbeliever who embraces Islam during the day of Ramadan, and explain why no qada is required.

Core Idea
No qada if obligation was absent at the start of the day; only abstention out of respect.

This lesson examines a specific case: what happens if a child reaches puberty or a non-Muslim embraces Islam during the day in Ramadan? The Hanafi school holds that such a person must abstain from eating and drinking for the rest of the day, but is not required to make up that day later. This ruling is based on the principle that the obligation of fasting starts at the beginning of the day, and if the person was not mukallaf at that time, the obligation never fully applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling from the matn
The base text states: 'If a child reaches puberty or a disbeliever becomes Muslim, he abstains on his day and does not make up anything.' Note the two parts: imsaak (abstention) and no qada.
2
Understand the reason for no qada
The obligation of fasting requires that the person be mukallaf at the beginning of the day. Since they were not, the obligation never arose. Thus, no qada is needed.
This distinguishes the case from one who breaks a fast without excuse—they must make up.
3
Recognize the evidence from the hadith of Ashura
The Prophet commanded those who ate on Ashura (when it was obligatory) to abstain for the rest of the day. This proves that such abstention is obligatory (wajib) in analogous situations.
The hadith is the basis for the ruling, though it is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut).
4
Differentiate between fasting and prayer
If one becomes mukallaf during prayer time, he must pray that prayer. This is because the cause (sabab) in prayer is the part of time connected to performance, while in fasting it is the very first part of the day.
This distinction, based on Zufar's qiyas, explains why qada is required for prayer but not for fasting in this scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو بلغ أو أسلم كافر أمسك يومه ولم يقض شيئاً فالإمساك قضاء لحق الوقت بالتشبه وعدم القضاء لعدم وجوب الصوم عليهما فيه وأطلق الإمساك ولم يبين صفته للاختلاف فيه، والأصح الوجوب لموافقته للدليل وهو ما ثبت من أمره عليه الصلاة والسلام بالإمساك لمن أكل في يوم عاشوراء حين كان واجباً. وأطلق في عدم القضاء فشمل ما إذا أفطرا في ذلك اليوم أو صاماه، وسواء كان قبل الزوال أو بعده، لأن الصوم لا يتجزى وجوباً كما لا يتجزى آداء، وأهلية الوجوب منعدمة في أوله فلا يجب. وقيد بالصوم لأنه لو بلغ أو أسلم في أثناء وقت الصلاة أو في آخره وجبت عليه اتفاقاً وهو قياس زفر، وفرق بین الصوم والصلاة بأن السبب في الصلاة الجزء المتصل بالأداء فوجدت الأهلية عنده، وفي الصوم الجزء الأول هو السبب والأهلية معدومة عنده قال في فتح القدير: وعلى هذا فقولهم في الأصول الواجب المؤقت قد يكون الوقت فيه سبباً للمؤدى وظرفاً له كوقت الصلاة أو سبباً ومعياراً وهو ما يقع فيه مقدار به کوقت الصوم تساهل، إذ يقتضي أن السبب تمام الوقت فيهما وقد بان خلافه، ثم على ما بان من تحقيق المراد قد يقال : يلزم أن لا يجب الإمساك في نفس الجزء الأول من اليوم لأنه هو السبب للوجوب والإلزام سبق الوجوب على السبب للزوم تقدم السبب فالإيجاب فيه يستدعي سبباً سابقاً والفرض خلافه، ولو لم يستلزم ذلك لزم كون ما ذكروه في وقت الصلوات من أن السببية تضاف إلى الجزء الأول فإن لم يؤد عقيبه انتقلت إلى ما يلي ابتداء الشروع فإن لم يشرع إلى الجزء الأخير تقررت السببية فيه واعتبر حال المكلف عنده تكلف مستغنى عنه إذ لا داعي لجعله ما يليه دون ما يقع فيه ا هـ . وقد يقال : إن قولهم يقتضي أن السبب تمام الوقت مسلم لو سكتوا وهم قد صرحوا بأنه لا يمكن جعل كل الوقت سبباً في الصلاة، وذكروا أن السببية تنتقل من جزء إلى جزء. وقوله ثم على ما بان إلى آخره فيه بحث، أما على اختيار شمس الأئمة السرخسي من أن السببية لليالي والأيام فقد وجد السبب بالليلة فالإمساك إنما وجب في الجزء الأول باعتبار سبق عدمه أولى للقطع بأن صوم يوم لا يهز لها فلم يبق إلا منعه عن وطئها وذلك إضرار به، فإن انتفي بأن كان مريضاً أو مسافراً جاز .
His saying: (And if a child reaches puberty or a disbeliever becomes Muslim, he abstains on his day and does not make up anything.) So the abstention fulfills the right of the time by imitation, and the absence of qada is due to the fact that fasting was not obligatory upon them in it. And he left the description of the abstention unrestricted due to difference of opinion on it, and the most correct is that it is obligatory because it agrees with the evidence, which is established from his command (peace be upon him) to abstain for the one who ate on the day of Ashura when it was obligatory. And he left unrestricted in the absence of qada, so it includes if they broke the fast that day or observed it, and whether before zawal or after, because fasting is not divisible in obligation just as it is not divisible in performance, and the capacity for obligation is absent at its beginning, so it is not obligatory. And he restricted it to fasting because if he reaches puberty or becomes Muslim during the time of prayer or at its end, it becomes obligatory upon him by agreement, and this is the qiyas of Zufar. And he differentiated between fasting and prayer because the cause in prayer is the part connected to performance, so capacity is found at it, while in fasting the first part is the cause and capacity is absent at it. He said in Fath al-Qadir: On this basis, their statement in usul that 'the time-bound obligatory act may have time as a cause for the performed act and as a container for it, like the time of prayer, or as a cause and a standard, which is what occurs in a measure, like the time of fasting' is an approximation, for it implies that the cause is the entire time in both, and the contrary has been shown. Then, based on what has been clarified of the intended meaning, it may be said: It would follow that abstention itself would not be obligatory in the first part of the day, because it is the cause for obligation, and the obligation would precede the cause due to the necessity of the cause preceding, so making it obligatory requires a prior cause, contrary to the assumption. And if that is not entailed, then it would necessitate what they mentioned about the time of prayers: that causation is attributed to the first part, and if one does not perform immediately after it, it transfers to the next beginning; and if one does not start until the last part, causation is established there and the state of the obligated person at that time is considered—an unnecessary artifice, as there is no need to make it what comes after rather than what occurs in it. End. And it may be said: Their statement implies that the cause is the entire time, which is conceded if they were silent, but they explicitly stated that it is not possible to make the entire time a cause in prayer, and they mentioned that causation transfers from part to part. And his saying 'then on what has been clarified' etc. contains discussion. As for the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi that causation is for nights and days, the cause is found through the night, so abstention is only obligatory in the first part based on the precedence of its absence. The first is more appropriate because it is definite that the fast of a day does not weaken her, so nothing remains except preventing him from intercourse with her, and that is harmful to him. If that harm is negated because he is sick or traveling, it becomes permissible.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning a child who reaches puberty (bulugh) or a disbeliever who embraces Islam during the day of Ramadan. The matn states: such a person must abstain from eating, drinking, etc. for the remainder of the day but does not have to make up (qada) that day. The commentary explains the reasoning: the abstention is to honor the time (by imitating those who are fasting), but qada is not required because the obligation of fasting did not apply to them at the beginning of the day. The text notes that there is a disagreement about whether the abstention is obligatory or recommended; the most correct view (al-asahh) is that it is obligatory, based on the Prophetic command to abstain on the day of Ashura when it was originally obligatory. The ruling applies whether they broke the fast during the day or observed it, and whether the event occurred before or after midday, because the obligation of fasting cannot be divided (it is not a part-day obligation) and the capacity for obligation was absent at the start. The text then distinguishes between fasting and prayer: if a child becomes mukallaf during prayer time, he must pray that prayer, because the cause of obligation in prayer is the part of time connected to performance, so capacity exists at that moment. This is the qiyas of Zufar (a Hanafi imam). The difference is that in prayer, the cause is the latter part of the time (the part when performance occurs), while in fasting, the cause is the very first part of the day, and capacity is absent then. The commentary then delves into the usul discussion about the nature of the cause (sabab) in time-bound obligations, citing Fath al-Qadir. It explains that for fasting, the cause is the entire day (or the first part), so if one becomes mukallaf after the day has started, the cause already existed without capacity. This is a subtle point about how causation works in time-bound acts. The passage ends with a reference to Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi's view that causation is for nights and days, so the night preceding the day is the cause. Overall, this section provides a clear ruling and the sophisticated legal reasoning behind it, including an intra-Hanafi comparison with prayer.

Key Terms
الإمساك (al-imsaak): Abstention from eating, drinking, and other fast-breakers from the time one learns of the obligation until the end of the day, though the fast is not counted as a full obligatory fast.
السبب (al-sabab): The cause of obligation; in fasting, the sabab is the first part of the day (the part when the obligation would normally begin), which is why if one becomes mukallaf later, the cause already occurred without capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the correct ruling for a disbeliever who embraces Islam at noon during Ramadan?
A) He must fast the rest of the day and make up that day later.
B) He must only abstain for the rest of the day with no qada.
C) He need not do anything because he missed the intention before dawn.
D) He must make up the entire day but is not required to abstain now.

Key Takeaway
When a person becomes mukallaf during the day in Ramadan, he must abstain for the remainder but does not need to make up that day, because the obligation was absent at the start.

Bulugh or Islam During the Day: Imsaak Without Qada
Commentary on ruling for bulugh/Islam mid-day

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a child who reaches puberty or a disbeliever who accepts Islam during the day of Ramadan, explain why imsaak is wajib but no qada is required, and distinguish between the cause of obligation in fasting and prayer.

Core Idea
No qada for mid-day bulugh/Islam because capacity was absent at dawn; imsaak is wajib out of respect for the time.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common scenario: a child reaches puberty or a person converts to Islam during the day in Ramadan. The ruling is clear—they must abstain for the remainder of the day, but no make-up is required. However, the basis for this ruling involves a deeper usuli discussion about the nature of cause (sabab) in fasting versus prayer. In this lesson, we will unpack the Hanafi position, its proof from the Ashura hadith, and the reasoning behind the distinction from prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The matn states: 'And if a child reaches puberty or a disbeliever becomes Muslim, he abstains for his day and does not make up anything.' Note the two parts: imsaak (abstinence) and no qada.
2
Understand why imsaak is wajib
The correct view (al-sahh) is that imsaak is obligatory (wajib), based on the command of the Prophet (ﷺ) on Ashura to abstain after eating. The principle 'al-amr yufidu al-wujub' applies, though it is not fard because the capacity for full obligation was absent at dawn.
Dalil: Ashura hadith (zanni thubut and dalala).
3
Grasp why no qada is required
Qada is not required because the cause of fasting obligation (the first part of the day) occurred when the person was not yet a mukallaf (ahliyyat al-wujub absent). Fasting is not divisible—obligation cannot attach mid-day. The person is only asked to respect the time by imsaak.
4
Compare with prayer to see the distinction in the cause
For prayer, if one becomes mukallaf mid-time, the full prayer is obligatory immediately because the cause (the time) is a container that attaches to performance. In fasting, the cause is the first part of the day, which is a standard (mi'yar), not a container. The cause must be present when capacity exists, but it wasn't.
This refutes Zufar's analogy (qiyas) from prayer to fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو بلغ أو أسلم كافر أمسك يومه ولم يقض شيئاً فالإمساك قضاء لحق الوقت بالتشبه وعدم القضاء لعدم وجوب الصوم عليهما فيه وأطلق الإمساك ولم يبين صفته للاختلاف فيه، والأصح الوجوب لموافقته للدليل وهو ما ثبت من أمره عليه الصلاة والسلام بالإمساك لمن أكل في يوم عاشوراء حين كان واجباً. وأطلق في عدم القضاء فشمل ما إذا أفطرا في ذلك اليوم أو صاماه، وسواء كان قبل الزوال أو بعده، لأن الصوم لا يتجزى وجوباً كما لا يتجزى آداء، وأهلية الوجوب منعدمة في أوله فلا يجب. وقيد بالصوم لأنه لو بلغ أو أسلم في أثناء وقت الصلاة أو في آخره وجبت عليه اتفاقاً وهو قياس زفر، وفرق بين الصوم والصلاة بأن السبب في الصلاة الجزء المتصل بالأداء فوجدت الأهلية عنده، وفي الصوم الجزء الأول هو السبب والأهلية معدومة عنده قال في فتح القدير: وعلى هذا فقولهم في الأصول الواجب المؤقت قد يكون الوقت فيه سبباً للمؤدى وظرفاً له كوقت الصلاة أو سبباً ومعياراً وهو ما يقع فيه مقدار به كوقت الصوم تساهل، إذ يقتضي أن السبب تمام الوقت فيهما وقد بان خلافه، ثم على ما بان من تحقيق المراد قد يقال : يلزم أن لا يجب الإمساك في نفس الجزء الأول من اليوم لأنه هو السبب للوجوب والإلزام سبق الوجوب على السبب للزوم تقدم السبب فالإيجاب فيه يستدعي سبباً سابقاً والفرض خلافه، ولو لم يستلزم ذلك لزم كون ما ذكروه في وقت الصلوات من أن السببية تضاف إلى الجزء الأول فإن لم يؤد عقيبه انتقلت إلى ما يلي ابتداء الشروع فإن لم يشرع إلى الجزء الأخير تقررت السببية فيه واعتبر حال المكلف عنده تكلف مستغنى عنه إذ لا داعي لجعله ما يليه دون ما يقع فيه ا هـ . وقد يقال : إن قولهم يقتضي أن السبب تمام الوقت مسلم لو سكتوا وهم قد صرحوا بأنه لا يمكن جعل كل الوقت سبباً في الصلاة، وذكروا أن السببية تنتقل من جزء إلى جزء. وقوله ثم على ما بان إلى آخره فيه بحث، أما على اختيار شمس الأئمة السرخسي من أن السببية لليالي والأيام فقد وجد السبب بالليلة فالإمساك إنما وجب في الجزء الأول باعتبار سبق السبب عليه وهو الليل، وأما على اختيار غيره من أن السببية خاصة بالأيام وأن الليالي لا دخل لها في السببية، فلأن لزوم تقدم السبب إنما هو عند الإمكان، أما عند عدم الإمكان فلا والصوم منه لأن وقته معيار له مقدر به يزيد بزيادته وينقص بنقصانه فلا يمكن أن يكون الجزء الأول خالياً عن الصوم ليكون سبباً متقدماً، ولا يمكن أن يكون ما قبله سبباً لعدم الصلاحية فلزم فيه مقارنة السبب للمسبب، وقد صرح بأن السبب في الصوم مقارن للمسبب صاحب كشف الأسرار شرح أصول فخر الأسلام البزدوي بخلاف وقت الصلاة فإنه ظرف فأمكن تقدم السبب على الحكم حتى لو لم يمكن بأن شرع في الجزء الأول سقط اشتراط تقدم السبب وجوزت المقارنة إذ لا يمكن جعل ما قبل الوقت سبباً . وذكر بعض المتأخرين من الأصوليين أن السبب في الصوم اليوم الكامل لا الجزء منه، ولا شك في المقارنة على هذا
His statement: (And if a child reaches puberty or a disbeliever becomes Muslim, he abstains for his day and does not make up anything). So the abstinence is fulfilling the right of the time by way of imitation, and no making up because fasting was not obligatory upon them in it. He gave absolute abstinence and did not clarify its description due to disagreement about it. The correct view is that it is obligatory, because it agrees with the evidence, which is what is established from his command (peace be upon him) to abstain for one who ate on the day of Ashura when it was obligatory. And he gave absolute statement in no making up, so it includes whether they broke the fast on that day or fasted it, whether before noon or after, because fasting is not divisible in obligation just as it is not divisible in performance, and the capacity for obligation is absent at its beginning, so it is not obligatory. He restricted it to fasting because if he reaches puberty or becomes Muslim during the time of prayer or at its end, it is obligatory upon him by agreement, and it is the analogy of Zufar. And he distinguished between fasting and prayer: the cause in prayer is the part connected to performance, so capacity is found with it; in fasting, the first part is the cause and capacity is absent with it. He said in Fath al-Qadir: and upon this, their statement in usul that the time-bound obligation may have time as a cause for the performed act and a container for it like the time of prayer, or as a cause and standard which is what the quantity occurs in like the time of fasting, is lenient, because it implies that the cause is the entire time in both while the contrary has become clear. Then upon what has become clear of realizing the intended meaning, it may be said: it would necessitate that abstinence is not obligatory in the very first part of the day, because it is the cause for obligation, and obligation would precede the cause due to the necessity of the cause preceding, so its obligation requires a prior cause, which contradicts the assumption. And if that were not entailed, then what they mentioned about prayer times would necessitate that causality is attached to the first part: if he does not perform immediately after it, it transfers to what follows the beginning of commencement; if he does not commence, up to the last part causality is established in it and the condition of the mukallaf is considered at it, which is unnecessary trouble, as there is no need to make it what follows it rather than what occurs in it. End quote. And it may be said: their statement implies that the cause is the entire time, that would be conceded if they were silent, but they explicitly stated that it is not possible to make the entire time a cause in prayer, and they mentioned that causality transfers from one part to another. His statement: 'then upon what has become clear' etc. There is research: upon the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi that causality is for nights and days, the cause exists by the night, so abstinence is only obligated in the first part by virtue of the cause preceding it, which is the night. And upon the choice of others that causality is specific to days and nights have no part in causality, the necessity of the cause preceding is only when possible; when not possible, it is not, and fasting is from it because its time is a standard for it measured by it, increasing with its increase and decreasing with its decrease, so it cannot be that the first part is empty of fasting to be a prior cause, nor can what is before it be a cause due to lack of suitability, so the concurrence of cause and effect is necessitated. And the author of Kashf al-Asrar, commentary on Usul Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, explicitly stated that the cause in fasting is concurrent with the effect, unlike the time of prayer for it is a container, so the precedence of cause over ruling is possible, even if it is not possible as when he commences in the first part, the condition of precedence is dropped and concurrence is permitted since what is before the time cannot be made a cause. And some later usulis mentioned that the cause in fasting is the complete day, not a part of it, and there is no doubt about concurrence upon this.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This is an expansion probe from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The passage addresses the ruling for a child who reaches puberty or a disbeliever who embraces Islam during the day of Ramadan. The statement from the matn says they must abstain (imsaak) for the rest of the day but do not have to make up the fast. The commentator explains that the imsaak is out of respect for the time and to resemble those fasting; it is not a full obligation to fast, so qada is not required. The ruling on the obligation of imsaak itself is disputed, but the preferred view (al-sahh) is wajib, based on the hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) commanded those who had eaten on Ashura to abstain when it was made obligatory. The commentator then discusses the difference between fasting and prayer: for prayer, if one becomes obligated mid-time, the full prayer is obligatory because the cause (the time) attaches to the performance; for fasting, the first part of the day is the cause, but at that moment the person was not a mukallaf, so no obligation arises for that day. This is the view of Zufar, and the commentator elaborates on the usuli discussion of sabab (cause) in fasting versus prayer, citing Fath al-Qadir and the views of Sarakhsi and other usulis. The key distinction is that the time of prayer is a container (zarf) for the act, so the cause can precede, while the time of fasting is a standard (mi'yar) and the cause (the day) must be concurrent with the effect. Some later usulis hold the cause is the entire day, which also implies concurrence. All this analysis supports the ruling that no qada is required for the day of bulugh/Islam.

Key Terms
إمساك (imsaak): Abstaining from food, drink, and other nullifiers of fast; here it is wajib out of respect for the time, not as a full fast.
قضاء (qadaa): Making up a missed obligatory fast; here it is not required because no obligation was established at the start of the day.
سبب (sabab): The cause that triggers the obligation; in fasting, the first part of the day, but when ahliyyah is absent, no obligation arises.
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): Legal capacity for obligation, which is acquired with puberty or Islam; absent at dawn for these individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A child reaches puberty at 10 AM during Ramadan. According to the passage, what must he do?
A) He must abstain for the rest of the day and make up the fast later.
B) He must fast the rest of the day and no make-up is needed.
C) He must abstain for the rest of the day but does not need to make up.
D) He may eat as the fast is not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
A person who becomes mukallaf mid-day in Ramadan must abstain out of respect but owes no make-up, because the capacity for obligation was absent at the beginning of the day.

Principle of Imsaak for Those Who Acquire a Qualifying Characteristic During the Day
Critical refinement of the imsaak principle and its applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of imsaak for those who acquire a qualifying characteristic during the day and explain the terminological refinement between al-Nihaya and Fath al-Qadir.

Core Idea
Imsaak is required by resemblance, but the literal wording fails to include intentional breakers; they are included by analogy.

This passage examines a foundational principle (asl) in Hanafi fiqh that determines when a person must abstain from food and drink (imsaak) for the remainder of the day, even if they were not obligated to fast at dawn. The commentator scrutinizes the precise wording from two major Hanafi works, revealing the juristic care in defining legal categories. Understanding this principle helps correctly classify fasting-related obligations for various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its scope
Read the opening: the author states a principle: anyone who at the end of the day is in a state that would have made fasting obligatory if present at dawn must abstain. List the cases given.
The principle is about 'imsaak tashabbuhan' (abstaining by resemblance), not a full fast.
2
Analyze the wording in al-Nihaya
Al-Nihaya uses 'man saara' (who becomes). This implies a transformation from one state to another. Which cases involve transformation? Which do not?
Intentional eating does not involve transformation; the person was already capable of fasting.
3
Examine the revision in Fath al-Qadir
Fath al-Qadir changes 'saara' to 'tahaqquq' (verifiably exists) to include cases like intentional eating. But the commentator points out that the word 'law' (if) still implies absence at dawn. Do you see the problem?
'Law' indicates the state was not present at dawn, so the intentional eater is still excluded.
4
Understand why the intentional eater is still listed
Even though not covered by the literal wording, the intentional eater is included because his ruling (imsaak) is the same. The Hanafi jurists included him by analogy (ilhaq).
This shows the difference between a literal definition and the applied ruling.
5
Identify the best formulation
The commentator notes that al-Bada'i' has a simpler expression: 'imsaak is obligatory for anyone with an excuse.' This avoids the terminological difficulties.
This is the preferred formulation for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف بالمسألتين إلى أصل وهو أن كل من صار في آخر النهار بصفة لو كان في أول النهار عليها للزمه الصوم فعليه الإمساك كالحائض والنفساء تطهر بعد طلوع الفجر أو والمجنون يفيق والمريض يبرأ والمسافر يقدم بعد الزوال أو الأكل والذي أفطر عمداً أو خطأ أو مكرهاً أو أكل يوم الشك ثم استبان أنه من رمضان أو أن الشمس قد غربت أو تسحر بعد الفجر ولم يعلم ومن لم يكن على تلك الصفة لم يجب الإمساك كما في حالة الحيض والنفاس . ثم قيل : الحائض تأكل سراً لا جهراً، وقيل تأكل سراً وجهراً، وللمريض والمسافر الأكل جهراً. كذا في النهاية. وغير في فتح القدير عبارة هذا الأصل فقال : كل من تحقق بصفة في أثناء النهار أو قارن ابتداء وجودها الفجر وتلك الصفة بحيث لو كانت قبله واستمرت معه وجب عليه الصوم فإنه يجب عليه الإمساك تشبهاً . قال : وقلنا كل من تحقق ولم نقل من صار بصفة إلى آخره يعني كما في النهاية ليشمل من أكل عمداً في نهار رمضان لأن الصيرورة للتحول ولو لامتناع ما يليه ولا يتحقق المفاد بهما فيه .اهـ. والحاصل أن من أكل عمداً في نهار رمضان لم يدخل تحت عبارة النهاية باعتبار أنه لم يتجدد له حالة بعد فطره لم يكن عليها قبله وكلمة «صار» تفيد التحول من حالة إلى أخرى بخلاف تحقق ولا يخفى أن ما هرب منه وقع فيه لأنه وإن غير «صار» إلى «تحقق» أتى بكلمة «لو» المفيدة لامتناع ما يليه المفيدة أن الصفة لم تكن موجودة أول اليوم فلا يشمل كلامه من أكل عمداً فليتأمل . فظهر من هذا أن من كان أهلاً للصوم في أوله كمن أكل عمداً لا يدخل تحت الضابط أصلاً على كل منهما، وإنما أدرجوه في هذا الأصل وإن لم يدخل تحته باعتبار أن حكمه وجوب الإمساك تشبهاً فهو مثله لأن غرضهم بيان الأحكام. وعبارة البدائع أولى وهي : أما وجوب الإمساك تشبهاً بالصائمين فكل من كان له عذر في قوله : (وعبارة البدائع إلى قوله وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية) سقط من بعض النسخ .
And the author indicated by the two issues a principle: that whoever becomes at the end of the day in a state such that if he were in that state at the beginning of the day, fasting would be incumbent upon him, then he must abstain, like the menstruating woman and the postpartum woman who become pure after dawn, and the insane person who regains sanity, the sick person who recovers, the traveler who arrives after noon, and the one who ate intentionally or mistakenly or under coercion, or the one who ate on the day of doubt and then it became clear that it was Ramadan, or that the sun had set, or who ate after dawn without knowing, and whoever is not in that state, imsaak is not obligatory, as in the case of menstruation and postpartum bleeding. Then it was said: the menstruating woman eats secretly, not openly; and it was said: she eats secretly and openly. For the sick and traveler, they may eat openly. Thus in al-Nihaya. And in Fath al-Qadir, the wording of this principle was changed: 'Everyone who is verifiably in a state during the day or whose state coincides with the dawn, such that if it were before and continued with him, fasting would be obligatory upon him, then imsaak is obligatory by way of resemblance.' He said: 'We said everyone who is verifiably (tahaqquq) and did not say who becomes (sar) in a state' etc., meaning as in al-Nihaya, to include the one who intentionally ate in Ramadan, because 'sara' implies transformation from one state to another, while 'tahaqquq' does not entail transformation. But it is not hidden that what he fled from fell into, because although he changed 'sara' to 'tahaqquq', he used the word 'law' (if) which implies impossibility of the ensuing, indicating that the state was not present at the beginning of the day, so his wording does not include the one who ate intentionally. So reflect. So it becomes clear that one who was ahl (capable) of fasting at its beginning like the one who ate intentionally is not included under the criterion in either wording; they only included him in this principle although he does not enter under it because his ruling is the obligation of imsaak by way of resemblance, so he is like them since their purpose is to explain rulings. The expression of al-Bada'i' is more suitable: 'As for the obligation of imsaak resembling the fasters, it applies to everyone who had an excuse.' His statement: (and the expression of al-Bada'i' up to his saying 'and in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya') is omitted in some copies.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
In this critical refinement, the commentator discusses a principle (asl) derived by the author from two preceding issues (likely the bulugh/Islam and other cases). The principle states: anyone who, by the end of the day, is in a state that, had it existed at dawn, would have made fasting obligatory, must abstain (imsaak). Examples include a menstruating woman who becomes pure after dawn, an insane person who regains sanity, a sick person who recovers, a traveler who arrives after noon, one who broke fast intentionally, mistakenly, or under coercion, one who ate on a doubtful day and it became clear it was Ramadan, one who ate thinking the sun had set, and one who ate after dawn unknowingly. Excluded are those not in such a state, like a woman still menstruating at the end of the day. The passage then examines the precise wording of the principle. Al-Nihaya used 'sara' (became), which implies a transition from one state to another. Fath al-Qadir changed it to 'tahaqquq' (verifiable existence) to include cases like intentional eating where there is no change of state (the person was already capable of fasting). However, the commentator points out that the revision still uses 'law' (if), which indicates the state was absent at dawn, so it still fails to include the intentional eater. In reality, the intentional eater does not fit the principle in either phrasing; he is listed only because his ruling (imsaak) is the same. The best formulation is from al-Bada'i', which simply says: imsaak is obligatory for anyone with an excuse. The note at the end signals that the text from al-Bada'i' is missing in some manuscripts.

Key Terms
أصل (asl): A foundational principle or maxim from which rulings are derived; here referring to the rule about imsaak for those who acquire qualifying characteristics.
إمساك تشبهاً (imsaak tashabbuhan): Abstaining not as a wajib fast but out of respect and to resemble fasting people; this is the nature of the imsaak in these cases.
صار (saara): A verb meaning 'to become' or 'transform into'; used in al-Nihaya's wording to indicate a change of state.
تحقق (tahaqquq): Verifiable existence or realization of a state; used in Fath al-Qadir's revision to broaden the scope.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which wording BEST captures the intended scope of the imsaak principle?
A) Al-Nihaya's 'man saara'
B) Fath al-Qadir's 'man tahaqquq'
C) Al-Bada'i's 'man kana lahu dhur' (anyone with an excuse)
D) All are equally valid.

Key Takeaway
The imsaak principle is carefully worded; the intentional eater is included by analogy, not by the literal wording of the principle.

The Cause of Fasting and Concomitance (Muqarana)
Usul discussion on sabab of fasting and necessity of muqarana.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi view on the cause (sabab) of fasting and explain why cause and effect must be concomitant (muqarana).

Core Idea
Fasting requires its cause to be present together with the act — unlike prayer, the time cannot precede the fast.

This lesson explores the usul debate about the cause that triggers the obligation of fasting. The author presents two views: that the cause is the night (the time of determining the month) or that it is the day itself. He argues that fasting is unique because its time is the standard – the first part of the day cannot be empty of the fast. This leads to the principle of concomitance (muqarana), which is the foundation for the ruling on imsak later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views on the sabab of fasting
One view holds that the cause is the night (the time of moon-sighting). The other view holds that causality is specific to days. Note that the author's phrasing 'السبب عليه وهو الليل' indicates the first is the preferred or default view.
The second view is attributed to 'some later usulis' who say the cause is the complete day.
2
Analyze the argument against the precedence of cause
Al-Marghinani argues that in fasting, the cause cannot precede the effect because the time of fasting is the measure: it increases and decreases with the day. The first part of the day cannot be empty of fasting, otherwise there would be no preceding cause.
This is compared to prayer, where time is a container (zarf) and the cause (entering time) can precede the actual prayer.
3
Understand the conclusion of concomitance
Since the cause cannot precede, it must be concurrent with the effect (muqarin). The author of Kashf al-Asrar confirms that the cause in fasting is concomitant. The later usuli view that the cause is the full day also implies concomitance.
This sets the stage for the principle that the ruling applies the moment the quality arises during the day.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم فصل
السبب عليه وهو الليل، وأما على اختيار غيره من أن السببية خاصة بالأيام وأن الليالي لا دخل لها في السببية، فلأن لزوم تقدم السبب إنما هو عند الإمكان، أما عند عدم الإمكان فلا والصوم منه لأن وقته معيار له مقدر به يزيد بزيادته وينقص بنقصانه فلا يمكن أن يكون الجزء الأول خالياً عن الصوم ليكون سبباً متقدماً، ولا يمكن أن يكون ما قبله سبباً لعدم الصلاحية فلزم فيه مقارنة السبب للمسبب، وقد صرح بأن السبب في الصوم مقارن للمسبب صاحب كشف الأسرار شرح أصول فخر الإسلام البزدوي بخلاف وقت الصلاة فإنه ظرف فأمكن تقدم السبب على الحكم حتى لو لم يمكن بأن شرع في الجزء الأول سقط اشتراط تقدم السبب وجوزت المقارنة إذ لا يمكن جعل ما قبل الوقت سبباً .
وذكر بعض المتأخرين من الأصوليين أن السبب في الصوم اليوم الكامل لا الجزء منه، ولا شك في المقارنة على هذا وأشار المصنف بالمسألتين إلى أصل وهو أن كل من صار في آخر النهار بصفة لو كان في أول النهار عليها للزمه الصوم فعليه الإمساك كالحائض والنفساء تطهر بعد طلوع الفجر أو والمجنون يفيق والمريض يبرأ والمسافر يقدم بعد الزوال أو الأكل والذي أفطر عمداً أو خطأ أو مكرهاً أو أكل يوم الشك ثم استبان أنه من رمضان أو أن الشمس قد غربت أو تسحر بعد الفجر ولم يعلم ومن لم يكن على تلك الصفة لم يجب الإمساك كما في حالة الحيض والنفاس . ثم قيل : الحائض تأكل سراً لا جهراً، وقيل تأكل سراً وجهراً، وللمريض والمسافر الأكل جهراً. كذا في النهاية. وغير في فتح القدير عبارة هذا الأصل فقال : كل من تحقق بصفة في أثناء النهار أو قارن ابتداء وجودها الفجر وتلك الصفة بحيث لو كانت قبله واستمرت معه وجب عليه الصوم فإنه يجب عليه الإمساك تشبهاً . قال : وقلنا كل من تحقق ولم نقل من صار بصفة إلى آخره يعني كما في النهاية ليشمل من أكل عمداً في نهار رمضان لأن الصيرورة للتحول ولو لامتناع ما يليه ولا يتحقق المفاد بهما فيه .اهـ. والحاصل أن من أكل عمداً في نهار رمضان لم يدخل تحت عبارة النهاية باعتبار أنه لم يتجدد له حالة بعد فطره لم يكن عليها قبله وكلمة «صار» تفيد التحول من حالة إلى أخرى بخلاف تحقق ولا يخفى أن ما هرب منه وقع فيه لأنه وإن غير «صار» إلى «تحقق» أتى بكلمة «لو» المفيدة لامتناع ما يليه المفيدة أن الصفة لم تكن موجودة أول اليوم فلا يشمل كلامه من أكل عمداً فليتأمل . فظهر من هذا أن من كان أهلاً للصوم في أوله كمن أكل عمداً لا يدخل تحت الضابط أصلاً على كل منهما، وإنما أدرجوه في هذا الأصل وإن لم يدخل تحته باعتبار أن حكمه وجوب الإمساك تشبهاً فهو مثله لأن غرضهم بيان الأحكام. وعبارة البدائع أولى وهي : أما وجوب الإمساك تشبهاً بالصائمين فكل من كان له عذر في أول النهار مانع من الوجوب أو مبيح للفطر ثم زال عذره وصار بحال لو كان عليه في أول النهار لوجب عليه الصوم لا يباح له الفطر كالصبي إذا بلغ، والكافر إذا أسلم، والمجنون إذا أفاق والحائض إذا طهرت، والمسافر إذا قدم، وكذا كل من وجب عليه الصوم لوجود سبب الوجوب والأهلية ثم تعذر عليه المضي بأن أفطر متعمداً أو أصبح يوم الشك مفطراً ثم تبين أنه من رمضان أو تسحر على ظن أن الفجر لم يطلع ثم تبين أنه طالع ، فإنه يجب عليه الإمساك تشبهاً. فقد جعل لوجوب الإمساك أصلين، وجعل بعض الفروع مخرجة على أصل وبعضها على آخر فلا إيراد أصلاً والله الموفق.
قوله : (وعبارة البدائع إلى قوله وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية) سقط من بعض النسخ .
Book of Fasting, Section. The cause [for the obligation of fasting] is the night, but according to the choice of others that causality is specific to days and that nights have no role in causality, because the necessity of the precedence of the cause is only when possible, but when not possible it is not, and fasting is from this [category] because its time is a standard for it measured by it, increasing with its increase and decreasing with its decrease, so it is impossible for the first part [of the day] to be empty of fasting to be a preceding cause, nor can what precedes it be a cause due to lack of suitability, so concomitance of cause and effect is necessary in it. And the author of Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi has stated that the cause in fasting is concomitant with the effect, unlike the time of prayer for it is a container, so it is possible for the cause to precede the ruling, even if it is not possible, as when he began in the first part, the condition of precedence of the cause falls away and concomitance is permitted since it is impossible to make what is before the time a cause. And some later usulis mentioned that the cause in fasting is the complete day, not a part of it, and there is no doubt about concomitance on this. And the author indicated by the two issues a principle: that whoever becomes characterized at the end of the day with a quality such that if he had it at the beginning of the day fasting would be obligatory upon him, then refraining is incumbent on him, like the menstruating woman and the woman with postpartum bleeding who become pure after dawn, or the insane person who regains sanity, the sick person who recovers, the traveler who arrives after noon, or the one who eats, and the one who broke his fast intentionally or unintentionally or under coercion or ate on the day of doubt then it became clear that it was from Ramadan, or that the sun had set, or who ate suhur after dawn without knowing, and whoever did not have that quality, refraining is not obligatory, as in the case of menstruation and postpartum bleeding. Then it was said: the menstruating woman eats secretly not openly, and it was said she eats secretly and openly, and for the sick person and traveler eating openly. Thus in al-Nihaya. And [the author] changed in Fath al-Qadir the wording of this principle and said: Everyone who is realized with a quality during the day, or its initial existence coincides with dawn, and that quality is such that if it were before him and continued with him, fasting would be obligatory upon him, then refraining is obligatory upon him in emulation. He said: We said 'everyone who is realized' and we did not say 'whoever becomes characterized' etc., meaning as in al-Nihaya, to include the one who ate intentionally in the day of Ramadan, because 'becoming' (sayrurah) implies transformation, even if it implies impossibility of what follows it, and the meaning is not realized by both in that case. End. The upshot is that the one who ate intentionally in the day of Ramadan is not included under the wording of al-Nihaya because he did not have a new state after his breaking that he did not have before, and the word 'sara' (became) indicates transformation from one state to another unlike 'tahaqqaqa' (was realized). And it is not hidden that what he fled from he fell into, because although he changed 'sara' to 'tahaqqaqa', he came with the word 'law' (if) which implies impossibility of what follows it, implying that the quality did not exist at the beginning of the day, so his speech does not include the one who ate intentionally, so let it be considered. So it appears from this that whoever was eligible for fasting at its beginning, like the one who ate intentionally, does not enter under the control at all according to both [wordings], but they included him in this principle even though he does not enter under it, because his ruling is the obligation of refraining in emulation, so he is like it, because their purpose is to state the rulings. And the wording of al-Bada'i' is more appropriate, which is: As for the obligation of refraining in emulation of those fasting, then everyone who had an excuse at the beginning of the day that prevented obligation or permitted breaking fast, then his excuse ceased and he became in a state such that if he had been in that state at the beginning of the day fasting would be obligatory upon him, breaking fast is not permissible for him, like the boy when he reaches puberty, the infidel when he converts, the insane when he recovers, the menstruating woman when she becomes pure, the traveler when he arrives, and likewise everyone upon whom fasting became obligatory due to the existence of the cause of obligation and eligibility, then proceeding became difficult for him, such as breaking intentionally, or becoming a breaker on the day of doubt then it became clear that it was from Ramadan, or having suhur thinking that dawn had not risen then it became clear that it had risen, then refraining is obligatory upon him in emulation. So he made for the obligation of refraining two principles, and made some branches derived on one principle and some on the other, so there is no objection at all. And Allah is the granter of success. His statement: (And the wording of al-Bada'i' up to his statement 'and in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya') is omitted from some copies.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, combining usul al-fiqh reasoning with a fiqh principle. The author, al-Marghinani in his commentary al-Hidayah (and here al-Bahr al-Raiq is commenting on Kanz al-Daqaiq), begins by discussing the cause (sabab) of fasting. He presents two views: one that the cause is the night (because the obligation of fasting is established by sighting the new moon or completing Sha'ban, which occurs at night), and another that the cause is the day itself. He argues that for fasting, the cause and effect must be concomitant because the time of fasting is the standard—it cannot be that the first part of the day is empty of fasting. This is unlike prayer, where the time is a container and the cause (e.g., entering the time) can precede the actual prayer. This usul discussion sets the stage for a key principle: whoever ends the day with a quality that would have obligated fasting had it been present at dawn must refrain from eating (imsak) for the rest of the day, out of emulation (tashabbuhan) with the fasting people. Examples include a menstruating woman who becomes pure after dawn, a madman who recovers, a traveler who arrives, etc. The author then critiques the wording of al-Nihaya, which uses 'sara' (becomes), and prefers the wording of al-Bada'i' which uses 'tahaqqaqa' (is realized) and adds the phrase 'if it had been before him' to include the case of intentional eating. The principle has two foundations: those who become eligible anew during the day, and those who were eligible but broke their fast. The author concludes that there is no objection to having two principles because different branches are related to each.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): The cause that triggers the obligation of fasting; discussed here as either the night or the complete day.
المقارنة (al-muqarana): Concomitance; the principle that the sabab must be present together with the musabbab (the ruled act) in fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preferred view on the sabab (cause) of fasting?
A) The dawn (fajr) because that is when the fast begins.
B) The night (layl) because the moon is sighted at night.
C) The complete day because the fast spans the whole day.
D) The intention (niyyah) because it is the basis of all acts.

Key Takeaway
The cause of fasting must be present together with the act because the time of fasting cannot be empty of the fast.
