Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
The Principle of Imsak for Those Becoming Eligible During the Day
Fiqh principle on wajib imsak for those becoming eligible mid-day.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of imsak tashabbuhan to various scenarios of eligibility change during the day.

Core Idea
Whoever becomes qualified to fast after dawn must refrain from eating for the rest of the day out of emulation.

This lesson covers the practical fiqh principle derived from the usul discussion on the cause of fasting. The author presents a key principle: anyone who, at the end of the day, has a quality that would have obligated fasting had it existed at dawn must observe restraint (imsak) for the remainder of the day. This includes a menstruating woman who becomes pure, a traveler arriving home, and even someone who intentionally broke their fast. The lesson also examines the different wordings of this principle in al-Nihaya and al-Bada'i', and why the latter is preferred.

Guided Path
1
State the principle as given in al-Nihaya
Al-Nihaya states: 'Whoever becomes characterized (sara) at the end of the day with a quality such that if he had it at the beginning of the day fasting would be obligatory, then imsak is wajib.' Understand the phrase 'sara bi-sifa' (became characterized with a quality).
Examples immediately follow: menstruating woman becoming pure, insane person recovering, etc.
2
Identify the individuals covered by the principle
List the examples: menstruating woman (after dawn), postpartum woman, insane person who recovers, sick person who recovers, traveler who arrives after noon, one who ate intentionally or unintentionally, one who ate on the day of doubt then it becomes Ramadan, one who ate suhur thinking dawn had not come, etc.
The principle also covers those who were eligible at dawn but broke their fast, e.g., intentional eater.
3
Contrast the wording of al-Nihaya with al-Bada'i'
Al-Nihaya uses 'sara' (became) which implies transformation from one state to another. This wording excludes the intentional eater because he did not undergo a new transformation (he was always an eligible person). Al-Bada'i' uses 'tahaqqaqa' (was realized) and adds the condition 'if it had been before him' to include intentional eaters.
Al-Marghinani prefers al-Bada'i's wording because it covers all cases without exclusion.
4
Understand the two principles in al-Bada'i'
Al-Bada'i' establishes two origins for the obligation of imsak: one for those who had an excuse at dawn (e.g., menstruation, travel) and another for those who were already obligated but then broke their fast. Both result in imsak, but the legal basis differs.
This resolves the objection that the intentional eater is not included under the first wording.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Test the principle: A woman becomes pure from menstruation at 2 PM during Ramadan. Ruling: She must refrain from eating for the rest of the day (imsak wajib). She does not make up the fast later (since she was not a fasting person at dawn), but she must make up the day after Ramadan.
The imsak is out of respect for the sacred time, not as a valid fast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم فصل
السبب عليه وهو الليل، وأما على اختيار غيره من أن السببية خاصة بالأيام وأن الليالي لا دخل لها في السببية، فلأن لزوم تقدم السبب إنما هو عند الإمكان، أما عند عدم الإمكان فلا والصوم منه لأن وقته معيار له مقدر به يزيد بزيادته وينقص بنقصانه فلا يمكن أن يكون الجزء الأول خالياً عن الصوم ليكون سبباً متقدماً، ولا يمكن أن يكون ما قبله سبباً لعدم الصلاحية فلزم فيه مقارنة السبب للمسبب، وقد صرح بأن السبب في الصوم مقارن للمسبب صاحب كشف الأسرار شرح أصول فخر الإسلام البزدوي بخلاف وقت الصلاة فإنه ظرف فأمكن تقدم السبب على الحكم حتى لو لم يمكن بأن شرع في الجزء الأول سقط اشتراط تقدم السبب وجوزت المقارنة إذ لا يمكن جعل ما قبل الوقت سبباً .
وذكر بعض المتأخرين من الأصوليين أن السبب في الصوم اليوم الكامل لا الجزء منه، ولا شك في المقارنة على هذا وأشار المصنف بالمسألتين إلى أصل وهو أن كل من صار في آخر النهار بصفة لو كان في أول النهار عليها للزمه الصوم فعليه الإمساك كالحائض والنفساء تطهر بعد طلوع الفجر أو والمجنون يفيق والمريض يبرأ والمسافر يقدم بعد الزوال أو الأكل والذي أفطر عمداً أو خطأ أو مكرهاً أو أكل يوم الشك ثم استبان أنه من رمضان أو أن الشمس قد غربت أو تسحر بعد الفجر ولم يعلم ومن لم يكن على تلك الصفة لم يجب الإمساك كما في حالة الحيض والنفاس . ثم قيل : الحائض تأكل سراً لا جهراً، وقيل تأكل سراً وجهراً، وللمريض والمسافر الأكل جهراً. كذا في النهاية. وغير في فتح القدير عبارة هذا الأصل فقال : كل من تحقق بصفة في أثناء النهار أو قارن ابتداء وجودها الفجر وتلك الصفة بحيث لو كانت قبله واستمرت معه وجب عليه الصوم فإنه يجب عليه الإمساك تشبهاً . قال : وقلنا كل من تحقق ولم نقل من صار بصفة إلى آخره يعني كما في النهاية ليشمل من أكل عمداً في نهار رمضان لأن الصيرورة للتحول ولو لامتناع ما يليه ولا يتحقق المفاد بهما فيه .اهـ. والحاصل أن من أكل عمداً في نهار رمضان لم يدخل تحت عبارة النهاية باعتبار أنه لم يتجدد له حالة بعد فطره لم يكن عليها قبله وكلمة «صار» تفيد التحول من حالة إلى أخرى بخلاف تحقق ولا يخفى أن ما هرب منه وقع فيه لأنه وإن غير «صار» إلى «تحقق» أتى بكلمة «لو» المفيدة لامتناع ما يليه المفيدة أن الصفة لم تكن موجودة أول اليوم فلا يشمل كلامه من أكل عمداً فليتأمل . فظهر من هذا أن من كان أهلاً للصوم في أوله كمن أكل عمداً لا يدخل تحت الضابط أصلاً على كل منهما، وإنما أدرجوه في هذا الأصل وإن لم يدخل تحته باعتبار أن حكمه وجوب الإمساك تشبهاً فهو مثله لأن غرضهم بيان الأحكام. وعبارة البدائع أولى وهي : أما وجوب الإمساك تشبهاً بالصائمين فكل من كان له عذر في أول النهار مانع من الوجوب أو مبيح للفطر ثم زال عذره وصار بحال لو كان عليه في أول النهار لوجب عليه الصوم لا يباح له الفطر كالصبي إذا بلغ، والكافر إذا أسلم، والمجنون إذا أفاق والحائض إذا طهرت، والمسافر إذا قدم، وكذا كل من وجب عليه الصوم لوجود سبب الوجوب والأهلية ثم تعذر عليه المضي بأن أفطر متعمداً أو أصبح يوم الشك مفطراً ثم تبين أنه من رمضان أو تسحر على Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, combining usul al-fiqh reasoning with a fiqh principle. The author, al-Marghinani in his commentary al-Hidayah (and here al-Bahr al-Raiq is commenting on Kanz al-Daqaiq), begins by discussing the cause (sabab) of fasting. He presents two views: one that the cause is the night (because the obligation of fasting is established by sighting the new moon or completing Sha'ban, which occurs at night), and another that the cause is the day itself. He argues that for fasting, the cause and effect must be concomitant because the time of fasting is the standard—it cannot be that the first part of the day is empty of fasting. This is unlike prayer, where the time is a container and the cause (e.g., entering the time) can precede the actual prayer. This usul discussion sets the stage for a key principle: whoever ends the day with a quality that would have obligated fasting had it been present at dawn must refrain from eating (imsak) for the rest of the day, out of emulation (tashabbuhan) with the fasting people. Examples include a menstruating woman who becomes pure after dawn, a madman who recovers, a traveler who arrives, etc. The author then critiques the wording of al-Nihaya, which uses 'sara' (becomes), and prefers the wording of al-Bada'i' which uses 'tahaqqaqa' (is realized) and adds the phrase 'if it had been before him' to include the case of intentional eating. The principle has two foundations: those who become eligible anew during the day, and those who were eligible but broke their fast. The author concludes that there is no objection to having two principles because different branches are related to each.

Key Terms
الإمساك تشبهاً (al-imsak tashabbuhan): Refraining from eating, drinking, and other nullifiers of fasting for the remainder of the day out of respect for the sacred time, not as an actual fast.
الصيرورة (al-sayrurah): Transformation from one state to another; used to describe the change that obligates imsak.
التحقق (al-tahaqquq): Realization or actualization of a quality; used in al-Bada'i' to include those who do not undergo a state change.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle, which of the following persons must observe imsak (refrain from eating) for the rest of the day?
A) A woman who becomes pure from menstruation at 5 PM.
B) A traveler who arrives home at 3 PM and had been fasting.
C) A person who intentionally broke his fast at noon.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of imsak is wajib out of emulation for all who would have been required to fast if their quality had existed at dawn, with the wording of al-Bada'i' preferred for its inclusiveness.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
10 min
Specific Rulings on Eligibility During the Day: Boy, Infidel, Traveler, and Unconsciousness
Specific furu' cases on fasting eligibility after dawn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for each of the five scenarios: boy reaching puberty, infidel converting, insane person recovering, traveler intending break then arriving, and unconscious person; and identify the underlying principle of eligibility (ahliyyah) at dawn.

Core Idea
A person who becomes eligible to fast during the day may not count that day as obligatory; capacity at dawn is key.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a series of specific cases (furu') from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya, building on the general principle that the obligation of fasting is tied to legal capacity (ahliyyah) at the beginning of the day. Each case illustrates how different states—boyhood, disbelief, insanity, travel, and unconsciousness—affect the validity of the fast and the requirement of qada'. The passage also notes an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and the majority (Zahir al-Riwaya) regarding the boy who reaches puberty before noon.

Guided Path
1
Identify each scenario and its key feature
The passage covers five distinct cases: (1) a boy who reaches puberty before noon, (2) an infidel who converts to Islam before noon, (3) an insane person who recovers before noon, (4) a traveler who intends to break his fast then arrives home before noon, and (5) a person who becomes unconscious during the day. For each, note the time of eligibility and the intention.
These cases all involve someone who becomes eligible to fast after dawn.
2
Apply the principle of ahliyyah (legal capacity)
The decisive factor is whether the person possessed legal capacity (ahliyyah) at dawn. The boy and infidel lacked it at dawn, so their fast after eligibility does not count as obligatory. The insane person, if he recovers before noon, is considered like an ill person—illness does not negate obligation—so his fast is valid. Travel and unconsciousness are treated differently.
Ahliyyah al-wujub (capacity to be obligated) is required at the start of the day for obligatory fasts.
3
Handle the traveler's case with care
If a traveler intended to break his fast, then arrived before noon and intended to fast, his fast is valid if he intended before midday. This is because travel does not negate eligibility. However, if he breaks after intending to fast, no kaffarah is due due to the doubt (shubha) of the permit. If he intended to fast the night before and did not break his resolve, he cannot break later.
The permit to break disappears once he settles with intention before noon.
4
Distinguish unconsciousness from insanity
Unconsciousness (ighma') is like an illness: it weakens but does not remove intellect. Therefore, a person who becomes unconscious during the day must make up the fast (qada'), unless the unconsciousness began the night before (i.e., covered the entire day). Insanity, when it does not encompass the whole day, is also like illness and does not negate the obligation.
Both are treated as excuses for delay, not for dropping the obligation entirely.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on the boy's fast
According to Zahir al-Riwaya (majority), the boy's fast is nafl (voluntary), and he must still complete his Ramadan obligation. Abu Yusuf holds that it counts for the obligatory fast. The relied-upon position is the majority view. This thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) affects whether he needs to make up that day.
Abu Yusuf's reasoning: eligibility is established by the time of intention before noon.

Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a collection of specific rulings (furu') from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya, applied under the general principle just discussed. It covers several cases: (1) A boy who reaches puberty before noon and an infidel who converts to Islam before noon: their fasting (if they intended before noon) is not valid for the obligatory fast of Ramadan because they lacked eligibility at dawn. However, the boy's fast counts as a voluntary fast (nafl), but the infidel's does not, because he lacked the basic eligibility (ahliyyah) for worship before conversion. Abu Yusuf disagrees and holds that the boy's fast is valid for the obligatory. (2) The insane person who recovers before noon: his fast is valid for the obligatory, because insanity that does not last the whole day is like illness, which does not negate the obligation of fasting, unlike boyhood, disbelief, or menstruation, which are inherently contradictory to fasting. (3) A traveler who intends to break his fast but then arrives home before noon and intends fasting: his fast is valid if he intended before midday, because travel does not negate eligibility. If he intends the obligatory fast of Ramadan, it becomes obligatory for him to continue because the permit for breaking (rukhsah) disappears once he settles. However, if he breaks, there is no expiation (kaffarah) due to the doubt of the permit. Similarly, if a traveler intended to fast at night and did not break his resolve before dawn, he must fast and cannot break later. (4) Unconsciousness (ighma'): a person who becomes unconscious during the day must make up (qada') the fast, except for a day that began while he was already unconscious from the previous night; that day does not require makeup because the unconsciousness from the night prevents intention. The reason is that unconsciousness is like an illness that weakens but does not remove intellect. (5) A side note about the traveler: if fasting harms him, he should not fast and does not have to make it up? The text is unclear at the end.

Key Terms
الفتاوى الظهيرية (al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya): A collection of legal rulings attributed to Imam al-Zahiriyya, used as a source for later Hanafi fatwa.
الأهلية (al-ahliyyah): Legal capacity required for an act of worship to be valid; a disbeliever lacks ahliyyat al-wujub for fasting.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The transmitted rulings of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, forming the core of the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His fast is invalid because he intended to break.
B) His fast is valid if he intended before midday, and it becomes obligatory for him to continue.
C) His fast is valid but he must make it up later.
D) He must break his fast and pay kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on fasting for those who become eligible during the day hinges on whether they possessed legal capacity at dawn; only the insane person, due to analogy with illness, has his fast count as obligatory.

Ruling on Those Whose Excuse for Fasting Ends During the Day
States the ruling for those whose impediment to fasting ends during the day.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a person whose impediment to fasting is removed during the day, and distinguish between cases where the fast becomes valid (madman) and where it does not (boy, disbeliever, menstruating woman).

Core Idea
When an excuse for not fasting ends during the day, refraining (imsaak) is wajib, but the fast is not valid and must be made up, except for the madman who recovers before noon.

This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies a practical scenario: what happens when a person who was not obligated to fast (due to childhood, disbelief, menstruation, travel, etc.) becomes obligated during the day? The Hanafi school distinguishes between different impediments, leading to nuanced rulings. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the rules of Ramadan fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
The passage lists categories of people who were not obligated to fast at dawn but become obligated during the day. The general ruling is that they must refrain from eating, drinking, etc. for the remainder of the day, but their fast is not valid and they must make up that day later.
Examples: boy reaching puberty, disbeliever converting, menstruating woman becoming pure, traveler arriving home.
2
Recognize the Two Components of the Ruling
The ruling consists of two parts: (1) wajib al-imsaak (obligation to refrain) for the rest of the day, (2) qada (make-up) of that day later, because the day's fast was not a valid Ramadan fast.
The obligation to refrain is based on tashabbuh (imitation of fasting people), not on the validity of the fast.
3
Note the Exception for the Madman
Unlike the other categories, if a madman recovers his senses before noon and intends to fast, his fast is valid for the obligatory fast and he does not need to make it up. This is because insanity when not extensive is analogous to sickness, which does not negate the obligation to fast.
This exception is from the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya).
4
Analyze the Intra-Madhhab Ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that the boy's fast before noon is valid for the obligatory fast, contrary to the majority view. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the majority view: the boy's fast is not valid for the obligatory, but counts as supererogatory.
The practical difference: according to Abu Yusuf, the boy does not need to make up the day; according to the majority, he must.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العوارض: في وقته صح ويقضي بإغماء سوى يوم حدث في ليلته وبجنون غير ممتد وبإمساك بلا صوم رمضان في أول النهار مانع من الوجوب أو مبيح للفطر ثم زال عذره وصار بحال لو كان عليه في أول النهار لوجب عليه الصوم لا يباح له الفطر كالصبي إذا بلغ، والكافر إذا أسلم، والمجنون إذا أفاق والحائض إذا طهرت، والمسافر إذا قدم، وكذا كل من وجب عليه الصوم لوجود سبب الوجوب والأهلية ثم تعذر عليه المضي بأن أفطر متعمداً أو أصبح يوم الشك مفطراً ثم تبين أنه من رمضان أو تسحر على ظن أن الفجر لم يطلع ثم تبين أنه طالع ، فإنه يجب عليه الإمساك تشبهاً. فقد جعل لوجوب الإمساك أصلين، وجعل بعض الفروع مخرجة على أصل وبعضها على آخر فلا إيراد أصلاً والله الموفق. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية : صبي بلغ قبل الزوال ونصراني أسلم ونويا الصوم قبل الزوال لا يجوز صومهما عن الفرض غير أن الصبي يكون صائماً عن التطوع بخلاف الكافر لفقد الأهلية في حقه. وعن أبي يوسف: إن الصبي يجوز صومه عن الفرض وقيل : جوابه في الكافر كذلك . إليه أشار في المنتقى . ثم في ظاهر الرواية فرق بين هذا وبين المجنون إذا أفاق في نهار رمضان قبل الزوال ولم يكن أكل شيئاً ونوى الصوم جاز عن الفرض لأن الجنون إذا لم يستوعب كان بمنزلة المرض والمرض لا ينافي وجوب الصوم بخلاف الصبي والكفر والحيض لأنها منافية للصوم اهـ.
Chapter on Impediments: At its time it is valid and he makes up [the fast] except for a day that occurred at night due to unconsciousness, and due to non-extensive insanity, and due to refraining without [valid] fast of Ramadan at the beginning of the day [when there is] a preventative of obligation or a permitter of breaking fast, then his excuse ceased and he became in a state that if he had been upon it at the beginning of the day, fasting would have been obligatory upon him, then breaking fast is not permissible for him. Examples: a boy when he reaches puberty, a disbeliever when he accepts Islam, a madman when he recovers, a menstruating woman when she becomes pure, a traveler when he arrives. Likewise everyone upon whom fasting becomes obligatory due to the existence of the cause of obligation and eligibility, then he was unable to continue by intentionally breaking fast, or he began the day of doubt as a non-faster then it became clear that it is from Ramadan, or he ate suhur thinking dawn had not risen then it became clear that it had risen. Then he must refrain as a form of imitation. He made for the obligation of refraining two principles, and made some branches derived from one principle and some from the other, so there is no objection at all. And Allah grants success. And in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: A boy who reaches puberty before noon and a Christian who accepts Islam and they both intended fast before noon, their fast is not valid for the obligatory fast, except that the boy is fasting as supererogatory, unlike the disbeliever due to lack of eligibility in his case. And from Abu Yusuf: The boy's fast is valid for the obligatory. And it is said: His answer regarding the disbeliever is likewise. Al-Muntqa pointed to it. Then in the apparent narration, there is a difference between this and the madman: if he recovers during the day of Ramadan before noon and had not eaten anything and intended fast, it is valid for the obligatory, because insanity when it is not extensive is like sickness, and sickness does not contradict the obligation of fasting, unlike boyhood, disbelief, and menstruation because they contradict fasting. End.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from al-Hidayah (the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq). The author discusses the category of people whose excuse for not fasting ends during the day. He states the general principle: if a person had an impediment (like being a traveler, sick, menstruating, etc.) that either prevented the obligation or permitted breaking the fast, and that impediment is removed during the day, then they must refrain from eating and drinking for the rest of the day, but their day is not counted as a valid Ramadan fast and they must make it up later. The author gives examples: a child who reaches puberty, a disbeliever who accepts Islam, a madman who recovers, a menstruating woman who becomes pure, and a traveler who arrives at his destination. Additionally, he includes those who intentionally broke their fast or were uncertain and then confirmed it was Ramadan. He notes that the obligation to refrain is based on two principles (aslain). Then he quotes from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya about the specific cases of the boy and the convert, noting that according to that source, the boy's fast counts as supererogatory but not obligatory, while the convert's fast is not valid even as supererogatory due to lack of eligibility. Abu Yusuf differs, holding that the boy's fast is valid for the obligatory. The apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) distinguishes a recovering madman: his fast before noon is valid for the obligatory because insanity that is not extensive is like sickness, which does not negate the obligation of fasting, unlike boyhood, disbelief, and menstruation, which directly negate fasting.

Key Terms
العوارض (al-‘awāriḍ): Impediments or barriers that affect the obligation or validity of fasting, such as travel, sickness, menstruation, etc.
الإمساك (al-im'sāk): Refraining from eating, drinking, and other nullifiers of fast for the rest of the day, even though the fast is not valid.
التشبه (al-tashabbuh): Imitating those who are fasting; the obligation to refrain is out of respect and resemblance, not as a valid fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, if a disbeliever accepts Islam during the day in Ramadan before noon and intends to fast, what is the ruling?
A) His fast is valid for Ramadan and he does not need to make it up.
B) He must refrain from eating but his fast is not valid and he must make up the day.
C) He does not need to refrain because he was not obligated.
D) His fast is valid as supererogatory but not for Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that when an excuse ends during the day, imsaak is wajib but qada is required, except for the madman whose fast is valid if he recovers before noon.

Ruling on a Traveler Who Intends to Break Fast and Then Returns Home
Explains validity of traveler's fast after intending to break and returning before noon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a traveler who intends to break his fast, returns before noon, and intends to fast, and explain the legal reasoning including the role of concession and analogy.

Core Idea
A traveler's fast becomes valid if he returns before noon and intends to fast, because the concession ceases.

In this passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, the author addresses a nuanced scenario: what happens when a traveler intends to break his fast but then returns home? The text clarifies that the fast is valid under specific conditions, using analogical reasoning to support the ruling. Understanding this helps distinguish between intention and actual nullification of the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling
Read the passage. The scenario: a traveler intends to break his fast, then returns home (before noon) and intends to fast. The ruling given: the fast is valid.
2
Understand the legal basis
The text explains that travel does not negate eligibility for obligation nor the validity of commencement. The concession to break the fast ceases when the travel ends before noon, making fasting obligatory.
Key concept: rukhsa ends when its cause ends.
3
Trace the analogical reasoning (dalil aqli)
The author compares with the case of a resident who travels during the day: such a person cannot break his fast because residence is stronger. Similarly, a traveler who returns and intends is even more obligated. Also, intention of breaking without action is ineffective, as shown by the analogy with prayer.
This is a qiyas type reasoning.
4
Note the exception: no kaffara
If the traveler actually breaks his fast (despite the valid intention), no kaffara is required because of shubhat al-mubih (doubt due to the concession).
Shubha removes the obligation of kaffara.
5
Apply to related scenarios
The text also covers: if the traveler intended to fast from the night, he cannot break. And if he never intended to break, the ruling is even more straightforward.
These are extensions of the same principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (ولو نوى المسافر الإفطار ثم قدم ونوى الصوم في وقته صح) إن نوى قبل انتصاف النهار لأن السفر لا ينافي أهلية الوجوب ولا صحة الشروع. أطلق الصوم فشمل الفرض الذي لا يشترط فيه التبييت والنفل وحيث أفاد صحة صوم الفرض لزم عليه صومه إن كان في رمضان لزوال المرخص في وقت النية، ألا ترى أنه لو كان مقيماً في أول اليوم ثم سافر لا يباح له الفطر ترجيحاً لجانب الإقامة فهذا أولى إلا أنه إذا أفطر في المسألتين لا كفارة عليه لقيام شبهة المبيح، وكذا لو نوى المسافر الصوم ليلاً وأصبح من غير أن ينقض عزيمته قبل الفجر ثم أصبح صائماً لا يحل فطره في ذلك اليوم ولو أفطر لا كفارة عليه . وأشار إلى أنه لو لم ينو الإفطار وإنما قدم قبل الزوال والأكل فالحكم كذلك بالأولى لأن الحكم إذا كان الصحة مع نية المنافي فمع عدمها أولى، ولأن نية الإفطار لا عبرة بها حتى لو نوى الصائم الفطر ولم يفطر لا يكون مفطراً، وكذا لو نوى التكلم في الصلاة ولم يتكلم لا تفسد صلاته كما في الظهيرية
His statement: 'And if a traveler intended to break his fast, then arrived and intended fasting at its time, it is valid' — if he intended before midday, because travel does not contradict eligibility for obligation nor the validity of commencement. He (the author) used the absolute term 'fast', so it includes the obligatory fast for which night intention is not required, and supererogatory. When it established the validity of the obligatory fast, he must fast it if it is Ramadan because the concession is removed at the time of intention. Do you not see that if he was a resident at the beginning of the day then traveled, it is not permissible for him to break his fast, giving preference to the aspect of residence; this is even more apt. But if he broke his fast in either case, there is no expiation (kaffara) upon him due to the doubt of the permissibility. Similarly, if a traveler intended to fast at night and woke up without having broken his intention before dawn and then woke up fasting, it is not permissible for him to break his fast that day, and if he breaks it there is no expiation. He indicated that if he did not intend to break his fast, but arrived before noon and had not eaten, the ruling is likewise by greater reason, because if the ruling is validity along with the intention of a contradictory act, then without it it is even more apt. And because the intention of breaking fast is not considered, such that if a fasting person intends to break his fast but does not do so, he is not a breaker; similarly, if he intends to speak in prayer but does not speak, his prayer is not invalidated, as in al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author addresses the scenario of a traveler who has the license to break his fast. If he intends to break his fast (nawa al-iftar) but then returns to his hometown (or becomes a resident) before noon and subsequently intends to fast, his fast is valid provided he makes the intention before midday. The reasoning is that travel does not negate the eligibility for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) nor the validity of commencing the fast. Since the obligation of fasting was present in principle, and the concession (rukhsa) of breaking the fast ceased when the travel ended before noon, the fast becomes incumbent. The author draws a comparison: if a resident travels during the day, he is not allowed to break his fast because residence is stronger; here, the traveler who returns is even more obligated. However, if he actually breaks his fast in either scenario, no kaffara is required because the doubt (shubha) of the concession remains. The author also notes that if a traveler intended to fast from the night and did not revoke his intention, he cannot break his fast. And if he never intended to break, the ruling is even clearer. Finally, he gives an analogy: intention of breaking fast without actually breaking does not nullify the fast, just as intending to speak in prayer without speaking does not invalidate prayer.

Key Terms
التأثير (al-ta’thīr): Effect or influence; here, the intention of breaking fast does not actually affect the fast unless action accompanies it.
شبهة المبيح (shubhat al-mubīḥ): Doubt regarding the permissibility of breaking the fast, due to the existence of a concession (rukhsa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on a traveler who intends to break his fast (nawa al-iftar) but then returns home before noon and intends to fast?
A) His fast is invalid and he must make up the day.
B) His fast is valid because the concession ceased.
C) He must continue breaking his fast for the rest of the day.
D) His fast is valid only if he had not eaten anything.

Key Takeaway
A traveler's fast is valid if he intends before noon after returning, and intention alone does not break the fast.

Ruling on Unconsciousness (Ighma') and Making Up Fasts
Explains the ruling on qada for unconsciousness and the presumption of intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making up fasts due to unconsciousness and apply the presumption of intention to determine which days require qada.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness excuses delay, not obligation: the first day is valid by presumed intention; subsequent days require qada.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario: a person unconscious for multiple days in Ramadan. The Hanafi school distinguishes the first day from later days based on the likelihood of intention. By exploring the author's reasoning and extensions, you will learn how the school uses analogy (qiyas) to sickness and the principle of 'apparent intention' to resolve such cases.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the ruling: the person must make up (qada) all missed fasts except for the first day if the unconsciousness began at night (before Fajr).
The exception applies only to the first day.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
Unconsciousness is compared to sickness: it is an excuse for delay but not for dropping the obligation entirely. The intellect (hija) remains intact, so the obligation is not removed.
This qiyas (analogy) is the basis.
3
Examine the presumption of intention
For the first day, it is presumed that the person had the intention to fast (niyyah) because it is the usual practice. For subsequent days, since the unconsciousness is continuous, no intention is presumed.
The text says 'al-zāhir wujūduhā minhu' (apparently it existed from him).
4
Apply the rule to other scenarios
The author extends the ruling: if unconsciousness occurred in Sha'ban (before Ramadan), all days must be made up because no intention existed. Similarly, a habitual sinner or a traveler who becomes unconscious must make up all days due to lack of evidence of intention.
These are derived applications (furu').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ويقضي باغماء سوى يوم حدث في ليلته) لأنه نوع مرض يضعف القوى ولا يزيل الحجى فيصير عذراً في التأخير لا في الإسقاط. يقضي اليوم الأول لوجود الصوم فيه وهو الإمساك المقرون بالنية إذ الظاهر وجودها منه، ويقضي ما بعده لانعدام النية ولا فرق بين أن يحدث الإغماء في الليل أو في النهار في أنه يقضي اليوم الأول، وإنما ذكر المصنف حدوثه في ليلته ليعلم حكم ما إذا حدث في اليوم بالأولى لوجود الإمساك وهو ليس بمغمى عليه. وأشار إلى أن الإغماء لو كان في شعبان قضاه كله لعدم النية، وإلى أنه لو كان متهتكاً يعتاد الأكل في رمضان أو مسافراً قضاه كله لعدم ما يدل على وجود النية
His statement: 'And he makes up for unconsciousness except for a day that occurred at its night' — because it is a type of sickness that weakens the powers and does not remove the intellect, so it becomes an excuse for delay, not for dropping (the obligation). He makes up the first day because fasting existed in it, i.e., refraining coupled with intention, for apparently it existed from him. He makes up what is after it due to absence of intention. There is no difference between whether the unconsciousness occurred at night or during the day in that he makes up the first day. The author only mentioned it occurring at night to let one know the ruling if it occurred during the day, by greater reason, because there was refraining and he was not unconscious. He indicated that if the unconsciousness was in Sha'ban, he makes up all of it due to absence of intention; and if he was a transgressor who habitually ate in Ramadan or a traveler, he makes up all of it due to lack of evidence indicating existence of intention.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the sharh. It deals with the case of a person who becomes unconscious for part of Ramadan. The author states that such a person must make up (qada) the missed fasts except for the first day if the unconsciousness began at night (i.e., before dawn). The reasoning: unconsciousness is like a sickness; it weakens the body but does not remove the intellect (hija), so it is an excuse for delaying the fast but not for dropping it entirely. For the first day, since the person was unconscious from before dawn, it is presumed that he had the intention (niyyah) to fast, so that day is considered valid and need not be made up. For subsequent days, since he was unconscious, there is no intention, so they must be made up. The author clarifies that this applies whether the unconsciousness started at night or during the day—the first day is still valid. The mention of 'night' is to highlight that if it started during the day, the ruling is even clearer because he already refrained from food and was not unconscious. The text also notes that if the unconsciousness occurred in Sha'ban (i.e., before Ramadan), all days must be made up because no intention existed. Similarly, if the person is a habitual sinner who eats in Ramadan or a traveler, all days must be made up because there is no evidence of intention.

Key Terms
الإغماء (al-ighmā’): Unconsciousness; a state where a person loses awareness but is still alive.
الحجى (al-ḥijā): Intellect or mental faculty; unconsciousness does not remove it permanently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, if a person becomes unconscious (ighma') just before dawn on the first day of Ramadan and remains unconscious for three days, how many days must he make up?
A) All three days.
B) Only the first day.
C) The second and third days only.
D) None, because unconsciousness drops the obligation.

Key Takeaway
Unconsciousness excuses delay, not obligation; the first day is valid by presumed intention, subsequent days require qada.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
12 min
Ruling on Insanity and Making Up Missed Fasts
Distinguishes non-extensive and extensive insanity regarding qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between extensive and non-extensive insanity regarding fasting obligations and state the Hanafi ruling on qada for each type, including the intra-madhhab dispute on original insanity.

Core Idea
Extensive insanity drops both fasting and qada; non-extensive requires qada because the obligation is not negated entirely.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a critical distinction in fasting obligations: insanity that engulfs the entire month of Ramadan removes the obligation altogether, while partial insanity requires makeup fasts. The author also navigates a subtle dispute among Hanafi imams about original insanity (reaching puberty already insane) versus accidental insanity. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the concepts of legal capacity (ahliyya) and hardship (haraj).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of insanity
Distinguish between extensive (mumtadd) insanity that covers the entire month and non-extensive (ghayr mumtadd) that does not. The basic ruling: non-extensive requires qada, extensive drops both fasting and qada.
The text defines 'non-extensive' as not encompassing the whole month; any recovery during the month makes it non-extensive.
2
Understand the rationale
Non-extensive still carries obligation because the cause (the month) and legal capacity (ahliyya bil-dhimma) exist, and there is a benefit in obligation without hardship. Extensive drops due to overwhelming hardship (haraj).
The author contrasts this with unconsciousness (ighma'), which never extends the whole month usually.
3
Recognize the sub-case of last-day recovery
If one recovers on the last day of Ramadan, it counts as non-extensive, whether recovery is before or after noon. This was contested by Hamid al-Din al-Darir, but the correct position is that qada is required.
The fast is valid at that time (like night), so the day is part of the month and obligation stands.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on original insanity
Imam Muhammad distinguishes original insanity (reaching puberty insane) from accidental: original is like boyhood, no address (khitab), so no qada. Abu Yusuf uses istihsan to oblige qada for both. The mufta bihi is no qada for original insanity, as supported by Shams al-A'imma.
The dispute hinges on whether the obligation was ever addressed to the person. The relied-upon position treats original insanity as never obligated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وبجنون غير ممتد أي يقضيه إذا فاته بجنون غير ممتد وهو أن لا يستوعب الشهر، والممتد هو أن يستوعب الشهر ، وهو مسقط للحرج بخلاف ما دونه لأن السبب قد وجد وهو الشهر والأهلية بالذمة. وفي الوجوب فائدة وهو صيرورته مطلوباً على وجه لا يحرج في أدائه بخلاف المستوعب فإنه يحرج في أدائه فلا فائدة فيه، والإغماء لا يستوعب الشهر عادة فلا حرج وإلا كان ربما يموت فإنه لا يأكل ولا يشرب، أطلقه فشمل الجنون الأصلي والعارض وهو ظاهر الرواية، وعن محمد أنه فرق بينهما لأنه إذا بلغ مجنوناً التحق بالصبي فانعدم الخطاب بخلاف ما إذا بلغ عاقلاً ثم جن وهذا مختار بعض المتأخرين . ودخل تحت غير الممتد ما إذا أفاق آخر يوم من رمضان، سواء كان قبل الزوال أو بعده، فإنه الشهر خلافاً لما في غاية البيان عن حميد الدين الضرير أنه قال : إذا أفاق بعد الزوال في آخر يوم من رمضان لا يلزمه شيء. وصححه في النهاية والظهيرية لأن الصوم يصح فيه كالليل. اعلم أن الجنون ينافي النية التي هي شرط العبادات فلا يجب مع مطلقاً للحرج وما لا يمتد جعل كالنوم لأن الجنون لا ينفي أصل الوجوب إذ هو ثابتة له باعتبار آدميته حتى ورث وملك وكان أهلاً للثواب كأن نوى صوم الغد بعد غروب الشمس فجن فيه ممسكاً كله فلا يقضي لو أفاق بعده وصح إسلامه تبعاً، وإذا كان صح المسقط الحرج لزم اختلاف الامتداد المسقط فقدر في الصلاة بالزيادة على يوم وليلة عندهما، يلزمه قضاء جميع الممتد منه بالذمة وهي وعند محمد بصيرورة الصلاة ستاً وهو أقيس لكنهما أقاما الوقت مقام الواجب كما في ينو فلا شك في عدمها قوله : ( وعن محمد أنه فرق بينهما أي قال إن بلغ مجنوناً ثم أفاق بعض الشهر ليس عليه قضاء ما مضى، وروى هشام عن أبي يوسف أنه قال : في القياس لا قضاء عليه ولكني أستحسن فأوجب عليه قضاء ما مضى من الشهر لأن الجنون الأصلي لا يفارق العارض في شيء من الأحكام وليس فيه رواية عن أبي حنيفة واختلف فيه المتأخرون على قياس مذهبه والأصح أنه ليس عليه قضاء ما مضى كذا في المبسوط كذا في العناية. وفي مواهب الرحمن: وألزمناء بالقضاء لو أفاق بعضه ولم نسقطه إلا في الأصلي على الأصح ا هـ. لكن في شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان وجواب الكتاب مطلقاً فيجري على إطلاقه وهو الصحيح. نص عليه في المنتقى قوله : ( وصححه في النهاية والظهيرية أي صححا ما في غاية البيان وكذا في العناية وفي المجتبى والمعراج وعليه الفتوى وهو مختار شمس الأئمة كما في الإمداد، ومشى عليه
His statement: 'And due to non-extensive insanity' means he makes it up if he missed it due to non-extensive insanity, which is that it does not encompass the month. Extensive insanity is that it encompasses the month, and it drops the hardship unlike what is less than it, because the cause existed, which is the month and eligibility in the dhimma. In obligation there is a benefit: his becoming obligated in a way that is not burdensome in performance, unlike the extensive which is burdensome, so there is no benefit. Unconsciousness does not encompass the month usually, so no hardship; otherwise he might die because he does not eat or drink. He (the author) used a general term, so it includes original and accidental insanity, which is the apparent narration. From Muhammad: he distinguished between them because if he reaches puberty insane, he is joined to the boy, so the address is non-existent, unlike if he reached puberty sane then became insane. This is the choice of some later scholars. Under non-extensive includes if he recovers on the last day of Ramadan, whether before noon or after, because it is the month, contrary to what is in Ghayat al-Bayan from Hamid al-Din al-Darir who said: if he recovers after noon on the last day of Ramadan, nothing is obligatory on him. It was corrected in al-Nihaya and al-Zahiriyya because the fast is valid in it as in the night. Know that insanity contradicts the intention which is a condition of worship, so it is not obligatory with it at all due to hardship, and what is not extensive is made like sleep because insanity does not negate the origin of obligation; it is established for him due to his humanity, so he inherits, owns, and is eligible for reward. For example, if he intended the next day's fast after sunset, then became insane, and remained fasting the whole day, then he does not make it up if he recovers after, and his Islam is valid subsequently. If the drop of hardship is valid, then the extent of the drop must vary. He estimated in prayer as exceeding a day and night according to them. He must make up all of the extensive from it in the dhimma. According to Muhammad, it becomes six prayers, which is more analogous, but they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) made the time stand in place of the obligation, as in 'yanu' there is no doubt of its absence. His statement: 'And from Muhammad he distinguished between them' meaning he said: if he reached puberty insane then recovered some of the month, he does not have to make up what passed. Hisham narrated from Abu Yusuf that he said: in qiyas there is no qada upon him, but I do istihsan and oblige him to make up what passed of the month because original insanity does not differ from accidental insanity in any ruling. There is no transmission from Abu Hanifa on this, and the later scholars differed based on qiyas of his madhhab. The most correct is that he does not have to make up what passed, as in al-Mabsut and al-‘Inaya. In Mawahib al-Rahman: we oblige him to make up if he recovers some of it, and we do not drop it except in original insanity according to the most correct. End. But in Sharh al-Jami‘ al-Saghir by Qadikhan, the answer of the book is absolute and it proceeds on its absoluteness, which is sahih. It is stated in al-Muntaqa. His statement: 'And it was corrected in al-Nihaya and al-Zahiriyya' meaning they corrected what is in Ghayat al-Bayan, and similarly in al-‘Inaya, al-Mujtaba, and al-Mi‘raj, and upon it is the fatwa, and it is the choice of Shams al-A’imma as in al-Imdad, and he proceeded upon it.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling and dispute map on insanity (junun) affecting fasting. The author first defines non-extensive insanity (ghayr mumtadd) as insanity that does not engulf the entire month, and extensive insanity (mumtadd) as that which does. The obligation to make up missed fasts (qada) applies only for non-extensive insanity because the obligation is still present (the month has passed and the person has eligibility/dhimma). The benefit of obligation exists: it becomes a requirement without undue hardship. Extensive insanity completely removes the hardship, so no qada is required. The author notes that originally the ruling covers both original and accidental insanity according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). Imam Muhammad distinguishes: if one reaches puberty while already insane, it is like a boy (no obligation before sanity), whereas if one was sane then became insane, qada is required. Some later scholars prefer this. A sub-case: if one recovers on the last day of Ramadan, it counts as non-extensive, whether recovery is before or after noon, contrary to Hamid al-Din al-Darir who said no qada if after noon. The correct position is that qada is required because the fast is valid at that time like night. The text then explains that insanity negates the intention (niyyah) which is a condition for worship, so it cannot be valid, yet the underlying obligation remains (due to humanity). For non-extensive, it is treated like sleep: the fast is valid if intention was present at night. For example, if one intended the next day's fast, then became insane but remained fasting, no qada is needed upon recovery. The author then discusses the point of estimating the extent of insanity that drops obligation: in prayer, according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?), it is set at more than a day and night; according to Muhammad, it becomes six prayers which is more analogical, but they consider the time as substitution. Then the intra-Hanafi dispute is detailed: Muhammad holds that if one reaches puberty insane then recovers, he does not make up past days; Hisham reports from Abu Yusuf that by qiyas there is no qada but he does istihsan to oblige it. There is no explicit narration from Abu Hanifa, and later scholars differed; the most correct is that no qada is required for the period of original insanity. However, some texts (Mawahib al-Rahman) oblige qada except for original insanity. But Qadikhan in his commentary says the absolute answer from the book is that it is not required, which is correct. The fatwa is according to al-Nihaya and al-Zahiriyya, and it is the choice of Shams al-A’imma al-Halwani.

Key Terms
الجنون الممتد (al-junūn al-mumtadd): Insanity that encompasses the entire month of Ramadan, dropping the obligation of fasting and qada.
الجنون غير الممتد (al-junūn ghayr al-mumtadd): Insanity that does not cover the whole month, requiring qada for missed fasts.
الأهلية بالذمة (al-ahliyya bil-dhimma): Legal capacity based on being a human; the person remains liable for obligations despite insanity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, if a person reaches puberty while already insane (original insanity) and remains insane for the entire month of Ramadan, what is the ruling on qada?
A) He must make up all missed days.
B) He does not have to make up any days.
C) He must make up half the days.
D) He must make up only if he recovers during the month.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation to make up missed fasts due to insanity depends on whether the insanity is extensive (whole month) or non-extensive, and on whether it is original or accidental.
